THE RED PAGE.
FOR AUSTRALIA.
THE object of life, for everything
living, is the satisfaction of natural
appetites. Even in the simplest
organisms there must exist some quality
of satisfaction ia the fulfilment of func-
tion to differentiate them (for example)
from the unorganised rock-crystal or the
insentient machine. The self-conscious-
ness of the ephemerae may be so vague
and slight as to be incognisable even by
themselves; yet their day's flight is the
satisfaction of an instinct, and in that
satisfaction they have their share, how-
ever minute, of the principle of Jife.
As organisation becomes more complex,
the share increases. To the bird
which trills to its mate in the sun-
shine must be conceded some con-
sciousness of an achieved object of exist-
ence—some sense of the nature and
value of the reaction of its organism to
the power of living which the universe
supplies. And the human animal differs
from others only in the greater com-
plexity of its organisation, which gives it
more desires to satisfy, and inore capaci-
ties of satisfaction. The crowds which will
witness the celebration of the establish-
ment of the Commonwealth in Australia
seek, in their way and degree, precisely
the same object as the bullocks which are
slaughtered for their sustenance or the
horses which are tamed to draw their
vehicles—they seek to live as fully and
continuously as they are able. And from
pageanjt or music or oration each man in
the,-crowd will receive precisely as much
stimulus as his physical constitution per-
mits—the word physical being used in its
wider sense to include the psychical which
flowers from it. He will get the satis-
faction of such appetites as he possesses,
in the measure that his vital energy
allows, to the extent which the celebra-
tion supplies.
* * * m
This is obvious enough; but the
is often forgotten. In the sway which
the habit of words, the habit of facts,
exercise over the mind, we are apt to lose
sight of the physical basis of life by
attending too exclusively to modes of
living. Yet the utter dependence of
nearly all human beings on the satisfac-
tion of sensual appetites for their realisa-
tion of life shows how closely still even the
best of us are allied with the brutaL So to
the crowds which will witness the celebra-
tion of the Commonwealth we supply, as
means of rejoicing, flags, and bands
of music, and triumphal arches, and the
spectacle of soldiers marching, and the
empty verbiage of temporary politicians.
Those are the methods by which the
appetites of crowds are satisfied—
barbarous enough, since usually neither
the blare nor the glare is harmonious,
and the speeches, in a country where
oratory is unknown, are usually inept
and futile ; —and not barbarous enough,
since, in many of the times we call bar-
barous, pageantry was a science and
speech-making an art. And the man in
the crowd will stare till his eyes are
itigued, and listen till his ears are
tigued, and run hither and thither till
s legs are- fatigued; and when his
bck of nervous energy is exhausted,
I will return to his crude home
Ih the knowledge that he has not lost
Jay, since his senses of seeing and
hearing and his capacity for locomotion
have been satiated to the .utmost. And,
with the best wit in the world, we can
devise no other method of public rejoic-
ing which will satisfy the crowd so well.
The crowd’s ideal of the pleasure of life
is still essentially the child’s idea, the
savage’s idea, 'of Blare and Glare—a
series of sensations as violent as possible,
culminating in nervous and
auto-intdxication.
* * * *
The celebration by Blare and Glare of
the establishment of the Commonwealth
is the process by which we ma'ke intelli-
gible to the crowd the supposed signifi-
cance of the Commonwealth : it is a kind
of picture-writing by which we give objec-
tive shape to the idea of the Nation. If
a sufficient number of violent sensations
are associated with that idea, the crowd
cannot but be impressed with the im-
portance of the Nation. For the rest, it
will translate the idea into its own men-
tal language by the aid of instinct. In
the process of evolution, the character or
the necessities of the human animal have
at different times made desirable or in-
evitable the acquisition of various in-
stincts secondary to the main instincts of
Self-Preservation and Self-Reproduction.
These instincts have been inherited and
transferred in greater or less intensity
from race to race, from generation to
generation. Chief among them are the
instinct of the Family, the instinct of
the Tribe or Community, the instinct of
Religion or Ghost-Worship, the instinct
of the Race or Country. And looming
beyond these we can see the instinct of
Humanity and the Universe arriving in
a natural progress—based on the idea
of expansion of the Ego in the
All. But neither the instinct of
Humanity nor any other secondary in-
stinct is intellectually defensible unless
conceived in terms of the individual’s
primary instinct of Self-Preservation,
unless conceived as Self-Preservation
raised to a higher plane. Self-Preserva-
tion is the only instinct which absolutely
justifies itself, since to preserve one’s-
self is to live, and to live is at all events
to fulfil a function, to receive sensations,
to satisfy the original appetite for living.
All the other instincts involve a gift of
life. The individual gives to his mate,
to his children, to his community, to his
country, to humanity. And the gift is
only intellectually defensible when it is a
valid exchange, when the individual
receives in the sensations which his
organism demands an equivalent of the
energy which he expends upon others. It
is not more’ blessed to give than to
receive. But it is blessed to give when
the gift brings an equivalent receipt of
the sensations which comprise the quality
of life of which the individual is capable
: —and if possible a little more than an
equivalent. And that is the sole reason-
able basis of ethics for any individual.
**, * •
We are no longer content with the
primary instinct of Self-Preservation.
Other instincts have developed so greatly
that many human beings are willing
and eager to sacrifice their lives to
the life of others ; —-but only be-
cause such dying is the best way of
living they can find at a given point of
life. The man who fights for a shibboleth
and is killed satisfies his appetite equally
with him who runs away for the satisfac-
tion of his appetite of mere living.. The
problem of life for any individual is, How
can I satisfy most appetites 1 how receive
most and best sensations ?—and he solves
it according to his nature and capacity.
The man in the crowd is a crea-
ture of habit: he accepts without con-
sideration the instincts which heredity
and environment have implanted *in him.
But it would be profitable to him and to
others to pass their instincts and habits
in array, and ask of each/ How does this
increase my life 1 What does it give me ?
How does it pay me?
* * * *
The instinct of Patriotism, of Love of
Race and Country, is that which the
establishment of the Commonwealth calls
to stand at the bar of judgment. How
will the Commonwealth benefit the man
in the crowd who welcomes and acclaims 1
What fuller life will it give him 1 What
appetites of living will it satisfy ? And,
on a reasonable survey, it may be found
that, except for a slightly increased
chance of self-preservation, a slightly in-
creased chance of profitable living, it
gives him nothing—except what he can
makfe out of it. He is still dependent for
his life on himself: the Common-
wealth is worth just so much as
he can make it worth, and no
more. There is no salvation in the
abstract idea of Australian union: its
chief value is as a stimulus to effort, and
the effort must always be the concrete
effort of the men who compose the union.
There is no profit in having a nation to
die for, unless the individual’s instinct to
die for a nation is so strong that dying is
his best living. There is no profit in
having a nation to live for, unless the
nation will give in satisfaction of the
individual’s appetites an equivalent of the
energy which he devotes to living for it.
The Commonwealth, as an abstract idea?
may give the satisfaction of the instinct
of devotion to country ; it can supply a
target for a hitherto unsatisfied instinct;
but beyond that, as an abstract idea,
it gives nothing. The practical value of
the Commonwealth to any individual, as
a means of better living, will depend up-
on himself and the other individuals who
compose the Commonwealth. And, after
the celebration and the shouting, the
worth of these constituents will be much
as before. There is no magical quality
in this union : no millennium is to be
reached by the declaration of a Federal
Act in so many chapters , and so many
clauses. We suppose that the Common-
wealth will give us greater scope for
effort, a wider field for energy; but it is
always our effort, our energy.
• • • *
The Commonwealth must justify itself.
For those who have a patriotic instinct,
whose profit to themselves comes merely
in satisfaction of the appetite for patriot-
ism, it may be justified already. But for
those whose patriotism does not go so
far as instinct, who ask that patriotism
stand and deliver its reasons why energy
should be expended in its service,
who ask that it supply an equiva-
lent in life for the life which it
exacts, the Comrdonwealth must supply
reasons. Australian union should be
made to pay every Australian individual
—not in money, but in fuller life. The
standard of living in Australia is so low
at present—not in comparison with the
standard of living in other countries, but
in comparison with an attainable ideal,
in comparison with the actual attainment
of some Australian classes—that it is
easily possible for good government to
elevate it for all. But good govern-
ment does not consist merely in
the passage of Parliamentary Acts,
devised mainly to meet a temporary
emergency, and needing continual amend-
ment. It does not consist in the adapta-
tion Of policies to current needs, or in the
adaptation of means of commerce or
finance to the temporary requirements of
the community. These things are neces-
sary and have their value; but they
are not the things which are most
necessary and of the greatest value.
Not to politics, but to statesmanship we
must look for the creation of a Common-
wealth which will fully justify itself to
the individuals who compose it; and
statesmanship in Australia is hardly to
be seen. Yet its main principles, as
applied to this country, are so simple and
familiar that they have been vulgarised
into almost-discredited catch-words.
» * * *
The first is the development of natural
resources, involving the settlement of the
community on the land as self-supporting
producers of the means of living. Cant
phrases these, in the mouth of every
hustings ranter ; yet so little valued in
the strife of petty factions and the
advancement of self - seeking poli-
ticians that after more than a hun-
dred years of government one-third,
of the whole Australian population
is concentrated in the capital cities.
Sydney, Melbourne, and Adelaide, in
particular, are like huge cancers whose
ramifications of disease spread far into
national life; and the non-productive inha-
bitants whom they nourish are' supported,
at the cost of the primary industries
of the community. In our overgrown
cities health and strength are sapped ;
the habit of productive labour is lost;
and the character of the people sensibly
deteriorates. For every growth of Sydney
or Melbourne, for every clerk added to
the immense civil service, the producing
industries of the country must sooner or
later pay unless the loan bill is met
by insolvency. The city tribe of clerks
and similar employees is the last class
which should be encouraged to increase.
Every one of them has to be carried on
the back of some struggling farmer or
miner or stock-raiser. And the cities
hitherto have been continually cockered
and pampered at the expense of the
country towns and the country settlers.
The first essential of national life, as of
individual life, is self-preservation; and
the way for a nation to preserve itself
is to engage as many as possible of
its people in the actual production of
food and natural wealth immediately
convertible into food. The first business
of the Commonwealth, for many years
to come, will be the transference of popu-
lation from city to country, where in-
dependent homesteads can be established;
and the deliberate starvation of endow-
ments to the cities that the country may
be fed. If our cancers are not extir-
pated, they may yet destroy the nation.
* * * »
Next in importance to the preservation
of national life is the preservation of the
race, the purification of the national
blood. This also is a vital necessity, to
which schemes of commerce and industry
must always rank second. The mixed
British breed on which we build our race
is as good a breed, on the whole, as
any to be found ; and for all men who
are fit to be merged in it the Common-
wealth should have a welcome. But,
living,as we do on the border of Asia, we
are always exposed to the danger of
Asiatic incursions, and it is certain that
the establishment of Asiatic settlements,
or of a Eurasian breed, will tend to de-
grade and destroy the white breed and
the white Commonwealth. The South
Sea Islander is similarly barred from
Australian citizenship. To good Asiatics
and South Sea Islanders bad whites
may be inferior; but the white
races have won from the centuries
a possibility of development along
their own lines which is not shared
by the Asiatic or the Islander. Nothing
at all—no question of local profit or tem-
porary advantage—should be permitted
to interfere with the vital and permanent
necessity of preserving Australia for
white Australians.
* * • *
The third abiding principle of states-
manship for the Commonwealth is the
principle of the equalisation of fortunes.
A proletariat has no reason to be patriotic.
In Australia we have not yet reached the
extremes of wealth and poverty to be
seen in elder nations; but the division
between rich and poor tends to increase,
and already we have a numerous class of
paupers living from hand to mouth, and
dependent on chance employment for
subsistence. Good government can do
much to level the disparity of classes. It
cannot make all men equal, but it can
give to all equal opportunities as far as
the sphere of government extends. The
Commonwealth can tax the rich for the
benefit of the poor, and ensure to some
extent a re-distribution of wealth at each
generation. It can place education within
the reach of all, capital within the reach
of the industrious—always with the aim
of making as many of its citizens as pos-
sible their own employers, independent of
wages. And this without thwarting en-
terprise or hampering real progress.
* * * *
These three principles are principles of
statesmanship, which should • always be
borne in mind, however the ship of state
may tack to catch a prevalent wind of
trade or politics. Everything else is sub-
ordinate. The mechanism of government
may alter from time to time ; but the
objects of government must always be
national existence, racial preservation,
and a national prosperity in which every
citizen may share in proportion to his
desert. And if these objects be reason-
ably well attained, it will be possible to
justify all the enthusiasm to which the
establishment of the Commonwealth has
given birth by proving to nearly every
individual in the community that Pat-
riotism. Pays.

As the years roll on the old-established
business of W. H. Paling & Co. continues to in-
crease, and the firm now occupies 48,000 square
feet of floor space. The firm are the largest
importers of pianos in Australasia ; sometimes
three ships, following each other at week or two
intervals, will bring 400 pianos to Paling & Co.
Makers include such well-known names as Stein-
way, Brinsmead, Lipp and Son, Feurich, Ecke,
Victor, and Belling. The firm’s third floor is
entirely taken up with the smaller class of musical
instruments—violins, guitars, banjos, polyphons,
musical boxes and band instruments. Paling’s
are agents for organs by Estey and Co. and Story
and Clark, and can supply elegant models at low
prices, suitable for churches, drawing-room, school,
or open-air services. Paling’s carry everything in
the musical line, and country visitors will find it
very enjoyable to pay Paling’s a visit.*

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Babies in the Bush.
They trotted away from the homestead fair,
Ere the leaves in the noon-tide drooped •
They trotted along by the stockyard bare,
And under the rails they stooped ;
Each chubby brown hand “lest a bad wind
bio wed ”
Held fast to a sailor hat —
They had begged to go “where the daisies
growed ”
And the buttercups out on the flat.
Oh, sing me a song of a fairy bright,
Of a spirit the bell-birds know,
That guides the feet of the lost aright,
Or carries them up thro’ the starry night
Where the bush-lost babie3 go.
We wander away as our fortune needs,
When our feet are strong to roam ;
But what is the spirit that always leads
The toddler’s feet from home ?
A belt of timber, and, miles beyond.
The awful scrubs aglow—
Oh, plead in prayer that it is not there
. That the bush-lost babies go !
We thought of the tales that the bush can tell,
As the awful suns went round —
“And we’ll find those babies asleep and well
As bush-lost babes are found.”
We searched all night and we searched all day,
We searched thro’ the broiling week,
But never a sign of a baby’s foot
Was found by that lonely creek.
The mother waits ’neath the noon-tide glare,
And under the midnight skies,
Till the wild fixed look of a life’s despair
Comes into her hopeless eyes.
But the strong man kneels by her side and turns
Her face from the clearing bare,
To the stars above, with a husband’s love—
And “ Our bush-lost babes are there ! ”
And she sings in her heart of a fairy bright,
Of a spirit the bell-birds know,
To guide the feet of the lost aright
And lead them on to a land of light
Where the bush-lost babies go.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
In Thule.
“ There was a king in Thule,”
And so the legends say,—
Who loved his mistress truly,
For ever and for aye.
There was a king in Thule,
And he would constant be;
There is a king in Thule,
In Thule, ’cross the sea.
And when the red sun, splendid,
The gate of dawn unbars,
And when the scroll unended
Is opened of the stars,
He sits still drinking, lonely,
And in his fancy he
His true-beloved only
For evermore may see.
Before, and aye behind him,
The tide of life may pass,
If but his vision find him
Her dead face in the glass !
N.s.w.
E. J. BRADY.
To spend the afternoon of Xmas day
pleasantly, get all your friends together in a
pretty drawing-room. When four o’clock comes
round the only thing you will need is a cup of
Atcherley and Dawson’s Globe Tea. After
that matters will go very cheerily till the bell
rings.
" Edson ” : Flit Kin had a wife. Perhaps
it would be more correct to say that Flit Kin’s
wife had him. Flit Kin was a small, worried
Chow, and his frau was a tough lump of an Irish-
woman, who ran him unmercifully and had
merely married him in order to have a good mate
to assist her in her mining operations. Mary was
a great alluvial digger, and knew the business as
well as any man on the Piper. She worked hard
herself, and made Kin work harder, and Kin sub-
mitted meekly, like a man without a soul, even
when the wife of his bosom landed him in the ribs
with a pick-handle, or prodded him with the
sluicing-fprk. One day I stood by the windlass
while Flit Kin was hauling dirt, waiting for Mrs.
Flit Kin to come to the surface. Presently the
missus gave a signal, and Flit started to haul. The
hole was a shallow one, and looking down I saw
Mrs. Kin’s face coming up out of the shadow
with wide eyes and protruding tongue. “ Lower
away ! Lower away !” I yelled. “ You’ve got
her round the neck ! ” Flit Kin looked at me
dully, and lowered again without any particular
haste. Then, when it was slack rope and I was
starting down, he said, with effective pantomime,
“ Lope loun’ my missy neck, all a-same hang ? ”
“Yes,” I answered. He looked at me with pro-
found reproach —“ Whaffoh you no’ min’ you own
dam business ? ” he said.


VICTORIAN JN& RAILWAYS.
CHRISTMAS & NEW YEAR EXCURSIONS
(1900 1901 )
Summer Excursions.
Holiday Excursion Tickets will be issued TO and
FROM ALL STATIONS (suburban excepted), and at the Central
Booking Office, Tlinders-street, Melbourne, from 15tli December
till 3rd January, both days inclusive (Sundays excepted), avail-
able for return for one calendar month from date of issue. The
journey must be commenced on the date the ticket is issued,
but, after a distance of 15 miles has been travelled, the journey
may be broken at the discretion of the passenger. On tickets
issued at Spencer-street and Prince’s Bridge stations, and at the
Central Booking Office, Plinders-street, bearing dates from 15th
till 27th December inclusive, and from 28tli December till 4tli
January inclusive, the journey may be commenced on any day
during the respective period, and the tickets will be available
for return till 28th January or 3rd Pebruary inclusive, as the
case may be.
SEASIDE AND GIPPSLAND LAKES EXCURSIONS.
p'ROM 15th November, 1900, till 30th April, 1901, Seaside Excursion Tickets at Holiday Excursion Fares will be
1 issued at the principal stations to Geelong', Queenscliff, Dean’s Marsh, Forrest, Timboon, Portland, Warrnam-
bool, Port Fairy, Franston, Hastings, Bittern, Morning-ton, Stony Point, Sale, Bairnsdale, Foster, Toora, Welshpool,
Alberton, or Port Albert, and combined railway and steamboat tickets tor the Gippsland Lakes. The tickets will be
available for return for three months, and the journey may be broken at Melbourne for three days going and
returning. Purchasers of seaside tickets to Queenscliff or to Warrnambool and Port Fairy (via Penshurst), and Port
Fairy (via Terang), may make Drysdale, or Marcus Hill, or Koroit respectively their destination instead. For full
particulars see posters at all stations.

Tickets between Melbourne and Queenscliff are issued in connection with P.S.S. Hvgeia and Ozone at 7s. 6d each
Melbourne and Queenscliff issue these tickets on Fridays and Saturdays ; and Messrs. Huddart Parker and Co. and
the Bay Excursion Co. on Saturdays and Sundays.
Tickets are available for return till the following Monday. Passengers going by rail return bv steamer and vice
versa. The journey cannot be broken.
Melbourne and Mornington. —During the summer months oombined Rail and Boat First-Class Return
Tickets are issued in connection with the pleasure steamers, Hygeia and Ozone, at 6s. 6d. each. The tickets are
issued at Prince’s Bridge and Mornington on Fridays and Saturdays, and by Messrs. Huddart Parker and Co. and the
Bay Excursion Company on Saturdays and Sundays. They are available for return till the following Monday Pas-
sengers going by rail return by steamer, and vice versa ; or if a passenger travel by rail to Mornington or Prince’s
Bridge on the combined ticket, and desire to return by rail, he may do so without extra payment. The journey
cannot be broken. ' J J

Yankee Doodle at the Front!
An incident in the Boer war as related by a returned Officer.
'Kk regiment of our Australian infantry was travelling (as depicted in
the smaller photograph) to Belmont. At Graspan we were
xzc
entertained by the
garrison of Gordon
Highlanders sta-
tioned there, and
when taking our
departure by rail
were accorded the
customary musical
send - off by the
garrison band,
which, on this occa-
sion, consisted of
two drummers and
a piper. As our
train moved off I
heard the cry on
all sides from our
boys —‘ Yankee Doodle !! ’ ‘ Yankee Doodle !! ’ and there in the distance
was an almost exact representation of the Yankee Doodle tobacco brand
so familiar to all Australians. The band after seeing us aboard were
returning back along the road to camp, and as we passed they sturdily
marched and played, whilst we, with the familiar tableau before us
reminding us so of home, cheered wildly. I instantly took out my kodak,
and, although the train was moving fast, secured a fairly good picture,
with the idea that perhaps my friends (the Dixsons) in Australia would like
to have it. On many a weary march afterwards, when smoking the army
regulation tobacco, did we long for just one pipe of the old far-off favorite.”

Time.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" Who painted Time with beard of white
And snows upon his head ?
He hath, in sooth, not painted right,”
The mourning Poet said.
Time is a Housemaid, young and fair,
Clear-eyed, with cheeks abloom,
Who, in the House of Joy-and-Care,
Toils ever with her broom.
Without the House of Joy-and-Care,
Without the open door,
A Narrow Box it standeth there
For aye and evermore.
And Virgin Time, along the Hall
Of this dull House of Life,
With ready broom she sweepeth all
Our sorrows and our strife.
The Narrow Box it holdeth well
The laughter and the tears ;
The Narrow Box it holdeth well
The Dust of all the years.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
McBride’s Bay Mare.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I once ’ad a ’oss,” said Mcßride, “ an’ she was
a bay mare an’ a fair cow, an’ no mistake.
“ She belonged to Jimmy Dow afore I ’ad ’er,
an’ he gave five-ten an’ an old plough for ’er, un-
broken. .
“Jimmy warn’t a bad sort, considering but he
jseter cuss something terrible, an’ when he wanted
that old ’oss ter go he useter cuss like mad.
“By-an’-by the old ’oss kinder got useter
this, an’ she wouldn’t go till yer cussed like mad.
Then Jimmy got in ter the habit of cussing all
along —just like other coves ’ud whistle an’ sing ;
so the old ’oss got kinder useter this too, an’ no
matter how ’ard ye was going, she’d stop as dead
as a door-nail the minute yer stopped cussing !
“Well, one wet season, when Jimmy an’two
other coves was bringin’ a mob over the border,
Jimmy got an abscess under ’is tooth, an’ every
time he’d open his mouth ter cuss, it useter give
him reg’lar jip, an’ as soon as he’d shut it an’ start
moanin’ the ole ’oss ’ud stop dead.
“ ’E cussed his head the size of a pumpkin fust
lay, an’ couldn’t open it the next, so them two
loves takes it in turns ter cuss the ole ’oss along.
“Fust Ned ’ud cuss a mile, an’ then Ted’ud
cuss a mile, an’ Jimmy ’ud be groanin’ and cussin’
till they gets ter Bourke.
“When they’d got them cattle yarded them
coves cleared, an’ Jimmy, knowin’ ’e ain’t fit ter
cuss ’ome ag’in, comes ter me.
“ I didn’t know nothin’ about that cuss dodge
then ; so when ’e arsts eight quid fer her, I beats
im down ter seven-ten an’ a bottle of whisky fer
’e’s tooth.
“ Well, I didn’t use ’er for a few days, ’n then
when I gets on she wouldn’t budge an inch. So I
starts cussin’ like mad, an’ as soon as I rips out
the first Word, orff she goes like a good ’un ; so,
seein’ ’ow well she goes, I stops, ’n’ she stops, an’ I
, falls right over her ears.
“ When I gits on ag’in I cusses pretty fair, an’
the more I cusses the more she goes, an’ we did
the five mile ter Boulton’s in no time.
“ ‘ Noo ’oss ? ’ sez Boulton ter me.
“ ‘ Yaas ! ’ sez I.
“ ‘ I think I know that ’oss,’ sez ’e, and ’e starts
cussin’ like mad an’ me an’ the ole ’oss goes like
, ’ell.
“When I gits back ’e tells me all about her ’an
when I knowed, I could er cussed ’er ten mile as
easy as drinkin’ beer.
“ When I starts ’ome ag’in I ’ad a fair cow of a
time. I was tired an’ couldn’t cuss more’n a
hundred yards, ’n’ then I’d stop, an’ she’d stop, an’
we’d take a rest, an’ then I’d cuss another
hundred, an’ then she’d stop agen, an’ we’d take
another spell, an’ if yer counts how many hundred
yards it’s from ’ere to Boulton’s yer’ll find ’ow
many spells we had afore we got ’ome.
“After that, when the kids ’ud be in bed and
I uhe missus ’ud be out yarnin’ at Green’s, I useter
go up into the loft and start practisin’ a five-mile
cuss. It was terrible ’ard work that, an’ it took
me three hours four nights a week afore I could
cuss seven-thirty-five, which was her time for the
five mile, if yer cussed middlin, without stoppin’.
“D’yer know that parson—the Baptist cove?
Well, ’e came over ter my place one day, an’ I
seen ’e’s gone on the ole ’oss as soon ’e spots ’er.
“ ‘ That’s a fine ’oss, Mr. Mac.,’ sez ’e.
“ ‘ Yes,’ sez I, ‘an’ she’s as good as she looks ! ’
“ ‘ I wanter buy a good ’oss, Mr. Mac.,’ sez ’e.
“ ‘ Ten quid on the table an’ she’s yourn,’ sez I.
“Well, ’e wanted ter see ’er paces, an’ so I
sticks ’im on the verandah an’ starts the ole ’oss
about a ’undred yards away, so’s ’e couldn’t ’ear,
an’ then starts cussin’, an’ the ole ’oss starts goin’
like blue-murder.
“‘Fine, very fine indeed,’ sez ’e, after I’d
finished. ‘l’ll take her at yer own figger,’ sez
’e, an’ ’e sticks the money on the table.
“ Well, when ’e changes saddles with ’is other
’oss, ’e gets on. ‘Now, me beauty ! ’ sez ’e ; ‘be
orff,’ sez ’e, an’ shakes the reins like this ; but she
wouldn’t budge.
“ ‘ Oh ! but you must, now,’ sez ’e ; ‘ you really
nust,’ sez ’e, an’ gives ’er a dig ; but she didn’t
take no notice, but jist ’angs ’er ’ead like a weepin’
willow what’s tired.
“ ‘ ’Ow’s this ? ’ sez ’e. ‘ She’s a jibber,’ sez ’e.
“ ‘No,’ sez I; ‘she ain’t,’ sez I.
“ ‘ Why don’t she go then ? ’ sez ’e.
“ ‘ That’s a pe-cule-er-arity ov ’ers,’ sez I.
“ "Ow?’ sez ’e.
“ ‘Yer ’ave ter cuss,’ sez I.
“ ‘ Cuss ?’sez’e.
“ ‘Yes,’ sez I, an’ I showed ’im ’ow, an’ him an’
the ole ’oss goes down the road like a ’ot wind.
“You oughter seen his face when ’e comes back
; leadin’ the ole ’oss an’ lookin’ blacker nor a
nigger.
I “‘D’yer mean ter inform me,’ sez’e, ‘as this
yer ’oss won’t go till yer swear at ’er V sez ’e.
“ ‘Fact,’ sez I.
“ ‘ Ver-il-y,’ sez ’e, ‘ this ’ere ’oss is a himp of
Satan ! ’
“ ‘ You’re wrong,’ sez I (fer I ’ad ’er papers in-
side) ; ‘ she’s outer Polly Brooks by Randulf ; ’
but ’e didn’t seem ter know that breed —leastways
he didn’t take no notice.
“ ‘ You must take ’er back,’ sez ’e.
“ ‘ Why ? ’ sez I.
| “ ‘ I’ll prossy-cute yer with false pretences ! ’
“ ‘ Well,’sez I, considerin’, ‘l’ll trive yer eight
quid fer ’er an’ say no more about it,’ sez I.
“ ’E argyfied a bit, but I got round ’im all right;
but when ’e went ’e got regular mad, an’ sez ’e :
‘ Yer a fittin’ pair,’ sez he, ‘ an’ it’s well fer ’osses
iher ain’t no after-life fer them ! ’ sez ’e.
“I think he was a bit ratty ! ” said Mcßride.

The Westralian Forestry Department,
learning one of the best lessons its neighbor,
South Australia, has to teach, is distributing
parcels of seed of the date-palm for experimental
sowing north of the Murchison. The idea is to
hand out packets to prospectors and others going
far afield, with the request to drop a seed or two
at every likely-looking spring or pool or “ soak.”
In eight or ten years plants should be in bearing.
A very good plan adopted by the explorers and
early surveyors of far-west N.S.W. and South
Queensland was to similarly distribute melon and
pumpkin seeds. The melons, even down to late
times, have kept many a droughty traveller on his
legs, while the pumpkins have been the mainstay
of several shearers’ strike-camps.
“ Living water” is the most appropriate descrip-
tion that can be given of the wonderful diamonds on dis-
play at the Goldsmiths and Silversmiths Alliance, 90

attention to their Souvenirs in commemoration of the
Australian Commonwealth, which comprise an immense
variety of miniature photographic medallions, set in solid
gold and crystal glass, containing life-like photos, of their
Royal Highnesses the Duke and Duchess of York, Lord
and Lady Hopetoun, and the leading celebrities of the
Commonwealth. They are also exhibiting their most
novel Registered Commonwealth Souvenirs, which have
been manufactured in the form of silver and gold
Brooches, Pendants, Hat Pins, Cuff Links, Shoe Lifts,
Seals, Match Boxes, Belt Clasps, Sugar Tongs, Berry
Forks, and Souvenir Spoons. The Goldsmiths and Silver-
smiths Alliance cordially invite visitors to Sydney during

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 8, 1900.
Nothing Done.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The whare looked very dirty. Its filth was un-
romantic, and the black pig which sunned its
belly at the doorway might have been taken for
the figurative Beast.
There was the usual porch, painted originally a
sickly yellow, but stained by wind and smoke to
a color that in human complexion we call “bad
liver.”
Buxton, an exceedingly cleanly young man,
plainly showed his displeasure and stepped
forward with Agag-like delicacy. The occupants
of the porch favored him with an apathetic stare.
“Tenakoe, pakeha !” whined the woman.
“A d— Englishman!” growled the man.
“ Hullo, you there ! Don’t put your confounded
feet on Pompey.”
Buxton apologised for disturbing the pig, and
nervously pulled down his shirt-cuffs. The back-
blocks were new to him, and the greeting of the
Pakeha-Maori was strange to the usages of Pall
Mall.
Meanwhile, the man and woman continued
smoking impassively, and the pig luxuriously
turned over in the mud.
“I —er—in fact,” began Buxton.
“Am a trespasser,” continued the man, rudely.
‘ Perhaps, but only by accident. I lost my
way—got into a road that led to nowhere.”
“I have heard my estate”—the man waved his
hand with a sarcastic gesture, indicating the
quarter-acre clearing—“ called many names, but
your title is certainly the most original ? Martha ”
—he turned to the woman —“Martha, you dirty
daughter of a dago dog, your respected husband’s
estate is termed ‘ Nowhere !’ ” He abused the
woman without raising his voice, in a calm, mono-
tonous way, as if to curse her was a weary duty,
and, the task finished, he questioned Buxton.
“ Who sent you here ? The loafers at the pub. ?
Or was it that white-livered skunk of a parson,
who’s afraid to face even my poor little Martha ? ”
The woman grinned, and showed a fine set of
teeth. Buxton shook his head, wonderingly.
“No. I don’t quite understand you. I lost my
way, that is all.”
“So, you’re not a Joshua, come to spy
out a blot on civilisation ? And, by the way, I
suppose you don’t carry any cheap jewellery or
beads on your person, because, if you do .
That’s only chaff. I know you don’t. Here,
Martha, bring the chair for our distinguished
European visitor.”
The woman rose to her feet, displaying a big,
heavy-boned figure. The outlines of her fleshy bust
and limbs clearly showed beneath the thin cotton
blouse and skirt which draped them, and her feet
were bare, as were the man’s. She plunged into
the darkness of the whare, presently returning
with a wooden kitchen chair.
“You hardly appreciate the honor,” remarked
the man. “That is my chair of state. When I
am quite sober—which happens possibly twice a
year—l sit in my chair and try to imagine that I
am a white man again. Occasionally I use it to
knock Martha down with, for which reason I
think you will find one of the legs somewhat
groggy- I notice,” he continued, “ that you have
let your pipe go out. I would advise you to
smoke ; strangers sometimes regard the odor of
rotten shark as rather overpowering. It’s a
matter of habit. I expect I should equally dis-
like caviare—now.”
Buxton lit his pipe gratefully. “Look here,”
he said, hesitatingly, “I don’t know your name,
and I’m not enquiring into your business—but,
damn it all, man, you needn’t make things worse
than they are. You know what I mean. You’re
—you’re just trying to exaggerate the concern.”
“ Thanks,” said the man slowly ; “you’re white,
and we’ll drop the play-acting biz altogether.
Martha, you slut, clear. This talk is pakeha.”
When the woman had gone the men smoked a
while in silence.
Then the Pakeha-Maori: “ Varsity man ? ”
“Yes. John’s, Oxford.”
“Ah ! I’m a Balliol.”
After which another relapse of energetic
smoking.
“A tourist?”
“No. Dabble in black-and-white line. Just
now I’m working up illustrations for a globe-
trotter who’s caught the pen-fever.”
The man blew clouds of smoke more energeti-
cally.
“ Funny thing, that. Some time or other I was
in the same line myself. As far as I can make
out it must be twenty years ago.”
“Ever do anything now?” queried Buxton
with interest.
“ Yes, when I’m sober. That’s perhaps twice a
year, as I told you before, and, strange to say, the
fit’s on now.” From inside his shirt he pulled out
a small drawing-book and handed it to Buxton.
“Just filled it, and to-morrow I shall burn it.
Funny, isn’t it?”
Buxton opened the book, and, with close atten-
tion, turned the leaves. It was five minutes
before he had finished, and he still held the book
in his hands, almost reverently.
“Good God ! man,” he said, excitedly, “d’you
know what this is? It’s genius—downright
genius! They’re rough, some of them—but,
Caesar, they’re great! If I could do that work I’d
have my country-house and a yacht in a few years’
time. Why, you’ll be the rage in no time. Come
away from this place, and you’ll ”
“ Never do anything! Never did, never shall,
never can ! ”
“But you have ! ” cried Buxton, holding up the
book.
“ That will be gone to-morrow,” said the man,
quietly. “It will go with my soul. My soul
that sits up in the trees somewhere, and mocks
and jeers and laughs at me. It says * You
beast, you shameless low animal, would you claim
what is mine. Burn it, tear it, destroy it. You
never did it. You never could. You sold
your birthright years ago drunkard, sot
that you are; and now you would steal mi/
property. So, you see, I burn the book, and
my soul is satisfied and says ‘ now you may drink
and lie in the dirt, and accept Martha’s
and—be happy ! ’ Of course, this is all rot and
rubbish, but it seems real to me—nerves bad, I
suppose—and—and I can’t get away from it.”
Buxton looked at him hopelessly.
“Consider,” he said, falteringly, “what the
world loses through your scruples.”
“Loses!” answered the man. “Loses the
sight of genius degraded. I think it may recover
the loss.”
Buxton looked at the book in his hands with an
expression on his face easy to read.
“You would like it ? ” said the man. “ I will
make a deal with you. I’ll sell one page at a
price.”
“ How much ? ” queried Buxton.
“ I’ll take payment in kind—one page—any one
page—for a case of fizz—the genuine article—and
three bottles of three-star. The other fit’s coming
on, and I’d like to taste King’s Peg again.”
Buxton carefully selected a page and tore it out
of the book. “It’s a bargain,” he said; “I’ll
take this one, and send you the stuff down from
Auckland.”
“ A coincidence,” murmured the man, looking
at the torn-out page. “That’s Martha’s father
on the spree.”
* * • •
Six months later Buxton revisited the whare.
It was empty, and in the corner of the clearing
some neatly-cut characters on a small board
announced the reason :
H. G. H.
Died of King’s Peg.
25th March, 18 —
Nothing done.
RDOAR M. DELL.
My First and Last Tenant.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The agent explained to me that the occupant of
the house he was about to let—gazing at me with
ophidian eyes—would be suzerain of a house in the
next allotment. A fence ran between the two
residences. The house I was looking at was a
small, squat, sturdy cottage, with chimneys like
corkscrews, and, though up to its knees in accumu-
lated dirt and dust and general detritus, carried
an expression of dogged insobriety which pleased
me greatly. There are also in its construction
the evident signs of sudden happy thoughts—all
different and all contradictory—on the part of the
builder. Steps and passages led into rooms which
seemed to have no purpose. One room I remember
more than the others. It had no window ; it had
no fireplace ; it had no apparent reason for being
at all, and yet it had a distinct aliveness —the dull,
helpless, but malignant look which appears in all
things that man has deserted. The verandah had
a trellis, and over the trellis trailed an aged vine
that held out three—l counted them —bunches of
white grapes. Banksia roses ran over the trellis
and curved their slender arms around the dead-
brown branches of the vine. The impression was
of Anacreon very old and poor, but still proudly
and gravely drunk, rising suddenly out of his
quiet Grecian grave into a new plug-hatted world.
I saw all this, and, of course, the agent had me
in his hands. “That tree in the backyard,” I
observed, “is a fine tree.” It was a tall, mourn-
ful willow tree.
“Yes,” said the agent, “itis a fine tree —a very
fine tree.”
“ That branch, though—the one that hangs over
the little pool—is picturesque enough, but rather
cumbrous, don’t you think ? ”
The agent smiled pensively.
“ That branch has a history,” he said. “The
last tenant of this cottage—he was a jeweller by
trade, name of Lyons—tried to hang himself from
that branch. People next door cut him down
just in time.”
I took the house at once.
* * * «
The little cottage next-door was one of those
dwellings which are rarely seen off the operatic
stage and outside the drawing-books for beginners.
It was simply smothered in white roses, and even
from the chimney a daring rose shook a laughing
head in the air.
“You will, of course, collect the rent for this
place,” said the agent.
“ Of course,” I remarked.
I began to understand the feeling of Property.
I belonged to the Landlord Class.
* * * *
A week after I had taken the house my wife
was fortunate enough to find a tenant for the
cottage.
“ Artist! ” I said, thinking of the white roses.
No j rabbit-seller,” she replied. “His horse
is in the front yard.”
So it was—eating the roses.
Presently the Owner of the Horse appeared. A
weasel-faced man with shifty eyes.
“ Discarded jockey, or groom,” I said to myself.
I was wrong about the jockey—right about the
groom. He curried and caressed that old sphinx-
headed horse, and cooed to it, and made a whist-
ling sound—peculiar to grooms but apparently
much appreciated by horses—through his teeth.
“ Good fellow,” I said to myself again. “Ani-
mals know.” I thought so then. I now think
that they know a great deal more than is good for
them.
I opened a bottle of claret—9s. 6d. per dozen—
to celebrate the occasion.
My new tenant and I drank success to each
other. He did not presume, and I kept my pride
in hand. I felt as though I were a Squire talking
affably to one of his tenant-farmers.
I may say that I had paid a woman to scrub the
place all over. A previous occupant—one who is
now moving in larger circles—had painted his coat-
of-arms and written the motto of his family over
the kitchen fire-place. The good, honest hired
woman assiduously scrubbed coat-of-arms and
motto away. But the cottage was indubitably
clean.
I was in town that day and was talking to some
Bohemians—men whom I used to like as boon
companions and I told them that I was no longer
a Revolutionist or a hater of the Patman. Even
the Patman had responsibilities and excuses for
living.
Nevertheless, I was quite serious, and looked at
those good-hearted fine fellows—clever and bright
in their own way, but knowing nothing of busi-
ness or the Responsibilities of Capital—with a com-
passionate eye.
“ The question of landlord and tenant is as old
as the legend of the Garden of Eden,” said the
Cynic, stroking his grizzled beard. “Ifl am not
mistaken, you said last Saturday that landlords
were the scum of the earth.”
Well, Iliad said something of the kind, but he
is a poor sort of man who cannot occasionally
alter his mental point of view, and reconstruct his
horizon.
It might be good enough for them to sit guzzling
beer in a hotel, but a landlord has better business
in hand. I left them, and went home to look
after my property. Six shillings a week was the
rent of the cottage covered with roses.
The first week’s rent was paid in rabbits.
That was right enough. A man with a family
can always use rabbits, in one fashion or another.
The second week’s rent was paid in rabbits.
Toujours perdrix !
Then there was no more rent.
I was walking around my property one morning,
smoking and thinking that, after all, it was a
devil of a business to be a landlord. And then,
looking out from the window of my cottage, I saw
a face with curled chestnut hair and wonderful
laughing hazel eyes.
I went into my cottage and asked if any repairs
were necessary. Could Ido anything at all ?
The lady was the wife of the rabbit-seller, and
she was one of the prettiest women I had ever
seen. I considered that she was quite thrown
away on the weasel-faced man.
Six weeks more and no rent. I was in debt
myself, Ihe lady and her weasel-faced husband
were at my back-door explaining why they could
not pay me my rent regularly. The husband
offered me an ancient barraeouta as something off
the account.
“ I know these excuses,” I said. “ I have no
doubt that you mean well—but what am Ito do
in the meantime ? The barraeouta you offer me
is, no doubt, a good, respectable fish, but I cannot
use it as an asset. Kish is not currency with the
people I deal with ? ”
The couple seemed distressed, but I set my
mouth and made myself stern. “Itresolves itself
into a question of paying—or going,” I said,
stiffening my shoulders against the back-door.
And the woman with the pretty hazel eyes, and
the weasel-faced man implored me to give them a
little more time and not carry tilings to extremes.
At that same moment, at the front door, my
wife was - making the same old excuses to the
agent.
“It’s no use,” I heard him say, “My friend
must make a call on you.”
Hib friend was the bailiff—and he called.
Next morning when I went out with the
bailiff—he knew me—for a drink, I saw that my
tenant had gone, and had taken with him all the
roses that covered the cottage, and every lock
from every door, and every bit of iron, or lead, or
brass, he could lay his hands upon.
I have had many bailiffs since then, but no more
tenants.
VIOTOR J. DALEY.
Marble and Mire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were daring in our Springtime, for we left the
lands of old,
And we laughed at myth and mystic and the
legendary gold,
And the faith our fathers cherished, we dismissed
it with a jeer,
As a feeble thing and futile for the days that drew
a-near;
And we boasted in our puissance we would fashion
things anew,
Till a world of whitest wonder into vision round
us grew;
We would turn to fairest temple ev’ry dream of
our desire,
We would build a State of marble from a people
made of mire !
O, the cities of our vision—o, the castles of our
dream !
0, the spires that kissed the heavens and were
mirrored in the stream !
0, the laughing Peaks of Morning—o, the loving
lands of Day ;
And the clasped hands of nations that forgot to
fight and slay !
0, the cannon that we buried with the helmet and
the spear !
0 the grave wherein we covered ancient Mammon
with his gear!
0 the gods who laughed above us when they saw
the fools aspire
To the lifting up of marble when their hands are
filled with mire !
And the Sun beheld us toiling in his splendour as
he rose,
And the Day beheld us moiling all aweary at his
close,
And the Moon in gentle pity looked upon us sweet
and far :
(She had seen the Tower of Babel that should kiss
with yonder star.)
And the sages, who were watching with a wonder
in their eyes,
In the’r kindness to our blindness would admonish
and advise,
But we bade them wait a moment till we brought
the glory nigher,
Till we wrought a state of marble with a people
made of mire.
We are weak and we are weary—and our grief has
left us gray,
And the glories that we dreamt of are but pun>
heaps of clay,
And the men we thought of raising have but
spurned us with their feet,
And our noblest and our bravest have been stoned
in yonder street ;
And our broad and deep foundations were uprent
with pick and bar,
And the people clamoured— “ Leave us ! we are
happy as we are ! ”
And the axe was ever o’er us with the scourge and
with the fire—
We had dared to think of marble when the souls of
men were mire I
There were gentle souls who loved us, but we left
them sad and lone,
That the work might wax and prosper and the
goodly seed be sown ;
And they died in desolation, for the land was
waste and seie,
When we did not heed the calling of the gentle
hearts and dear;
But the weary world is left us with its scoffing
and its scorn,
With its chilling shades of Even and its memories
of Morn,
And the temple we were building is a burnt and
blackened pyre—
O, the curse of craving marble when the hearts of
men are mire !
Brisbane.
P. LUETIC.


A PAGE OF VERSES WRITTEN FOR “THE BULLETIN.”
Proletaria.
The sunny rounds of Earth contain
An obverse to its Day,
Our fertile Vagrancy’s domain,
Wan Proletaria.
From pole to pole of Poverty
We stumble through the years,
With hazy-lanterned Memory
And Hope-that-never-nears.
Wherever Plenty’s crop invites
Our pitiful brigades,
Lurk cannoneers of Vested Rights,
Juristic ambuscades.
And here hangs Rent, that squalid cage
Within which Mammon thrusts,
Bound with the fetter of a wage,
The helots of his lusts.
With palsied Doubt as guide, we wind
Among the lanes of Need,
Where meagre Hungers scouting find
But slavered baits of Greed.
The wet-lipped Lamia’s of Caste,
Awaiting our advance,
Our choicest squadrons’ fealty blast
With magic smile and glance :
Delilah-limbed temptations flit
Among our drowsy rows,
And on our willing captains fit
The badges of our foes.
What wonder sometimes if in stealth
Our starker outposts wait,
And, in the prowling eyes of Wealth,
Dash vitriol of Hate !
Or if our Samsons, ere too late,
Their treasons should make good,
By whelming in the temples’ fate
Their viper owners' brood !
Our polyandrous dam has borne
To Satan and to God
The hordes of Night, the clans of Morn,
That through our valleys plod.
Ah, motherhood of misery
For Christ-child as for pest !
The more her womb’s fertility
The drier grows her breast !
Too many linger on the track ;
A few outstrip the time ;
Some, God has tattooed yellow, black,
And some disguised with crime.
Art’s living archives here abound,
Carraras of Despair,
And those weird masks of Sight and Sound
The Tragic Muses wear.
Tho’ blind and dull, ’tis we supply
The painter’s dazzling dreams ;
The rolling flood of poetry
From our dumb chaos streams.
Nay, when your world is over-tired,
And Genius comatose,
Our race, by Nemesis inspired,
Old Order overthrows.
With earthquake-life we thrill your land,
Refill the cruse of Art,
Revitalise spent Wisdom, and
Resume our weary part.
The palace of successful Guilt
Is mortared with our shame;
On hecatombs of Us are built
The soaring towers of Fame.
We are the gnomes of Titan works
Whose throbbings never cease ;
Our unregarded signet lurks
On every masterpiece.
The floating isles, that shuttling tie
All peoples into one
By adept steersmen’s sorcery
Of magnet, steam, and sun ;
Religion’s hypocritic spires
And printed armouries;
The Helot lightning of the wires
That mesh your lands and seas ;
The viaducts ’tween Near and Far,
Whereon, o’er range and mead,
B icchantic Trade’s triumphal car
And iron tigers speed ;
The modern steely crops that rise
Where Technic Jasons sow :
—All these but feebly symbolise
The largesse we bestow.
And our reward ? In this wan land,
In clientage of Greed,
Despised, polluted, maimed, and banned,
To wander and —to breed.
V.
DANTON.
Kyr ielle: Of Her Eyes.
The room was dim. The lazy, flickering fire
Threw gleams of shadow, but few gleams of light;
Outside, the night-winds murmured menace dire.
Only her eyes—her sweet, moist eyes—were
bright.
We kissed and clung, and clung and kissed again ;
Our hearts made music to our souls’ despite.
My broken life lay languishing in pain . . .
Only her eyes—her sweet, moist eyes—were
bright.
“ To-night we love, to-morrow part. The rest ?
Who cares ! Nothing remains beyond to-night ! ”
She sobbed in answer, lying on my breast:
Only ... her eyes—her sweet, moist eyes—were
bright.
“ My soul is yours, my body, Love ! ” she cried.
“ Joy lingers with us till to-morrow’s light! ”
She wept and whispered love-words by my side;
Only her eyes—her sweet, moist eyes—were
bright.
So in ray arms she lay till Dawn peeped through
The blinds —Dawn with all miseries bedight.
She went, and took with her all hope I knew . . .
Only her eyes—her sweet, moist eyes—were
bright.
At noon she came again, and consciously
Flung to my ready arms . . . depth of delight !
Her face was pale. Her lips moved tremulously.
Only her eyes—her sweet, moist eyes—were
bright.
“For you,” she said, “ I give up all of good
That women prize, save love ; in all men’s sight
An outcast! ” And she trembled as she stood :
Only her eyes—her sweet, moist eyes—were
bright.

FRANK MORTON.
The Glory-Call.
They sat at wine, their eyes aglow,
Impassioned, large, and fierce with heat,
Nor heard the moments come and go
With soft and unresisting feet.
He sat with them, his beard adrip
With slowly-gathering golden dew ;
And evermore his burning lip
He quenched in yellow wine, and drew
A grea-t breath in, as though some fate
Had snatched him from uncouth desire.
All round about his comrades sate
With wine-drenched hands and eyes of fire.
Great sweetness thrilled them, and on love
They lingered, each strong life astart
With some old anguish close enwove
In the red tissue of its heart.
Power surged them, and they felt the strength
Of some lean lion in the grey,
Grey dusk that hurls his tawny length
Far forth upon the reeling prey.
One cursed the subtle clinging vine
That robbed his soul of wholesome breath ;
One swayed afoot with lifted wine
And drank derisively to Death.
White women names that shone serene
Above the tumbled mists of ill
Were clouded then, and Malice keen
With savage hunger ranged at will.
But ever as with laugh or sigh
They pledged and filled from eve to morn,
The cynic hours went slowly by
With sidelong eyes of ancient scorn.
He sat with them aloof and mute,
His lips compressed, his hands atwine ;
Strange scorn of self, with eye acute,
Had reft the witchery of wine.
He shook their voices from his ear,
The wine flung far—they saw it fall;
He heard bell-toned, high-raised and clear,
A Forward-Call, a Glory-Call !
He turned, but suddenly were raised
The tongues of friends with olden love
Gold-winged and sweet; soft words that praised
The rose path, and strong hands that strove.
He freed his soul; then softly in
Girl-visions floated, faces fresh
With lovesomeness and coy with sin,
Soft eyes dark-lashed and velvet flesh.
Some wound his hands with tressy train
Of scented hair, and some his heart
Assailed with song, but all in vain ;
He burst their wiles like threads apart.
Then warred the Past against his Will,
Her dagger tipped with bitter gall,
But fruitlessly; to shafts of ill
He stood an adamantine wall.
Thus forth he fared, and far behind
The revel faded ; yet a voice
Down stealing on a subtle wind
With satire stung his austere choice.
Men-ties and maid-ties, ties that wed
The world in one large love and trust,
From sudden severance dripped with red,
Till all his heart grew dry as dust.
He trod the woe-way, white and worn,
Foot-worn and white with dust of brave
Fore-stravers—led by some forlorn
But lovely hope to dare the grave.
Winds blew —he cared not; fell the rain—
He marched along with strength upstrung ;
No wine or kiss could wake again
Delicious memories on his tongue.
He kept his way, and when the years
Had wrung his soul with sobs and moans,
Came, through a wood-way dripping tears,
Unto a temple built of bones.
He entered, feeling here at least
A glory-crown should greet his eyes :
He saw instead a cowled priest,
An altar and a sacrifice.
Slow steps he made, and paused beside
The sacrifice, and strangely shook
With mortal fears, and opened wide,
Wide eyes, and, leaning downward, took
The veil away, and saw beneath
(The eyes how dim—how dull the gold !)
His slain youth there, sublime in death,
But pale and passionless and cold.
World-voices entered down the arch
Of Time defiant, with a weight
Of story—like a forest march
Of lions, dominant and great.
He heard them—heard without a sigh,
Transcendent in the light of morn . . .
But still the cynic hours went by
With sidelong eyes of ancient 3Com.

RODERIO QUINN.
Love- Tokens.
They glitter golden in the light
Those Cupid-gifts of yore,
Pale roses that were red or white,
Dear roses that she wore ;
Her first short letter—Ah, how sweet
That word of Long Ago !
And fluttering softly to my feet
Her last—how full of woe 1
I see between the roses twine
The wraiths of buried years,
I read between each fading line
Her laughter touched with tears ;
The broken sixpence joins its mate,
The gold tress finds its sheen,
And still I watch alone and wait
With all that might have been.
As some grey-bearded man may stand
In garden pathways fair,
And say “ ’Twas here I held her hand,
Here stroked her golden hair ;
And yonder, where the larkspur grows,
And the slow brown bee sips,
That very place, ’twixt rose and rose,
I kissed her rosier lips.”
I lift the love-gifts one by one,
And move them to and fro,
To turn and ted them in the sun
That laughed on Long Ago;
With such a pride as one may feel
Who names a treasured hoard :
“ This was my mother’s spinning-wheel,
And this my father’s sword.”
Of these, long-cherishcd lovingly
Through all the lonely years,
Weave only one brown wreath for me
Twined out and in with tears,
And bring them where the myalls wave
And lay them on my breast —
Her hair, her roses in my grave
Will give me better rest.
N.S.W.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
Larva Loquitur.
Listen, among your violets and wine,
White moonbeams fighting with the lamp’s red
ray,
Away beneath yon windy, crying pine,
I stand, and watch, and call, and plead, and pray.
And you, you heed not there beneath her spell,
With violets and music, and your Love,
White-throated like the warm, white, trailorous
snow
Strayed limbs seek fainting when their hope is
low.
(Loquitur Larva l Little ghost and grey,
Poor little ghost, gone out so long ago.
Why do you fret the eve of this white day,
With your imperious whispers. Ah, no, no.)
Listen, across your happy fire-lit eyes
I pass, pass, pass. Look back. Remember me.
The fire is fading and the violets droop,
And your warm-throated Love turns, shiveringly.
I steal between you. Here I stand and pray.
Here at your side, outside the lamp’s red reach.
Fool, do you think you have forgotten me 1
I am the Ghost that you have Ceased to Be.
(Loquitur Larva ! Bitter, hitter ghost,
I laid thee sleeping many and many a year,
I smothered thee with roses, and the most
Of life's good things I heaped upon thy bier.)
Ah, but I tossed the roses to the deep,
I threw away the memory of the tear.
I rose with shaking haste from death’s dull sleep,
Shattering the rotting gifts upon my bier.
And through the moonlight and the sighing pines,
And through the wet dark gardens of your home,
I come to warn you that my sleep is o’er,
That I will leave your side no more, no more.
CLoquitur Larva ! Oh, remorseless Past l
Then all my wounds and sacrifice are vain.
I suffer, suffer, suffer —hold me fast,
Oh, woman of my sold l laugh down the pain.)
Hush, would you tell the story of your Past
To that fair face that must recoil and spurn,
And turn away, grown sudden grey and ghast,
All fairness faded, while you watch and yearn,
And cry for love and cry in vain, “ Absolve ! ”
And plead for pity she will never give,
Her love drowned deep in seas of doubt and dread.
I take your hand, and turn your heart to lead.
(Out of the Dead Years, out of Sacrifice,
Bitterly burnt on many an altar pile,
Is there no spirit-shape from Paradise
Will haunt this phantom back to Death's dark aisle!)
Lo, I have wrecked your roses and your tears,
Weak offerings to the memory of your wrong.
Deep, deep my sleep, but through the silent years
Came creeping the new cadence in your song.
Deep, deep my sleep, but through its depths your
voice
Plunged sharper than a sword, and I awoke.
Calling aloud her happy, haunting name,
You pierced my sleep until I woke, and came.
(Out of the Dead Years, out of Sacrifice,
Bitterly burnt on many an altar pile,
Is there no spirit-hand from Paradise
Will drive this phantom back to Death's dark aisle ?)
Listen, among your violets and wine,
White moonbeams striving with the lamp s red
ray,
I leave the shelter of the windy pine,
And closer creep, and closer cling, alway.
Ah, hush! . . . Why does his voice fall whispering
Into the meshes of her yellow hair . . .
(Out of the Dead Years, out of Sacrifice,
N~o hand reached down to turn thee to thy bier.
’Twas She who leaned, as if from Paradise,
And drove thee into darkness with one tear.)
N.B.W.
LOUISE MACK.
The Dreamer.
The hour of beauty over, he arose
And left the house and book, and when a-nigh
The bush that follows where the streamlet goes
He heard a lamb’s faint cry.
A lonely note was in the spring-born’s bleat,
A hint of some field-sorrowing he read,
And to the stream he turned his wandering feet,
And found the mother dead.
Unpitying law, use, kindred, foul decay,
Pressed on his soul so delicate and fair :
“ Behold the dreamer cometh, let us slay! ”
Was whispered in the air.
Significant the rustle in the grass,
The sudden raising of the horned'brute head
Of hidden bull —he felt the whisper pass :
“ He cometh, lay him dead ! ”
The marsh was heavy with a storm-blue cloud,
The channel brimming full, the bridge was down,
And from far creeks and pools the voice was
loud :
“We have him, let him drown ! ”
He stood beside the river on the shore,
The reeds were straining, sad each creek and
bend,
Beyond the sandhills came the ocean roar,
He said, “ Here is the end ! ”
When, lo ! the great magician sun shone out,
The spaces of the marsh were searched with light;
Red cattle wandered ; rippled by the trout,
The pools were blue and white.
The quivering reeds set in a golden sheet
Made old Pan music, and the gums’ white boles
Were yellow-warm, and with a thundering beat
Swept-by the startled foals.
Homeward the dreamer went, his soul at rest,
Steadfast, consoled, unvext by coward fears,
And pitiful : and making to the west,
The sun burned on the Tiers.

JAMES HEBBLETHWAITE.
When London Calls.
They leave us—artists, singers, all—
When London calls aloud,
Commanding to her Festival
The gifted crowd.
She sits beside the ship-choked Thames,
Sad, weary, cruel, grand ;
Her crown imperial gleams with gems
From many a land.
From overseas, and far away,
Come crowded ships and ships—
Grim-faced she gazes on them ; yea,
With scornful lips.
The garden of the earth is wide;
Its rarest blooms she picks
To deck her board, this haggard-eyed
Imperatrix.
Sad, sad is she, and yearns for mirth :
With voice of golden guile
She lures men from the ends of earth
To make her smile.
The student of wild human ways
In wild new lands ; the sage
With new great thoughts ; the bard whose lays
Bring youth to age ;
The painter young whose pictures shine
With colours magical,
The singer with the voice divine—
She lures them all.
But all their new is old to her
Who bore the Anakim ;
She gives them gold or Charon’s fare
As suits her whim.
Crowned Ogress—old, and sad, and wise—
She sits with painted face
And hard, imperious, cruel eyes
In her high place.
To him who for her pleasure lives,
And makes her wish his goal,
' A rich Tarpeian gift she gives—
That slays his soul.
The story-teller from the Isles
Upon the Empire’s rim,
With smiles she welcomes—and her smiles
Are death to him.
For Her, whose pleasure is her law,
In vain the shy heart bleeds—
The Genius with the Iron Jaw
Alone succeeds.
And when the Poet’s lays grow bland,
And urbanised, and prim
She stretches forth a jewelled hand
And strangles him.
She sits beside the ship-choked Thames,
With Sphinx-like lips apart—
Mistress of many diadems—
Death in her heart !

Nissen's Secret.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John Westbrook, master-mariner, sailed one fine
morning out of Port Jackson on a six months’
trading cruise among the South Sea Islands, and
he never returned. His wife, having perfunctorily
waited the six months, with two months’ grace
added, and being too indifferent to make any in-
quiries, conveniently gazetted him dead, and
married Job Dixon, master-mariner, of the
schooner Neptune. In due course he sailed out of
Port Jackson on an extended cruise, and he never
returned. And the man who holds the key to the
mystery is precisely the one most oblivious of
there being any key to hold.
* * * *
The trading settlement lay bathed in brilliant
sunlight. Against the clear sky the palms were
defined to their smallest twigs. A big-boned,
pale-faced man sat on a pile of lumber by the
landing-place, and listlessly eyed a schooner that
was coming slowly up the lagoon. On the ground
behind lolled a little, shock-headed man with big,
vacant eyes and a significant droop of his nether
lip.
Two idlers strolled down from the station and
stood at a little distance.
“ Who’re they ?” queried one.
“ What! ” The other looked surprised.
“Don’t know Captain Westbrook and his man
Friday—watch-dog, body-guard, and all things in
that line ? But, then, this is your first visit to
the Gilbert Islands.”
“Well? tell me ! ”
“The story? Oh, there isn’t much of it.
Westbrook used to trade between these islands
and Sydney, but on his last visit with his ship he
took a cargo for ’Frisco. Coming back, he ran
into a breeze, and his old hulk strained so badly
that her crew had only time to take to the boats,
without food or water—or with very little —before
she went down. They moped about—l don’t
know how many days—going off by degrees till
only the captain and his familiar were left.
The little man—Nissen —was loony when the
Tortoise picked ’em up, and he’s a bit loony
now. The thing turned his brain. When
they found them, he was nursing the uncon-
scious captain, damping his head with a wet cloth,
and crooning to him one of those things the women
sing to youngsters. Now, he seems to think he
has a mission to watch over the “old man” —l
believe he thinks he’s a dog, though he doesn’t
bark. He’s always at his heels.”
“Ah! queer yarn,” the first man said, and
pointed to the schooner, which had now got an
anchor down. “ What craft’s that ? ”
“ That! Oh ! that’s the Neptune. Been
tramping about the islands a good while, this trip.
She was here a little time back. Left just before
the Tortoise brought those castaways here.”
A boat put off from the schooner, and landed
her skipper. Captain Westbrook looked after
him, nodded his head like a man who has a
thought, and strolled off up to the saloon. Nissen,
the watch-dog, followed at heel.
* * * *
“So she killed me at short notice, and married
you—eh ? ”
Captain Westbrook, grim-visaged and porten-
tous, faced the Neptune’s skipper across a table
in the saloon. Witless Nissen, excluded from
the room, stumped the verandah, disconso-
late. Captain Job Dixon, one hand drumming
the table nervously, the other thrust into his
jacket-pocket, was silent for awhile.
“ But —how did you know ? ” lie blurted at last.
The other laughed.
“Inquired,” he said. “I wanted news from
home. You were busy with the agent, so I
boarded one of your crew whom I happened to
know. He told me my wife had given me up as
dead, and married again. Married you —he said.
True, isn’t it ? ”
The Neptune’s skipper nodded.
“I took a run to ’Frisco,” Westbrook said.
“But I wrote to her, telling her of it. And she
thought I was dead ! Waited—let me see !
about eight months. Long time ! ”
“ See ! ” said Dixon, impatiently. “ This is all
rot. The woman’s right enough. I swear she
never got your letter—if you wrote one,” he added,
not so softly but that Westbrook heard.
“Don’t call me a liar,” he snarled. “I did
write. But—what are you going to do about it ?
Both can’t have the woman.”
“ No! ” Captain Job eyed the ceiling thought-
fully. “ Only one of us must go back.”
Westbrook laughed, leaned forward, and tapped
the other man on the wrist with his forefinger.
His voice, husky with the intensity of his signifi-
cance, hardly rose above a whisper.
“ You’ve said it — only one of us must go back! ”
Dixon sat bolt upright, white-faced and pal-
pably shaking, as the other’s meaning leapt into
his understanding.
“What!” he gasped. “You mean ... I
didn’t mean that ! No ! no ! ” —and he forced a
laugh. “You always were a joker, Westbrook,
and you’re at it again.”
“ I mean,” came the steady answer, “ only one
of us must go back 1 ”
“But—good heavens, man! you’re mad! I tell
you it can’t be —it’s horrible, horrible ! ”
“ Bah ! you carry a pistol, I’ll be bound, and
know how to use it, too. How often have you
used it ? ”
“Never!” said Dixon, earnestly. “Never, I
swear—on a man. Why—it’s murder ! ”
“Murder!—rubbish! we’ll fight fair. Both
can’t have her.”
“Well—well,” Dixon said, helplessly, “can’t
we arrange? . . . You don’t want her.” . . . His
voice died, and he rose to his feet.
“ You won’t have her, anyway.” Westbrook
jumped up in a rage. “ Fight, you hound, or I’ll
strangle you. Only one of us . . .”
At the threat, clinched by a swift forward
movement, coolness came to Dixon. He jerked
his right hand from his pocket, and the storming
Westbrook calmed in an instant before a menacing
revolver muzzle.
For ten seconds a life trembled on Dixon’s
trigger finger. Then he pocketed the weapon and
strode for the door.
“We’ll take a walk—you and I,” he said
through his teeth, “and ” parrot-like “only one
of us shall come back ! ”
* * • *
Nissen, still doing sentry-go on the verandah,
saw them leave, and, with an almost sane idea
that something was wrong, sneaked after them.
The two men skirted the beach till a bluff of rocks
screened them from all but very inquisitive eyes.
SHE WAS A BIT TOUGH.
MISS PASSY (old mutton dressed as lamb): “ They say the paw-paw is a wonderful tree. If you
hang up meat near it, the meat will become tender.”
MISS YOUNG : “How nice it would be to have one where you could sit in the shade of it, dear!”
Nissen scaled the bluff and dropped un-
seen behind a rock commanding a
view of the little, half-moon-shaped
battle-ground, and the
broad Sea outside.
When he lifced
his head cau-
tiously above the
rock he saw the
men facing each
other, pistols in
hand and lips
shut hard. He
gasped in dis-
may, and his
revolver, in-
stantly grin-
ning over the
rock, made the
third in the
drama ! He
