the one to go
back should
not be Captain
Dixon. I
Two shots
rang as one,
echoing a score
of times in the
little amphi-
theatre, and
two billetless
bullets flatten-
ed on the
rocks. West-
brook’s hand went
up.
“ Stop!” he shout- /
ed—“this won’t do!
These cursed revolvers
sound like cannon. We’ll
have a whole pack of gap-
ing dogs from the station
down upon us in a minute.”
“Well?” said
Dixon, tensely, over
his revolver sus-
pecting a trick.
“ Why knives, of
course,” said Westbrook, dropping his pistol*
“What else did we bring ’em for?”
Dixon recoiled a pace, shivered, and began to
protest. But the other only laughed and reached
to his belt. On the motion, Dixon drew, and
sprang to meet him, shouting defiance.
From the security of his entrenchment Nissen
eyed the combat with dull comprehension of its
tragic features, but with a certain relish, never-
theless. The consciousness that he held in his
hand power to decide the issue at will set him
chuckling gleefully.
Suddenly he saw the contending men stagger
and fall, Westbrook beneath. He screamed once
like a wounded horse, and simultaneously his
revolver went up, hurriedly drew a line on Dixon,
and barked.
For just one hour Dixon thought he had
killed Captain Westbrook. Panting with
rage and exertion, blood roaring in his ears, he
had not noticed the pistol explosion. What he
knew was that the squirming form beneath him
suddenly relaxed and lay still. Knife in hand, he
jumped to his feet, looked, then turned, and ran
like the murderer he thought he was.
For minutes afterwards, Nissen watched the
sea with intense eyes and quivering underlip.
Then he scrambled down the slope—mechanically,
like a man who does one thing and thinks of
another. There was no shake in his hands as he
lifted the man his well-meant but luckless shot
had slain. He bore him to a projecting ledge,
and dropped him into deep water. Then he
returned and—as far as possible—destroyed all
traces of the tragedy. All this with a stolid, pre-
occupied air, as if his dwarfed wits had met a
problem. And they had. He felt he had some-
thing else to do—but what was it ?
Nissen could not think.
• •* * *
It was an hour later that Captain Dixon
emerged, with whisky-steadied nerves, from the
saloon. The criminal does sometimes re-visit
the scene of the crime—but it was no “mysterious
attraction ” that took Captain Dixon there. He
went, on an afterthought, through the gathering
dusk, to recover his forgotten revolver, which
might have told a tale—and to throw something
to the sharks. Nissen, still hunting the phantom-
answer to his question, saw him, and the answer
materialised. He crouched behind a rock, and
waited.
Dixon, half-drunk and swayed by one idea,
saw nothing, not even the sought revolver—which,
seeing that Nissen clutched it, is not surprising.
With the aimlessness of a bewildered man he
staggered out on the projecting ledge, and the
movement called Nissen to action. He had some-
thing to do ! He stepped stealthily after. Sud-
denly his hand went up, and the Neptune’s
skipper went out in a spirt of red flame and a
splash in the sea.
“ THE HOLY TRUTH.”
BENEVOLENT OLD LADY: “Now, if I give you a
shilling will you promise me not to waste any
of it?”
HOPELESS BILL: “ Yes, lady; not a drop.”
And, with the act, the dregs of Nissen’s reason
ran quite out, and he turned towards the settle-
ment trolling a coarse sea-song. The secret was
safe with him —for he had already forgotten it!
P. T. FREEMAN.
Tim, the bush-carpenter, expatiates on the
text that “You has to be up to snuff a bit when
you’re on your uppers ” :
I was in Melbourne once, an’ hadn’t had a taste of beer
for near two days ; next to mad I was. Went up to
the door of one of them semi-detached villas they has in
the suburbs, an’ made the usual pitch to the missus. She
gets me into the kitchen an’ gives me a feed. Well, I had
to stow it away, tho’ I wasn’t a bit hungry—l’d just had
a good breakfast in the Mission, you see—but I had to, or
she’d think me a himposter. Then I starts the ole tale o’
sick wife an’ kids. She listens indifferent like, you know,
and then says, if I’d go to the cemetery an’ black-enamel
the names on a tombstone that she’d tell me the lay of,
why then, if it was done proper, I was to get ’arf-a-
crown. I told her I was that handy that I could
make a noo tombstone, if she wanted it. She
give me two bob to buy the enamel, so I has three
beers at a pub not too close. Then a couple of browns,
or so, gets me a little lamp-black and another copper
some oil to mix with it. I picks up a bit of bath-brick
too, to scrape down the blessed old tombstone with, and
of course I ’ad my quart billy o’ water too. First I
scrapes an’ washes down the stone. Then I lamp-blacks
it. Next I ’as a walk about the cemetery paths smokin’;
it was Monday mornin’ and not a soul there ’ardly, an’ I
’ad to let the thing dry, you see. Comes back an’ washes
all off excep, on course, what ’ad stopped in the letters.
The ’ole aotool job didn’t take me more nor ten minutes.
Nex’ day I bowls up to the ’ouse and the missus lets me
in beamin’ !—such a blow out as she had for me ! ‘ You
done that there letterin’ bootiful! ’ she says when I’d
finished ; 1 just bootiful! ’ I coughed an’ expresses my
gratitood o’ course. 1 But it must have took you ’ours ’an
’ours paintin’ in all that ebony enamel so careful.
There it is, so lovely black an’ shinin’! ’Ow many
’ours now was you?’ I tells her the biggest number
I thought she’d stomach, gets me bit of gold
an’ clears. There was heavy rain that night an’
I took a look over the fence next morning. The ’ole
family was there cussin’. Ter save me bloomin’ life I
couldn’t help bustin’ out larfin’, an’ next thing I knew
my bathbrick wot I’d left behind took me right under the
ear, an’ I cleared for me natural, with the ole woman’s
son arter me—hell-for-leather. I believe he’d a-got me
right enough, too, if a bloomin’ big dorg hadn’t rushed at
’im out of somebody’s front garden ; an’, by the time he’d
done arguin’ the pint with the dorg, I was a clean goner.


By the Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Isaac Lang, manager of the lease and belong-
ings of the Deep Coal Mining Company, Gipps-
land, was trudging from the nearest township out
to his people’s property, a narrow seam of unser-
viceable material at the end of a small tunnel at
the bottom of a gorge. The day was still young,
but the morning sun was hot and the way dreary
and wearisome. The cart-track writhed
along in a slow, despairing fashion, strangely sug-
gestive of dawdling bullock-team.s and indifferent
drivers; winding about fallen logs and sleeping
AN ABUSE OF CONFIDENCE.
CANNIBAL CHIEF: “ Thou crawling hypocrite! Sec what I found in
the pocket of the old cat thou gavest me this morning ! This
half-finished bottle tells its own tale of abject deceit. But,
ha ! think not to foil me thus. I’LL FATTEN THOU YET!"
oiumps, between tall, ragged white-gums and
occasional stunted cherry-trees. On the right an
interminable fence with a decided lurch to the
north ran out and on and on into eternity ; on the
left a fence without beginning or end, and with an
eager list to the south, raced back to infinity ; and,
right and left, before and behind, were baked grass
and gaunt, gesticulating trees with torn beards
and up-thrown hands, like chaffering Hebrews.
So far Lang had discovered only one break in
the monotony, but was grateful for that—the
weather-beaten fragments of a theatrical streamer
hanging from a smooth dead butt, advertising the
superlative merits of certain unknown artists in
song and dance, a strange and impertinent note in
an empty land.
Lang turned from the road at a familiar point,
and, facing a spur of the range to the left, cursed
in his heart the prospect of the climb in store, and
struck across country. After walking for half-an-
hour he came upon a lone slab-house built at a
little distance back from another track running at
right angles with the “main road.” He had
trudged the distance twice before, and had
noticed the house with passing wonder; amazed
that any sane man or woman could find motive for
remaining in that dismal, aching solitude, with the
whole wide busy world around them to choose
from. How the occupants lived, and, above all,
why, were problems that had engaged his atten-
tion temporarily ; but to-day he turned from his
straight course, and approached the house, at-
tracted by the sounds of loudly-disputing voices
and the clamor of dogs.
At the slip-panels two patient horses were
standing ready saddled. Before the door, in the
pits dug by burrowing pigs and the dust raked up
by many hens, two men hugging each other in
desperate grip were rolling and rioting. Around
these circled two large cattle-dogs, barking
amazedly, and half-a-dozen small mongrels made
sudden incursions and quick retreats, snapping at
the combatants, and yelping without cessation.
J ust as Lang seated himself comfortably, the cattle-
dogs clashed in their gyrations, and fastening,
fought tremendously over the bodies of their mas-
ters. A very tall thin old man and an exceedingly
stout, short old woman moved excitedly on the
outskirts of battle, protesting shrilly, much dis-
tressed, and a young woman leaned composedly
against the shoulder of a horse at a little distance,
apparently awaiting the issue of the conflict with
bland indifference.
The old man discovered Lang, and ran a few
steps towards him, beckoning excitedly.
“ Hi, hi, mister, mister !” he called.
The manager slipped from his rail, and, thrusting
his hands in his pockets, walked towards the house
in a leisurely manner that asserted his strict
neutrality. He stood for a moment looking down
at the combatants.
“ What’s the row?” he said.
“ Stop ’em ! Stop ’em ! ” ci-ied the long thin old
man fiercely.
“ Oh ! please do stop ’em,” wailed the thick old
woman.
Isaac regarded the fight dubiously.
“ It’s no business of mine, an’ it isn’t for me to
interfere rightly,” he said, “but ”
He seized the topmost combatant, and dragged
the men to their feet ; then with a pair of brawny
arms he pulled them apart, and held them.
“ Here, here ! no more silly business,” he said.
“ What’s all this about ? ”
The enemies were young men, under 25, one
very dark, the other fair, but both reduced to a
common complexion by the dust they had wal-
lowed in. They glared at each other, gasping for
breath.
“That swine wants to get away with my
girl! ” panted one.
“ It’s a lie —she’s mine ! ’’
blurted His rival.
Then they broke from
Lang, and rushed together
again, gripped and went
down, and raged in the
dust once more. Mean-
while the two big dogs
were fighting at large, and
monopolising the attentions
of all the little dogs; the
old couple were wringing
their hands in terror, splut-
tering and weakly protest-
ing; the girl remained
propped against her horse,
waiting patiently for a de-
finite settlement, and ap-
parently quite without
prejudice.
Isaac Lang took a fresh
hold, parted the enemies,
and stood between them, a
hand on the shirt-collar of
each.
“ Aleck, do behave ! ”
whined the old woman.
“ Y’ orter know better.”
“ Think I’m goin’ to let
him do me*out of my girl! ”
snorted Aleck, indignantly.
“She promised me, ye
thief!”
There was a struggle, but
it was only momentary ;
the big man twisted his
grip at the throat of each,
and they were still.
“It appears t’ me the
young lady can settle this,”
said Lang, suggestively.
“Linda!” squealed the
old woman.
Linda stirred awkwardly.
“ I dunno,” she said.
Linda was not beautiful.
Her hair was the color of
the baked grass; her face
was amazingly freckled, and
she had shocking feet, the
toes of which met inquisitively. She remained
passive.
“But I s’pose you’re the party referred to?”
said the astonished mediator.
“ Yes, she sez to me, on’y lars Sunday week ”
“ But she promised me long before that! ”
“Shut up ! ” Lang put the screw on again.
“Y’orter be ashamed, Jim Massen,” wailed
the agitated mother, in fretful terror.
“Don’t you want either of ’em, Miss?’’asked
Lang, with the air of a man who was determined
to see the matter through.
“ Linda! ” cried the old woman.
The girl blinked.
“I dunno—l s’pose so.”
“Which, then?”
“ I dunno. Which’d you say ? ”
“Oh, it’s nothin’ to do with me, Miss.”
Puzzled by this unpromising
situation, and feeling the folly
of intervening in so very in-
definite a dispute, Lang
liberated his prisoners,
and a wild and whirl-
ing disputation en-
sued, in which Jim
and Aleck and the
old couple engaged
with all their
energy. It only
lasted a minute or so,
as the rivals presently
fell to blows again,
toppled over locked

and recommenc-

attack, plung-
ed in the

renewed dis-
may of Linda’s

“ Mister ! Mister ! ”
piped the miserable
old man.
“Look here,” said
Isaac philosophically,
“ you let ’em be for a
while. They can’t hurt
each other much, an’
it’ll do ’em all sorts of

The old man seemed
relieved.
“I don’t want no
one killed on my laud,” he said.
“There won’t be any killin’, take my word,”
said Lang soothingly.
The dogs were a quarter of a mile off by tins
time, still fighting, and for ten minutes Lang and
Linda’s parents overlooked the battle in the dust;
the latter reassured and comparatively calm. And
now the manager heard the story of Linda’s loves.
11 Linda alwa’s was an undecided
so-to-speak sort o’ girl,” said the
old man. “She never knows what
she wants, an’ when she gets what
she thinks she wants, well, it turns
out ’taint what she wants after all.
You know what young girls is.”
Linda, who was 24, had, it
seemed, engaged herself to Aleck
in a moment of mental aberration,
and had promised to marry him on
that day and date. This was quite
satisfactory, but unfortunately Jim
had ridden up at the same time as
Aleck, and he reminded the young
lady that she had a previous
appointment with him for a mar-
riage, date unfixed. Trouble en-
sued. The girl was quite reason-
able according to her lights, and
declared that she would marry
either as soon as they would decide
which.
Isaac Lang began to be delighted
with the situation. It appealed to
his sense of humor, and he sud-
denly conceived an ambition to
clear up the difficulty. The com-
batants were very weary by this
time ; their enthusiasm had waned,
and they were content to cling to
each other, breathing profanity and
abuse. Lang took them in hand
for the third time, and divided
them. He made a mark for Aleck,
and set him on it. Ten paces off
he made a mark for Jim. He
planted himself between them,
armed with a hoe.
“Now,” he said, with decision,
“ I’ll knock the seven senses out of
the first man that moves, an’ then
the other can marry the girl before
he coraes-to.”
Linda was still awaiting the
issue; she leaned upon her horse
in a condition of apathetic reserve.
“ Look here, miss,” said the arbiter; “ you can’t
marry the pair of ’em, can you ? ”
Linda thought it over gravely.
“ ’Tisn’t religious,” she hinted.
“’Tisn’t legal,” said Isaac. “Well, why not
pick one, an’ be done with it ? ”
The girl looked from one to the other, and shook
her head drearily.
“ She promised me first! ” blurted Jim.
“Silence!” Lang commanded, brandishing his
hoe. “ She promised to marry you first,” he con-
tinued, in a judicial tone, “ an’ p’raps it’s fair she
should do it, an’ leave Aleck to take his chances ;
but, seein’ it’s the rule for a woman to marry the
man she promised last, Aleck seems to have first
claim. So it only remains for the two of you to
toss for it, allowin’ that the young woman’s agree-
able.”
“ Linda ! ” squealed the mother.
“Are you agreeable to tossin’ for it, miss ?”
Linda was quite agreeable to anything, and so
Isaac produced a penny.
“I toss—you cry,” he said to Aleck. “Now,
sudden death ! ”
“ Head ! ” cried Aleck.
It was Tail.
“ She’s yours,” said the peacemaker to Jim, and
the successful young man hurrah’d and capered
like a kid, while Aleck glowered upon him omin-
ously.
Now, for the first time, Linda developed a sort
of sullen interest. She demurred.
“ I’m goin’ to marry Aleck,” she said.

of the opposite fence, he went at breakneck pace
across the paddocks in pursuit of Linda.

EDWARD DYSON.
Re fearsome experiences. Present
writer’s brother went out badger-hunting once on
Tas. West Coast with the commander of a stranded
timber-barge and a new-cbum. The captain bore
the shovel and supplied the loose language, whilst
the chum supported a deadly old shotgun tied up
with bootlaces. Writer’s brother did the badger-
tracking and offered up prayers. Towards even-
ing they got fogged, and the skipper shinned up a
gum to take observations. He was an enormously
bulky person, and the tree was slippery, so he
called upon the chum to give him a shove behind.
Just about here, writer’s brother, who was calmly
watching the antics of the sailor, observed his
hair begin to rise and his bulging eyeballs fix
themselves in horror. The chum was assisting
the speechless mariner’s ascent with the muzzle
of the gun—and the hammer at full-cock ! When
the perspiring tar reached the ground he hunted
the chum to the coast with the shovel, leaving a
trail of blasted timber in their wake. In calmer
moments he said he would have had one consola-
tion had the chum drilled a hole through him—in
flattening the miscreant out in his fall and strang-
ling him in his death-grip.
“San Remo": A farm-hand at Kabra,
Central Q., when passing one night through some
tall grass, felt a snake twine itself round his leg,
inside his trousers, and climb upwards. He bent
down, and made a wild grab at where he thought
its head was, and, with “the luck of a Chow,”
seized the head, with a handful of trouser. About
10 o’clock that night there came to the Gracemere
Hotel a scared-looking individual, bent double,
and clutching one leg of his trousers with a death-
grip. The apparition nodded its head wildly,
muttering “ Snake ! snake ! ” and we all gathered
round to investigate. The wriggling tail showing
above the boot explained the situation, and Gum-
sucker Bill at once took command. “ Sit down,
mate,” said he —and called for a drink, which the
stranger eagerly swallowed. Stretching out the
man’s leg, Bill caught the snake by the tail.
“ Now,” said he, “ when 1 call ‘ go ! ’ let her go ' ”
But he of the snake wouldn’t chance that. “I
don’t let up on this ’ere snake while I can dror a
breath,” he said. Then, after scratching his head
a bit and cursing a lot, Bill took his knife and cut
off the snake’s head and a piece of the pants ; but,
even then, whether from terror or cramp, the
stranger could not relax his grip. Bill had to
force open his fist with the handle of his knife, and
the snake’s head fell snapping on the floor.


An Awful Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mb. Joshua Lilly had been detained in the city
by what he called a fortuitous concourse of events,
and long after midnight he started on foot for his
home in a distant suburb, hoping to be overtaken
on the way by a belated cab. No cab came in the
course of the first mile, and Mr. Joshua Lilly was
tempted by considerations of Mrs. Joshua —a large
woman who always sought explanations and never
accepted them —to take a short cut across Jamtin
Flat, a level sweep of land reclaimed out of a
swamp. The rain came, and hapless Mr. Lilly,
scurrying like a wet hen, was washed across the
railway line into Quob’s Corner.
Quob’s Corner is a tangled section of a poor
suburb, hopelessly cut off from respectability, and
given over to impoverished drunkards and mean,
ambitionless criminals. Joshua Lilly, the very
acme of respectability when dry and with his
linen in good order, knew nothing of the reputa-
tion of Quob’s Corner, but its dark, desolate
streets chilled him, and something like terror
chased him though the pelting rain.
He was one of those thin, sleek, twittering
little men who contrive to support a dignity in
fair weather and under favorable circumstances,
but his claim upon popular respect was due almost
entirely to starch. Starch was his moral back-
Washed into Quob's corner.
bone, and with a limp shirt and a sodden collar he
was a pulpy, characterless mute, washing about
in the darkness of Quob’s Corner, and most
devoutly he wished himself at home.
Suddenly, hastening along, head down, boring
his way through the rain, Joshua collided smartly
with some tremendous mass which seemed to open
and envelop him. For a few moments he swayed
about helplessly, moved by the mighty bulk, and
then he got his head free, and discovered to his
great joy that he was in the hands of the police.
“It’s all right, officer ! ” he gasped ; “I’m just
on my way home.”
He was clasped tighter, tucked under the
abundant arm of the law, and the officer swayed
above him like a willow wand stirred by a gale.
Presently, the myrmidon began to drift to leeward;
then he brought up with a jerk, and went over to
windward, righted again, and stood oscillating
from his base with the movements of a nautch-
dancer, and Lilly drifted or danced with him,
feeling strangely like a ladybird tackling a
mastodon.
“Really, constable—most respectable man,”
protested Mr. Lilly; “name of Joshua Lilly.
Kept late—fortuitous events, you know.”
But his custodian was off again, driving wildly up
the street this time, with a dangerous list to port
and the strange aimlessness of a strip of paper
careering in the wind. He ran aground against a
brick building, on which he leaned to recover his
breath, still clinging desperately to Joshua, who
had lost his hat in the flight and had been
towed in the mud for half the distance.
“Come, I say, Mr. Policeman,” protested Lilly,
with pitiful indignation, “ this is too bad, sir—too
bad altogether ! ”
The officer of the law was quite oblivious; he
clung to Joshua automatically, as he might have
clung to any trifling article he had picked up in
passing, and gazed far off into the night with dim,
unseeing eyes, and began to sing, beating time
vaguely with his free hand :
“ She’s the mosh dishtreshful counthry that ever yit was
seen ;
They are hangin’ min an’ winimin for the wearin’ av the
green.”
The policeman propped himself at a more com-
fortable angle with his back to the building, and
the rain sluiced down, and ran in miniature falls
off his waterproof. A convenient street-lamp
illumined his gigantic proportions with a sickly
glimmer, but up and down the street ranged the
black, silent houses, and not another living soul
could Lilly see anywhere. A town clock struck
two. Joshua was wet, dirty, and filled with
misery; he recognised the awful truth, and his
heart sank within him like a thing of lead. The
law was drunk —fatuously, fantastically drunk.
He thought of Mrs. Lilly again, and revolted
weakly.
“Let me go, policeman. This is an outrage.
If you don’t let me go I shall most certainly report
you.”

Quob’s Corner bodily from its bed. Up and down

“Help! Help! Help!” cried the wretched

Joshua repeated his shrill appeal, and Hooley

He produced his handcuffs, snapped one ring

He took flight with the wind, and Lilly went
with him. In spite of his fat, Hooley had marvel-
lous endurance, and after doing three laps round
the square he was so little distressed by his frantic
spin and the burden of Lilly that he fetched-up
in the middle of the road with his back to the
storm, and sang a song in a roaring baritone.
Somebody in the second storey of a squalid resi-
dence hard by threw up a window and hurled
curses and crockery, also some knobs off an
iron bedstead. Lilly seized his opportunity.
“ Help ! Help ! Oh, help ! ” lie moaned.
“Get home, ye silly old cow!” cried a voice
out of the darkness, and the window rattled down.
And still the warm rain marched over the be-
lated couple in succeeding battalions, rank after
rank,, and Hooley sang and Lilly moaned feebly,
id, in accents that might have touched a heart
f beaten brass, pleaded with the insensate
monster to whom he was riveted. The little man
had never been so utterly wretched in all his life.
He was soaked through and through ; his legs
were limp, and the very heart seemed washed out
of him. Half-a-dozen times he had been rolled in
the mud, and half-a-dozen times the rain had
sluiced him clean again. He was a mere atom on
the end of Hooley’s tether, and was whisked
hither and thither with as much thought as a
mastiff gives to the wagging of his tail.
Square in the middle of the road, leaning upon
the wind, with the storm-waters sweeping from
the great sides of him, the constable sang the
woes of Ireland, and his turbulent song chorded
well with the roar of the elements ; but a momen-
tary cessation of the gale left Hooley at the mercy
of the law of gravitation, and he plunged bodily
into the mud, throwing Joshua far and wide.
There was a lull while Joshua lay on his back with
outspread limbs, winded and quite beaten, and the
policeman rested, blinking up through the rain,
deliberately collecting his faculties and weighing
the evidence.
When Lilly cared to recollect and to think of
precautions, he found Hooley crawling on his
knees, and clawing in the mud, and moaning in his
desolation.

and presently found a snug retreat for himself
amongst a wheelwright’s lumber, where there were
dry shavings in a long, narrow case that had once
contained corrugated iron. The policeman made
his bed in the box and lay there indulging in
occasional bursts of song, but for the most of the
time bewailing his misfortunes and the faithless-
ness of woman, leaving his prisoner out in the
mud and the rain. Joshua was careless now if he
lived or died, and half convinced that he was dead
and had gone to hell, condemned to be dragged
through an endless night of rain and dirt by this
gibbering fiend that knew not manners nor ruth.
After a while Hooley’s chummerings had a
soporific effect on Joshua, who would have slept
again, but Hooley tugged him back into a full
consciousness of his misery.
‘‘Are ye there, ye wicked mahn V’ moaned the

The request was made in the voice of a tired

“I do not sing, policeman ; I have no voice. I

Lilly began to sing in a husky treble. For the
life of him he could think of nothing but “Hey

“ ! Tis a dthrunken low song, Charley, mf ye

Kneeling in the outer darkness and dampness,
Joshua crooned his ridiculous song over the moun-
tain of gross flesh spread out upon the shavings,
and all the time Hooley hiccuped weakly of
woman and drink and his besetting sorrow. Com-
ing day softened the darkness into greyness, and
Lilly sang on. The babbling of Hooley grew
fitful and drowsy; sleep was pressing hard upon
him, and Joshua, bowed above him, dreamed of
escaping before the coming of the sun. He recol-
lected another song, and changed his tune to
“ Hush-a-bye, baby, on the tree-top,” and no
mother lisping over her restless babe ever threw
such depths of precious feeling, such soothing
fervor, into the simple old cradle ditty.
The policeman slept, and Lilly sang on till
Hooley’s snore had deepened and broadened into
an emphatic assurance of sound slumber, and then
he allowed his song to die gradually, and com-
menced a cautious, but eager, hunt in the police-
man’s clothes for the key of the handcuffs. In
those few moments Joshua developed all the in-
sidious faculties of a gifted pickpocket; he was as
insinuating as a serpent, as soft as a dove.
He discovered the key at length. In his haste
and anxiety he unlocked the cuff from Hooley’s
wrist, and in a few seconds the sorely-tried and
long-suffering citizen was speeding towards his
home in the dim light of the new-born day.
On reaching the street in which he lived, Lilly
noticed, for the first time, that the handcuffs
dangled from his wrist. He had left the key with
Hooley. Hatless and ghastly pale; tattered,
breathless, muddy, handcuffed, and sopping wet,
Mr. Joshua tottered into the shelter of his own
home and the arms of his waiting spouse. What
happened thereafter is another story.

made for myself.”

Baby.’ In the Bugle-Horn you’ll find ‘ Septem-
ber the Fifth,’— one of them. Six other papers

anything so infamous?”

get a guinea each for them, and for that one about
pur wedding-day the Bugle-1 lorn man gave me

thought of this plan.” # ~

(Sighs deeply.)

April I’ll be safe, and I’ll try to get ten shillings
extra for it.”

of you ? ”

blessing for Edith Robbins ! I’ll have done good
for myself and Edith and Vincent.”
Man (in soliloquy): “Yet I said she had no
sleight-of-head—no ingenuity ! ”
Part 111.
A room in a house in the Goulburn valley.
Shelves full of books, a bench covered with seeds.
Vincent Calvert reading a letter from Edith
Robbins: “And even at the risk of losing
your good opinion I cannot contain my indig-
nation longer. In some third-rate English and.
Australian publications I have of late read
several anonymous sets of verses, the merit of
which was so much above the average that
they immediately attracted attention from quarters
whence attention is most flattering. The
style was so like yours that if I did not know you
never published verse I should have unhesitatingly
fitted them to your name. This day I was in the
editor’s room at the Bugle Horn office (the maga-
zine in which one of the poems appeared) and the
editor, who is a great friend of mine, showed me
a ‘ beautiful sonnet,’ ‘ The April Baby,’ which had
just come in ; also the note accompanying the
poem. The handwriting of the sonnet was yours ,
my friend; the name signed to the note was Leonora
Clayton!” (Drops letter and thinks aloud, his
eyes ablaze with surprise and pleasure.) “Pub-
lished them! The darling girl! Yet sometimes
I thought she did not appreciate or quite under-
stand my rhymes. Published them! Aha-a-a !
you lout that she married, you can’t write verses
to her ! ... It was her delicate way of letting
me know how she appreciated my remembrance-
for, of course, she thought I should see them. As
for you, Miss Robbins, you spiteful creature, I have
written my last letter to you. I’m convinced that
you had something to do with losing my love
Leonora to me. I’m convinced that those were
your unnatural, stilted, brainy letters written from
Hobart. Published them ! Dear, you are an
inspiration—l’ll write you verses every week after
this —nay, I’ll write now. lam inspired.”
A. CONOR-O'BRIEN.
Dear Bulletin. —One day of my life on
the selection —yesterday. The rain held off, as it
has for the past nine months, and I turned out of
bunk at five in the morning, and struggled on
behind the plough, urging the weak horses along,
till noon. Twice a week Igo to the creek for my
load of thick, slimy water, each load costing half
a day. Last night I got back from the Gil Gil at
8 ; watered, and gave the horses a little feed, and
went into the hut to boil the billy. There was
nothing to cook—only a cold mutton-bone and
some bread and jam—so I lay on the bunk
till the water should boil, but the water sang
me to sleep. Didn’t dream—too tired to dream.
When I woke, the billy was a ruin, and it must
have been midnight. Trying to open my eyes, I
staggered outside, and —waded through water and
and mud. Rain at last ! My heart stopped. A
moment later it was explainable. The foal had
tampered with the tap with his teeth or nose, and
the tank was empty. So this morning a weary
man and weary horses went forth again to the
creek.—Yours, Simply Hallelujah.


His Brother Ike—A True Bill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The stuffy, first-class compartment was filled with
sporting men, most of whom were studying their
race-books, or the latest tips in the morning
papers. A hard-faced, sinful-looking crowd, with
0:10 notable exception. He sat opposite me, next
the window—the prettiest young man I ever aw,
with a complexion any girl would have envied,
large frank blue eyes, and a sunny little moustache
carefully trimmed to give his teeth a show when
he smiled. His pearl-grey suit fitted him without
a wrinkle, showing to advantage the 12-stone
igure of an athlete in training. It was the
curious contrast between his boyish face, dainty
clothes and jewellery, and his brawny figure that
attracted my attention.
. ie s P°rtsman who sat on my left conveyed his
impression of the youth in a brief whisper-
“ Mug!” f
ti ln res Ponse to my inquiring look, he added—
Solly has him set. Just you watch how lie’ll
pal up to him for a little business.”
Solly was evidently the hook-nosed young-old
man who had seated himself beside his prey and
was now drawing his attention to something in
his race-book. A significant grin flitted round the
carriage, and several eyes were furtively fixed
upon Solly as he began talking in a low tone to the
youth. The latter, with his chin resting upon his
crutch-stick, listened attentively and with a beam-
ing smile. Suddenly he straightened himself and
stopped Solly’s eloquence with, “ I say, you know,
isn’t your name Abrahams ? ”
Yes, replied Solly, scowling at his fellow-
passengers’ sudden interest in the conversation.
Yes, that’s my name,” repeated Solly with a
hastily-constructed smile.
1 hen I’m very pleased to meet you ” was the
youth s reply as he shook hands with a vigor that
made the scared Hebrew wince. “My name’s
Jay. You may have heard about me from your
brother Ike. No ? Well I met him in Melbourne
two years ago. Guessed you were his brother
soon after you spoke to me.”
Solly’s face wore traces of many mixed emotions.
“Did you do any business with Ike?” he en-
quired, anxiously.

“I had just arrived in Melbourne with a chum
of mine, by the Orient; from London, you know,
where all the new-chums come from. We took
private diggings in East Melbourne, but spent
most of our time at the Bourke-street Coffee
Palace, learning to play pool with the marker.
It’s a nice, quiet billiard-room at the top of the
building, where the new-chums pass their time
when they’re not looking for billets. It was there
I met your brother. He wanted to play pool, too,
but the marker objected. However, he got very
friendly, and one day he told me in strict confi-
dence that he was connected with a racing-stable
in which was a horse that would certainly win the
Melbourne Cup, and that he was to back it for
the stable for all the money lie could get on.
“ Ike took such a fancy to me that he offered to
put £5O of mine on with the stable-money—just as
you are good enough to offer about this certainty
of yours for the Summer Cup. It’s wonderful
how so many stable commissioners take a fancy to
me.”

At this part of the story Solly began to get very
restless. He had made several attempts to turn
the conversation into another channel, but the
company persisted in being deeply interested in
his brother Ike —especially the sportsman on my
left, who frequently appealed to Solly not to in-
terrupt the gentleman.
Mr. Jay seemed to take it for granted that his
reminiscences of Ike were particularly interesting
and amusing to Solly, for he spoke with a smiling,
childish enjoyment that appeared innocent of all
intention to give offence.
Watching his face carefully during the narra-
tive, so far, 1 had come to the conclusion that he
was the most fatuous ass alive. Even when Solly
got up with a smothered oath and looked out
of the. window, the lovely youth didn’t
notice his resentment. He only smiled gently,
.and pushed Solly back into
his seat.

“ Well, I introduced Ike, and Joe occupied a few

Ike appeared in the billiard-room, as usual, to push
the business, Joe allowed himself to be convinced
that it was a good thing, and that the money must
go on at once. But he hadn’t his cheque-book with
him, so he arranged to meet Ike the next morning
at the bank.
“ -That night Ike opened another bottle or two,
and his cigars were above suspicion. The follow-
ing morning he waited outside the bank for three
hours. Joe apologised in the billiard-room that
afternoon for having missed the appointment,
and made another for the next day. He kept that
one, but as his watch was slow he arrived at the
bank too late to draw any money. Then he took
to making appointments further afield (some of
them were as far off as Geelong), and it was lovely
to hear him every evening explaining to Ike how
lie just missed meeting him—and to see Ike trying
to look as if it didn’t matter.

won’t have him doing any of his funny business in

to ask you for money, I’d have chucked him out

ing,’ replied Joe,’ ‘ but don’t you be afraid that

him alone until to-morrow—l shall have finished
with him then, and I’ll tell you all about it.’
“Joe then scribbled a note, which Edward pro-
mised to give Ike that evening, and for the rest of
the day we kept away from his usual beat.

it’s a bit of mozzle that I didn’t.”
On my way to the course I saw Mr. Jay

man who was juggling with them had, in an un-

CRIPPS CLARK.


That Dear Little Fool.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He had had the carriage all to himself since leaving
Wallangarra, and had fallen asleep during the
long run from Dalveen to Warwick. Someone got
in there —a girl. She herself would not have mat-
tered, but her friends did. They were of both
sexes, and their farewells were demonstrative, and
awoke the man abruptly. He was very comfort-
able where he was, and knew that girl - travellers
take a long time to settle, as a rule ; wanting
windows up and shutters down, and everything
reversed from the existing order of things. So he
did not move.
FEMININE AMENITIES.
“ He said I was the only woman he ever loved, but I can hardly
believe that.”
“Oh, it’s quite possible—he is so young that the others were pro-
bably girls. ”
The other girls kissed her ; the young men told
her to take care of herself, and prophesied that
Warwick would go into mourning at her de-
parture. Then the bell rang, and the mail-train
moved off, and the girl hung out of the window
and waved her handkerchief frantically—she was
a pretty girl, dressed simply, correctly, and ex-
pensively. Her fellow-traveller, watching her
through half-closed lids, saw that at a glance.
Presently, she took oS her sailor-hat,
put on a tweed cap, and placed a liberty-
silk cushion behind her head. Against
its deep purple her profile showed like a
cameo, and then the man remembered her.
He would discover himself to her by-
and-bye, but not yet; when he did, her
face would light in the old sweet manner,
just as it used to light when he rode up
to the horse-paddock slip-rails to give her a
bunch of buttercups gathered on the green
river-flats during the mid-day rest.
The sight of her face brought up recol-
lections of the monotonously-happy sta-
tion-life when he was jackarooing on Mis-
tral Creek—six years ago now. There
were several other young fellows gaining
experience also, but though there were
plenty of women of a sort in the neigh-
boring township, there was only one young
girl on Mistral Creek station itself—the
Boss’s 17-year-old daughter: a shy little
thing who dressed dowdily and had not
two words to say for herself. That was
wholly the Boss’s fault, for, though a
Christian in a Christianised country, he
had Oriental ideas on the subject of
women. Certainly he couldn t immure
them behind stone walls, but he discour-
aged the visits of men to his house, and
did his best to keep his daughter from
their society, with the consequence that
:she fell in love with the very first man she
was ever intimate with, that man being
Jack Sandilands. Jack had a reputation
to keep-up as the greatest lady-killer in
the district, and the very difficulties that
surrounded little Hester West made her
worth the winning.
After all, it wasn’t so difficult as he ex-
pected. Hester fell in love with him
almost at first sight, and love sharpened
her wits to design chances of meeting,
She was the frankest, most simple girl he
had ever met ; guiltless of coquetry in any
form, and that in itself wasn’t very amus-
ing. She believed in him so thoroughly
that he sometimes felt a little compunc-
tious, and wondered, when he went to
meet her after dusk in the woolshed,
whether she would risk quite so much for him if
she knew she wasn’t the only woman he was flirt-
ing with, nor yet the only woman he kissed, in the
district. But she did not know it—dear little fool
that she was !
Sometimes he would be sent for weeks to an
out-station; would chaff her on his return
about “the other fellows,” and should have been
proud of her indignant denials and of the scornful
manner in which she evidently received the
advances of others.
But, truth to tell, he was the sort of man who
would rather have enjoyed having a rival, and, liko
all men, inclined to undervalue the rose that fell at
his feet, though it were twice as sweet as its
sister “ hung high on the topmost bough.” Then
the time came when good luck stepped unex-
pectedly into his way, and he left station-life for a
town one.
The parting with the girl took place at the
horse-paddock sliprails. She cried as only seven-
teen-aud-a-half can cry—exhaustively and pro-
fusely; told him that she
loved him, and asked him
to take her away with
him. Which put him in
a very awkward situatio.',
indeed. For one thing, he
had no intention of ‘ ‘ settl-
ing-down ” for years to
come, and, for another,
this dowdily-dressed and
freckle-faced little bush-
girl, with her unformed
prettiness, wasn’t by any
means his ideal wife. But
ho was fond enough of her
to feel a perfect brute
when he explained to her
that flirtation was only a
pastime, and kissing by
no means equivalent to a
proposal. He wasn’t quite
sure, for a moment, that
he did not love her, she
looked so pathetic in the
early sunlight, leaning on
the rails with bowed gol-
den head and shaking
shoulders. Then he glanced
down at the clumsy shoes
and the badly-cut skirt
that displayed glimpses of
a serviceable, but ugly, red
flannel petticoat, and, re-
membering that the future
Mrs. Sandilands must be
stylish above everything,
and a woman to be proud
of, he rode away.
That was six years ago,
and they were together
now under very different
circumstances. The sight
of her face revived the old
half-forgotten tenderness,
and admiration leapt to
life. She was a pretty,
stylishly-dressed girl, and
looked very like his ideal
wife, at any rate. He did
not speculate as to how
she would view him ; she had been too much his
very own in those bygone days for him to have
any doubts on the subject.
He arose slowly and walked the length of the
carriage towards her, a fine specimen of manhood,
and the girl watched him critically. “Do you
remember me, Hester ? ” he said.
The girl smiled politely but vaguely.
“ Have you forgotten Mistral Creek?”

TEMPTATION.
MR. MICHAELSTEIN : “ Almost thou persuadest me to be a Christian! ”
“ Oh, dear no ! ” —smiling charmingly now, and
holding out a slim, gloved hand. “My old home
—and yes, of course, I remember you. Just at
first I did not. It must be quite six years since I
saw you.”
“ Six !” said Jack, “ they seemed like centuries
to me.”
“ Six, is it ? But not six centuries, surely. Do
I look six centuries older ? ”
The arch look that accompanied the question
rather upset Jack’s ideas. He had expected more
shyness, a slight confusion ; and the color had
neither deepened nor paled on the rounded
cheeks; indeed he distinctly saw her give her
dress a quick little pull which displayed to the
best advantage a foam of lacy petticoat and a
slender foot in buckled shoes. Evidently she was
more taken-up with the impression she wished to
make on him than with the impression he was
making on her.
“You have altered so little that I knew you at
once,” he answered, sitting down beside her. “I
wonder if you are one-quarter as glad to see me as
I am to see you ? ” he added softly.
“ Indeed lam quite as glad. What happy days
those were at Mistral Creek —and how kind every-
one was to me ! What a pretty place it was, too.”
“ Very—especially the road through the myalls.
You remember the sliprails across it? I thought
it the loveliest spot on earth once,” he said, mean-
ingly.
“ Dear old road !” cried Miss West, enthusias-
tically. “I always think of it when I see wild
jasmine in bloom : you used to bring me great
bunches of it when you came back from muster-
ing. How glad I used to be when I saw the
chestnut with the white blaze coming over t’ •>
ridge. If I was so pleased to see you then, surely
you do not doubt my gladness now ? ”
Miss West certainly had altered from the shy
little girl who blushed at every word, and pre-
ferred to listen rather than talk.
“I am sure / never brought you jasmine—you
said the scent was too strong. I used to bring you
buttercups—and, besides I did not ride the chest-
nut with the blaze—that was one of Frank King’s
horses. Perhaps he used to bring you flowers,
too ? ” He said the last words jestingly—in those
days she would not have accepted a flower of any
sort from other hands than his.
“ Buttercups, of course—how stupid I am ! Bub
six years is such a long time, you know.”
“ Perhaps,” he said tenderly, “ you will remem-
ber the woolshed better ? ” He knew that a girl
does not easily forget her first love-kiss and its
environment, and he looked straight into her blue
eyes with his handsome bold ones. The lashes
veiled the blue ones, as he thought they would.
She was silent just one moment —then she
laughed a little.
“I ought to be ashamed of it, I suppose,” she
said ; “ but it was such fun, slipping the chaperone
to meet you. Yet I used to be rather frightened
—it was all so dark and creepy in the woolshed ;
they are ghostly places, full of echoes and weird
sounds at night. How relieved I used to feel
when I heard ‘ Annie Laurie, ’ the tune you used
to whistle all the way from the quarters —you re-
member it was our signal.”
“Me ! why I never whistled a tune in my life.
Couldn’t tell ‘ Nellie Bly ’ from ‘ God Save the
Queen ’if you paid me. Rodwell used to whistle
‘ Annie Laurie ’; surely, Hester, you did not
meet him too ! ”
A sudden fierce wave of jealousy swept over
him. Was it possible that he could be jealous of
“ the dear little fool.” For one thing, looking at
this very self-possessed young lady, he wondered if
it was really possible that he had ever, even to
himself, termed her a little fool.
“Oh, but you did whistle; I’m sure jvu did, and
ery nicely, too. Of course, I never met anyone
else but you ; I remember it all perfectly.”
He watched her sweetly-unconscious face in-
tently. “You seem misty on a good many points,
Hester; but if you have forgotten all else you must
surely remember our last parting. I recall it—as
a man recalls the mistake of a lifetime burnt into
his soul with a lifetime of remorse ’’—and he really
believed that he was speaking the exact solemn
truth, and that he always had felt as he felt now.
“ Remember ! ” cried the girl. “ One does not
forget that kind of thing. Why, my hair almost
turned gray on the spot. Goodness gracious,
what an awful rage the Pater was in ! You are
right—it teas a mistake ; we should have remem-
bered it was Saturday evening and he would be
back earlier than usual.” Then she dropped her
eyelids again until the dark lashes rested on her
cheeks. “You were just—oh, I believe you
had just kissed me! —I had my hand on
your horse’s bridle, and I had just picked
up the ash-switch. Oh, I thought father
would kill you—it was a perfect inspiration
of mine to slash your horse and make it bolt.
You ought to be everlastingly grateful to me for
saving your life. I bolted too, directly you were
off—and ran home along the bed of the creek. I
was packed-off to boarding-school after that, and
the row I got into was something dreadful.”
Sandilands sprang to his feet. “By heavens,
Hester, you have given yourself away properly
this trip ! So you were fooling me all the time,
were you, and I was a damned fool to trust to
you as I did ! I have known many women, bub
none who could act like you. No, our parting
was a very different one to that. No doubt its
details were too tame to make any lasting impres-
sion on your mind ; my successors, or predeces-
sors, I don’t know which, seem ”
“ Oh,” interrupted the girl, penitently, “ don’t
be so angry ; you should not make such a fuss
about trifles. Even supposing I did flirt a little-
why, I believe it was you who told me that flirting
was a necessary finish to a girl’s education, and
that kissing meant simply nothing at all, and
should never be taken seriously. Now, didn’t you
say so ? No, I believe it was Mr. Jones.”
As she spoke the train stopped suddenly at a
station, and Sandilands threw open the door with-
out so much as saying good-bye to his late travel-
ling-companion, and made for the smoker at the
whol e °. u H°ok was upset, his theories
shattered, his opinion of himself lowered in every
way. And this was the girl who had parted from
rum m an agony of tears ! —Rodwell, Jones, and
King—how they must have laughed in their
sleeves at him ! There is no pain equal to that of
wounded vanity ; no passion that thrives on such
meagre food as jealousy. The girl watched him
crossing the platform until he disappeared into
the smoking-carriage; then she put her foot up on
the seat and re-arranged the cushion behind her
curly head. “ Now, I wonder,” she said softly
to herself, “ which hurts the most —wounded
vanity or a broken heart ? ”

ALICE MONTGOMERY.


REVENGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It chanced—no matter how or why—
Some things one need not tell—
That, in a spell of leisure, I
Was strolling round through Hell.
And there I saw a cage full tall
That was, by some strange whim,
Shaped like an editorial
Waste-paper basket grim.
And in that cage, like kerosene,
There burned in torture sore
The soul of one who once had been
A Sydney editor.
And like a demon in D-T’s,
There danced around the cage
A Shape that laughed in agonies
Of diabolic rage.
As round the cage the coal it piled,
I saw the Shape perspire
Till it was in a lather wild
Of soot, and sweat, and fire.
This sight my spirit so unnerved
That for a space I leaned
On Satan’s shoulder, and observed—
“ Who is this fearsome fiend °! ’’
Then Satan said, with humor dire,
“I thought that you would know.
A writer ’tis who feeds the fire
That roasts his ancient foe.
“He might have been in Heav’n—ah me
Amongst the cherubim;
But he preferred down here to be,
For this is Heav’n to him 1 ”
So to that editor you see
Of all his glory stripped,
He reads—while piling coals so free —
He reads through all eternity
Rejected manuscript,
C.R.

Island-trading schooner Wasp fetched the en-
trance to the lagoon early in the forenoon. She
hove-to, and showed a signal for a pilot. Then
Capt. Sanders went below At length a boat
came across the lagoon under a press of sail. She
luffed-up smartly alongside, and a man rose in the
stern-sheets a little, skinny fellow, with a
humped back, a beardless, wizened face, and a
tousle of red hair under a greasy cloth cap.
“ BULLETIN ” ADVT.
No. III.
“ Everybody is astonished at your ‘Cow-
Soap.' keeps the milk wonderfully
clean.” (Signed) JAMES CORNSTALK.
As he swung over the side the captain stepped
on deck, stopped short, and stared with the face
of an unbeliever who suddenly sees an indisput-
able ghost. The newcomer collapsed against the
rail and shook as if he would have a fit.
“ Spinnox ! ” the captain gasped—reeled, and
tumbled down the companion-ladder. The hunch-
back lifted a grinning face, and shook harder:
“ Captain Sanders! ”
When he went below a minute later he found
the captain seated at the cabin table. He leaned
his crooked shoulders against the bulkhead, and
grinned like a cat.
“ Quite a happy and unexpected meeting, isn’t
it, cap. ?” he said. “ You signal for a pilot, and
you find a friend long lost to sight but to memory
dear, as the song says. What a queer world we
live in, after all! Well, well ! ”
The captain silently stared at him.
“ You didn’t expect to see me, cap. ?”
The captain licked his dry lips. “No ! ”
“Of course. Thought I was dead. Let’s see—
two years ago, wasn’t it ? And all that time I’ve
been hungering for a sight of my old friend’s
face.”
His old friend’s face was twitching in a curious
fashion.
“ Who’s your mate now, cap. ?”
“ Got none.”
“No?”
The captain made invisible diagrams on the
table with a shaking forefinger.
“ What happened ? ”
“Fell overboard.”
“ Whew ! Dark night ? ”
The captain made diagrams.
“ Boat dropped—couldn’t find him ? ”
The captain made diagrams.
“Most likely hit his head against something,
and sank like lead—eh ? Just a cry and a splash,
and that was all—eh ? ”
“ I don’t know anything about it.”
Spinnox slid to the floor, and again his body
shook in that strange way—shook to an inward,
soundless laughter that was likely
to choke the man.
, “ Quite a trick of your mates —this
getting overboard. One of ’em did
the same two years ago—that was me.
Just hit my head, and went under.”
The captain was breathing hard, like
a man who had been running.
“ Hit my head against a belaying-pin
—which had somehow got out of its place,
and was waving about at the end of a
man’s arm. I went under, came up, and
managed to keep myself afloat for a couple of
hours. Then I was picked-up.”.
The captain breathed harder.
“See, cap. !” The hunchback showed a livid
scar through his tangled mane. His eyes glinted,
but his voice never rose. “See —where I hit the
pin,”
“Blast you !” The captain leapt up, foaming.
“Stow that cursed rot, will you? Next time,
you’ll hit the pin harder.”
Spinnox jumped to his feet, pointed a thin fore-
finger, and laughed.
“ Sit down, cap.,” he said. “ Let’s talk fair.”
And, queerly enough, the captain sat down, and
his rage oozed away in little mutterings of oaths
and threats. It was the mastiff cowed by the
terrier. But the dogs had met before.
“See, cap., I’m tired of this shore business. I
want to feel the sea under me again. What more
natural than that I should take my old berth—
mate of the Wasp, eh ? ”
The captain looked up once with wide eyes;
then he dropped them and sat silent while he
made a more elaborate diagram on the table.
“ Want to ship with me again—eh ?” he said at
last.
“ That’s it. Let bygones be bygones, and the
rest of it.”
“I’ll take you.”
* • * •
First day out from the island came a mystery
aboard the Wasp. A revolver-shot was heard in
the captain’s cabin. Mate Spinnox, who went to
investigate, was told with many emphatic compli-
ments through the closed door to mind his own
business. After that at odd times when the cap-
tain was below a regular popping of shots kept all
hands marvelling.
Taylor, the white-headed oracle of the fore-
castle, shook his head solemnly, but was other-
wise dumb. Half-wit Norris, the cook, with the
temerity of unreason, made a long shot. “He’s
going mad,” he said —a view that was literally
hammered upon the crew in the days following,
what time the skipper was on deck, for Captain
Sanders was a “hazer.” But Spinnox sought for
the method in this madness. Speculation was
resultless ; the thing demanded action. Spinnox
burgled the captain’s cabin during its owner’s
absence on deck. He found screwed to the bulk-
head a lead-splashed iron plate, bearing in white
paint the rude drawing of a man’s head.
“ Humph !” thought the mate, “Pistol-prac-
tice. Must have a reg’lar arsenal aboard.
Smith-and-Wesson never made those splashes.
Something lighter—something in the half-gun,
half-toy style. Practice good, too —centres, most
of ’em. But why ?—why ? ”
The remainder of that day he pondered in an
idle, speculative kind of way. By the next, the
thing had become a personal matter : he found
himself drawing mental pictures of a man’s head
upon the sails and twisting the gear into sundry
notes of interrogation.
“ Now ivhat’s the old man up to ? ” The hunch-
back paced the deck, still in a fog. Suddenly he
stopped short, cocked his wizened face, and
winked solemnly at a star that showed prominently
above the foremast. “Why, it’s me! The old
man’s plumping lead into my figurehead. Well,
that is a queer amusement. Safer than belaying-
pin's, though, for me —much safer.”
He stood for a full minute, shaking his soundless
laughter into the teeth of the warm night-wind.
Then he winked cheerfully at his stellar confidant,
and paced again like a man who has found a
pleasant solution to a troublesome problem.
* * * *
The moon turned a wan half-face from the upper
west. The wind was steady and fair. The
Wasp hummed through the moonlit sea. Spin-
nox had the deck. Half-wit Norris, squatting
against the weather side of the foremast, scraped
solace for a wounded jaw—the result of a blow
from the captain’s fist—and lugubrious discord
from a cheap violin, droning the beats slowly:
“One—two —three —four.” A couple of hands,
stretched on the forecastle, were negotiating a
wager as to how long the captain would tolerate
the half-wit’s noise.
Spinnox trod the deck with the noiseless steps
of a cat. Unconsciously, he marched to the
fiddler’s measure: “One —two —three—four.” He
fell into a pleasant reverie, and had no eye for
anything.
“One —two ...”
The sudden break in the monotone spun him
round like a vane in a veering wind, and—he met
the captain’s eye sighting him above a long-bar-
relled revolver.
Astonishment held everybody moveless. The
mate just stood, head thrust forward, staring and
making little clicks like a man trying to swallow
with a dry throat. Half-wit Norris turned
shocked face on the pair, his bent arm up, as it
paralysed in mid-air. The men on the forecas
crouched low, and stared, scarcely breatmng. ±ne
helmsman gripped the spokes with steel fingers.
Thus, for a full minute—and the expected shot
did not come. • ,
Suddenly, hunchback Spinnox pattered across
the deck like a monkey, rapidly shaking himself
into a frenzy. _ . ,
His movement lifted the spell. N orris pressed
a swollen jowl to the violin, and mechanically
took up his interrupted counting in a
hysterical way—“ three —four.” The men forward
sat up, shook cold sweat from their forelocks, and
And, all the time, there was the captain grinning
along the Smith-and-Wesson barrel. It was a
queer thing ! Laugh at it; call it momentary
paralysis, failure of volition —anything. The cap-
tain had fired so often in imagination at Spinnox
that he had lost the power to do it in reality. _
The mate was leaning against the side, shiver-
ing, and laughing idiotically. Suddenly lie
wrenched a belaying-pin from the rail, and stepped
forward. His a£m made a swift movement. The
pin whirled like a metal wheel through the dim
moonrays—straight to its mark. The captain just
crouched, turned half round, and crumpled to the
deck. The falling revolver sang a bullet into the
foremast above the fiddler’s head.
“Resignation of Captain Sanders ! ” screamed
the hunchback.
He was a veritable madman. He scampered
about the deck, cursing horribly. Norris cried
aloud at his approach, lifted the face of a
frightened child, and rolled to his back, whimper-
ing. The men, with the unanimity that terror
brings, dived for the forecastle scuttle. Aft, the
helmsman thrust the inscrutable face of a sphinx
across the wheel.
Spinnox saw none of these things. He sprang
into the rigging and shinned up. He swiftly rove
a rope and slid down a backstay. He slipped a
bight of the rope around the supine captain’s
neck, and, in an instant, had him swinging aloft
streaming out to leeward, like a ghastly pennant,
with the list of the schooner.
“ Resignation gazetted 1 ”
Hands on his hips, he stood watching, in a sort
of drunken ecstasy, the swaying of the suspended
figure. Then he sprang again into the rigging.
He parted the rope witli a swift knife-stroke.
“ Signed—Spinnox /”
The helmsman heard a splash in the sea.
* ¥ * *
Now, log-books are popularly supposed to be
strictly accurate chronicles, but a queer discrepancy
appeared in the two records of this one event,
For, when the Wasp reached Sydney, the log
simply ran, in the neat hand of Mate Spinnox:
“ Dec. —, Captain Sanders washed overboard and
drowned. Lat. —, long. —.”
P. T. FREEMAN.
“ Gentlemen,” said the auctioneer, when
presiding at a sale of choice station-lots, “the
property I am about to submit to your notice to-
day speaks for itself. I could expatiate upon it
for hours were I gifted with the power of elo-
quence, but as it is my misfortune to be no orator
I can only call your attention to tbe facts that if
you should call at any time you think fit you will
find the manager drunk, the superintendent in-
toxicated, the overseer just suffering a recovery,
the book-keeper in d.t.’s, and the station hands
more or less in a state of booze —and yet, gentle-
men, so magnificent is the soil and water-supply
that the station pays handsomely. Now then,
favor me with a bid for this exceptional lot of real
estate.”

FACES.


THAT OLD JOKE.
HEN this old Joke was new they clinched
With saurians in the slime :
They rode to fight on griffins grey
In warrior garb of grime.
Their heads were all a-bulge behind,
Their foreheads scarce in view
They’d just commenced to lose their tails
When this old Joke was new.
And then it died, this ancient Joke,
And slept for many a year,
Till Neolithic jesters came
And re-begot the jeer.
And, as they passed the humor-round,
Loud laughed the savage crew,
And thanked the gods who gave them breath
When such a Joke was new.
It died again—and rose again,
A Ghost of Laughing-Gas ;
It ran its race through days of Bronze
To this dear Age of Brass.
It’ s set a million eyes a-blink,
A million mouths askew—
It blooms a pure perennial jibe
This Joke antique, but new.
And all the wits of all the world
Are freshly travail-torn
When, year bv year - in labor dear,
This babe of Joy is born.
And each one swears the others lie
In claiming credit due
(He felt the pangs on Thursday last
He knows the Joke is new),
And ages wax and ages wane,
The stars grow dim and cold,
The prophets sleep in slumber deep,
The hero’s heart is mould ;
Pur skies are pale, our beer is stale,
Our outlook’s rather blue,
Unto all things old is a death-knell tolled
But the Joke—is always knew.
T. LUFTIG.


We Dreamed.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And we, we dreamed of love and stars, and waters
deep,
Of shining gleam-kiss’d waves and tears,
Of fallen worlds, torn from a wilderness of lights—>
We dreamed of whips, of staves and spears,
The broken garment of the night, the solemn rise
Of sun and sky, the bubbling note
Of birds new-waked, the whistling of the urchin
wind,
The white sail of a fisher-boat.
The long red line of dead and squatting hills of
.sand,
The smell of salt combined with bloom,
The sweetest minglement of needle-prick and kiss
Of fire resin-fed and gloom.
We sqw the broad-beaked, heavy-headed water-
bird
Drop like a shining stone from sight,
Or as a star, weak from long holding at the dome,
Lets loose and’s shotten thro’ the night.
We dreamed of torches, and of tigers’ yellow eyes,
Of shredded scarlet-threaded smoke,
Of burnt brown lazy arms and bits of unwashed
gold,
Of bird-note and of bull frog croak.
And snow-pale fleets of water-lilies and green
leaves,
Of bursting nuts and falling rain,
Of God, of single tears, of kneeling men, and much
That coupled heart with muscle-pain.
We dreamed of kings who fought with kings, like
dogs for bones,
Their dust b’own up against the moon,
All hair and crowns, and spotted with hot quarrel
blood,
Their stockings sinking to their shoon.
Of brazen bishops, kissing girls and dancing jigs,
Or eating cheese inside the church ;
Of lawyers talking medicine and swapping wigs,
Of schoolboys knowledgeless of birch.
Of muffled boat-oars and of flat-boned prancing
mares,
Of watching men ’neath stretching trees;
Of littlelakes, looking’s if God had spat them there,
Of ruined roses in a breeze ;
Of heavy soulless smiles reflected in a glass,
Of cups of poisoned amber wine ;
Of burnt-out flambeaux, cracking in a deathly dark
And fusing out a scent of pine.
Of woman-arms outspread for love, of gleaming
teeth,
Of pillowed folds of thick-rolled hair,
Of panting tiny breasts, and whispered naughtiness
Of Cupid in a gold-got lair ;
Of one who trode a midnight road with fright-
tight fist,
Of drunken bending shrubs, of hills
Low-browed and beetling, shelterless, begowned
with mist
That catches at the throat and chills.
Of fire and cake and ale, of blue tobacco-smoke,
Of rousing ten-voiced chorus songs ;
Of the wee chuckling of the clock before we woke
And broke the withey slumber thongs.

The Much-Desired Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Advertising for a wife is generally considered a
rather humorous affair, and when the subject is
mentioned in that station of life where people
usually have enough to eat, plenty to wear, and
sleep between fresh linen sheets, it is apt to raise
a smile. In reality, advertising for a lady corres-
pondent with a view to matrimony is a very
pathetic affair, as I recently found out. I was
seized with a desire to discover what manner of
people they are who go to the large daily paper
for a life-partner, and accordingly inserted in the
greatest daily of them all the following advt. at
the cost of Is. for each insertion :
j\/rATRIMONY. Man about 30, fair position, wishes
correspond refined, educated young woman,
view to above.
It wasn’t much of a bait. Variations of it were
inserted three times, and—is it a credit to the
advertising worth of that Sydney paper or a slur
on the economic credit of our alleged civilisation —
the applicants for that man and his fair position,
particulars unknown, were 91. Of the 91, less than
six, judging by their letters, were “ fast ” women.
Most of the 91 letters were ill-written and had
mistakes in spelling and grammar all over them.
Some were very guarded; some were painfully
frank and gave personal particulars as to height,
complexion, disposition, inclinations, and financial
status. One lady said she hadn’t a blemish on her
whole body barring two moles, one on her right
shoulder and the other just above her knee. She
was evidently a prize, and knew her own matri-
monial value. Some had a little money saved ;
some had a home but no regular income ; some had
their own furniture, but found it hard to pay the
rent and cost of living. Some had only their looks
and nice ways. A very few wrote merely that
they had observed the advertisement and would
be pleased to meet the gentleman whenever it was
convenient for his lordship. But these casual-

THE GIRL : “ But you’re NOT going to take me into dinner. Mother insists on your taking in
Mrs. Tonwait.”
THE JORNRY: “ What, Mrs. Tonwait going to dine! How hideously unnecessary !”
minded ones were few. The majority were in
deadly earnest.
The first lady who was to be honored with an
appointment made her own arrangements, and
named a busy street right in front of a public
office. She was to carry a red flower, while the
writer was to display a white tie and wear his hat
on one side. Time, 8 o’clock.
Ten minutes before the time I found a small,
dumpy woman in a very stained dress, glancing
up and down the street, while every now and
again she waved a red paper flower up and down in
a slow, deliberate, public sort of way. She couldn’t
wave it over her head, for in one hand she carried
a bag stuffed full of vegetables and paper pack-
ages. Under her other arm was a paper parcel,
from one end of which protruded a most aggres-
sive mutton-shank. The girl was not pretty, not
even attractive. Her eyes were small and peery,
and were almost submerged
in the rolls of fat which
formed her face.
Fortunately she turned
out to be only the servant;
her mistress had sent her
to watch and bring word
if the advertiser was a de-
cent-looking individual or a
larrikin. Apparently the
girl was satisfied, and in a
few minutes the mistress
appeared, in a black silk
blouse and a tailor-made
skirt and jacket, with her
head adorned by one of the
latest things in toques.
There was an odour of
opopanax, a gleam of brace-
lets and jewellery, and a
general aroma of well-to-do
moral laxity about this
charming dame. She told
her name and address (both
of which were false), and
proposed a trip to Manly,
so that we could talk. Was
much impressed by my
courage in addressing her
maid. Was I really look-
ing for a wife, or only for
a romance. There wasn’t
much romance in mar-
riage, for a man, but for a
woman there was whole
stacks. She had her own home, plenty to
live on ; but, after all, life to a woman was very
dull without a husband to look after her. Why
(growing earnest and confidential) she would
sooner have a husband who beat her occasionally
than none at all. She knew lots of men, but they
were too staid and respectable—she admired a
man who was a bit of a devil. What was the
advertiser’s screw? Only £3 per week—well, it
wasn’t much, but she had about as much and a
free house, and with the two combined they ought
to have good sport. Still, she didn’t fancy me
much. I had a limp ; she didn’t like limps. Also,
she rather admired curly hair, whereas mine—she
told me frankly—was as straight as a rat’s tail.
But, no doubt, I had courage. The man who
could keep an appointment with a woman loaded
with vegetables and meat and waving a red
paper flower up and down in George-street was a
hero, and she just adored courage. She would
like to correspond for a while and see if she grew
to like me a bit more.
She prattled on like that for three hours. Then
I got her home, and found out that the first ad-
dress was a lie. She was the only person I met
out of the crowd, bar one other, whose story was
not full of pathos.
The second lady was shy. It was a long time
before she could bring herself to meet a man she
didn’t know. She was a sad little thing, with big,
grey eyes and white, shell-like face. She was a
widow, toiling at service to rear and educate a
little son, who was getting on for seven. She
would be a good wife. She could cook nice things,
and was skilled in the making of conserves and
condiments, and she would be so grateful to any
gentleman who would be a father to her little boy
that she was certain she should love him passion-
ately in a very little while. Her little boy was
such a bright little fellow. He was living in
Bourke-street—would I come round and see the
little chap ? She wished him to have a good edu-
cation, and grow up a good and, perhaps, a great
man. And she looked up through the cold moon-
light with great, grey, wistful eyes—and I wished
that the earth would open and swallow up all cads
and fools who try to look into women’s psycho-
logical depths. It was terrible—l had to stop her
somehow. I took her hand, and told her shame-
facedly that I was already married, and was only
a journalist looking for information —something to
write about; and asked for her forgiveness. She
looked at me through a little mist, and said softly,
“ And I took you for quite a gentleman ! ”
The next eleven were just like her; poor
crushed women, left unprovided for by dead hus-
bands. Some were left by husbands who had
gone to seek fortunes in Westralia, and had only
written once or twice, and never come back —the
nan who goes West, it
would seem, seldom does
come back, unless he comes
crushed and broken. Some
had simply fallen victims to
other piratical women, who
had come along and lured
their husbands from them
by the lust of the eye. But
all all, were
pathetic. They were hungry
for a better time, and they
had only their bodies and re-
spectability to barter for
that better time, and the
joys and comforts of a home,
and they told of their quali-
fications and their abilities,
and when their arms were
plump they tried to indicate
the fact, nicely and- uncon-
sciously, in a heart-breaking
way.
Then there Was a girl,
who admitted quite frankly
that she had fallen was
goingto be honest, and would
make no man marry her
by telling him lies. She
spoke half-defiantly, and un-
der a strong sense of- injury,
and with an evi lent desire
to avoid even the appear-
ance of deceit. Her baby
was born in the Benevo-
lent Asylum. She wrote to
its father before she went
there, and found that he
had gone God-knows-where.
So when she L.t the Asylum she had to
take a place where they would let her
have her baby with her. No, she wouldn’t
part with it till it was ole 1 enough to
tell her when it was ill-treated. She wasn’t
going to let any murdedng baby - farmer
grab her money and kill her child. She got no
wages, on account of having the child with her.
She just earned shelter and food for both of them.
Yes, she was sick of it, but when she looked at
the baby it pulled her together again. How did
she dress? Well, she didn’t dress properly. The
woman who sweated her gave her cast-offs, and
asked the women-lodgers to do the same; and
when they did give them they mostly bemoaned
the loss of the money they would have got for
“them things, quite good and respectable,” in
Bathurst-street. Of course, the woman had to
keep her decently covered for her own credit’s
sake. She couldn’t have the housework done by
a naked girl, so she clothed her in a sort of way,
and growled about it. She had borrowed that
cape and skirt from a lady-lodger, to whom she
had explained that she had answered an advt.,

a gentleman he would find her, she hoped, a
pleasant and interesting companion. I tried to
create no necessity for the use of the dreadful-
looking shooter, and after some interesting con-
versation, took her home with great respect, and
held my hat in my hand as I said good-night.
HERMES.


King Tawhaio's Ngerengere.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“That business of Karepa’s in the river, said
Henderson, ‘ ‘ reminds me of Tawhiao’s ngerengere.
Egad, it was a circus ! ”
“ Hand the billy over here.”
“He was the Maori king, you know—some
pumpkins, I can tell you—and went to England
to see Queen Wikitoria about some land trouble.
“There was the king, a chief called I atara,
another, Te Wheoro. who could speak a little Eng-
lish ; I forget the third; but anyway the king had
a bad time. „ , ,
“ Kimberley was Colonial Secretary. He told
the king to go back and put his trouble before the
Government here ; it had nothing to do with
Downing-street—the king couldn’t see Wikitoria
on any account, not if he presented fifty green-
stone ‘ meres ’ and a hundred kiwi mats.
“ Tawhiao couldn’t understand. He knew that
Wikitoria was a big rangatira, but so was he. He
wasn’t eaten up with pride, but he thought they
might have a korero together. But it was no go.
The British Government is dunder-headed. Are
you listening ? ”
“Yes. It’s first-rate. But when are we com-
ing to the yarn ? ”
“.Yarn be bio wed !—this is the yam. Pass me
some bread—no, not with your fingers. Here,
take my sheath-knife.”
“ There you are. Fire away.”
“ The king put it all down to occult influences
which were working against him; he became
pouri ; he thought he had ngerengere (leprosy).”
“ Hold on,” said Karepa. “ I makee t’e talk. I
know when Tawhiao went to see Wikitoria. You
think it pecause his mana no good. That not it.
The king break his tapu aboard a ship, and then
ewerryt’ing go wrong wit’ him. Never mind,
Governor, Premier, Secretary. They think they
keep Tawhiao off from Wikitoria, but all t’e
Governor and Premier in Noo Seelan’ and
Ingarangi not stop the king, if his mayia all right.”
“ But how was it broken ? ”
“ I make-a-talk,” said Karepa., with half a
pigeon in one hand and his pannikin in the other.
“On board a ship were all sort plenty waiter-men
—taurekareka ewerryone—to bring food for the
king and t’e chiefs. These men not see the mana
belonga Tawhiao ; they not see the proper tikanya
to use wit’ so bik man ; they not see how werry
tapu he was. They pring his kai (food) allasame
as to ewerrypody on board a-that ship.
“ One stooard was werry bad man, worse of all.
He stand pehind when t’e king eat. He play t’e
tewil. But worse of all, he carry the kai pehind
the king, and put it ofer his shoulder. He touch
Tawhiao ; the ship gif a bik roll—he spill the lcai
ofer the king. That make Tawhiao feel bad—he
know all about it—an’ when he get to Ingarangi,
he no got a bit of tapu left. Ewerryt’ing go
wrong wit’ him.”
“ Have it how you like—this is my yarn, said
Henderson. “ The old boy came down to me in
his distress. I was living in a little village, 20
miles out of London. He said he was ngerengere.
But I couldn’t see anything wrong with him.
When I told him so, he pointed to his eyebrows,
and said they had come out. That was a sign the,
disease was working. ”
“I don’know,” said Karepa. “I ink he tell
you somethin’ make-a-talk.”
“Anyhow, that’s what he said.”

“Our housemaid called herself 'Phemia; the

“I explained to them the nature of our expected
visitors. ‘ln the country where I come from,’ I

They have been called the noblest savages in the

you needn’t be frightened—these that are coming
here are the king and three of the biggest chiefs.
Of course, you’ll treat them just as if they were
white people, like vou and me. ’
“The girls left the room as if they’d been sen-

kitchen.” .
“ But how about the wisdom of taking a pros-
pective leper into your family ? ” .
“ I never mentioned that. I didn’t know it till
he arrived, and then I thought the old boy s super-

“I met them at the railway-station. Iliere
was no sensation. First came the king and I,
then two of the chiefs, and lastly the third chief
and the interpreter who had piloted them. down.
They were dressed au pakeha, in the height of

“When we turned into the High-street, we
were joined by three small school-children, two

Tracey, the grocer, who took an interest m
missions to the heathen, Miss De Vine, daughter

and half-a-dozen dogs. .
“ The king stopped at Ibbotson s clothing-shop.
He wanted to buy something, he said. Like a
well-bred Maori, he took no notice of the stir he
was causing, but walked straight into the shop
and took off his coat.
“He wanted a woollen vest. But he was hard
to please, and the whole shop had to be turned
upside-down before he was suited. He chose a
thick, white jersey, tried it on over his waistcoat,
and paid down his money like a white man.
“Now, the old chief who had come along
behind was a perfect devil. The humor of the
situation seemed to tickle him, and while we were
in the shop he ‘ pulcana’d ’ at the crowd outside.
You know what that is. It seemed pretty funny
to him and the interpreter, but one young girl had
hysterics, and Miss De Vine’s horse shied.
Karepa, who was following the yarn, recognised
the word pukana, and gave a performance by way
of illustration. He rolled his eyes till only the
whites of them showed ; he lolled his tongue with
the air of a semi-idiot; he danced a few unmean-
ing steps; then he rushed upon us with an un-
earthly howl, and snatched at us with his long
bony hands. .
“Yes,” said Henderson, “that was something
like it, but he had more tattoo than you. He
ever such a demon.”
Karepa sat down, and re-lit his pipe.
“That frighten t’e pakeha, I t’ink.”
“It made the village constable want to arrest

The man doesn’t mean anything. Thatsafo o

Karepa will bear me out. ‘ I’ll be responsible for

half-a-crown into Coster’s hand. ‘Be
lenient,’ I said; ‘be lenient.’
“ The effect was magical.
