“ The Maoris went by the road, but we cut
across the fields and got to the ford first. We
crossed by a footbridge, and hid behind some
bushes on the further side. Five minutes later,
the Maoris came down to the river and did a lot
of subdued talking on the other side. Then the
three chiefs retired behind a hedge, and the king
was left alone.
“ The old boy put his tall hat on the grass, took
off his clothes, and got into the water.
“There was a willow growing on our side
of the river, and he waded across to this and
pulled off two or three wands, and stuck them
into the bank. Then he got back into the river,
and began his karakia.
“It was the weirdest sort of picnic, with
Coster’s teeth chaptering with fright, and the
king saying his mournful prayers in the water,
and the three chiefs ready to stoush us if they
knew we were there. ,
“But the first karakia was got through safely,
and the king splashed the water, as Karepa did.
Then he began to karakia again. Altogether, he
dismissed the evil spirits to about seven places,
and then he got right down into the water.
“ After that he recrossed to the other bank, and
put on the new jersey and the rest of his clothes.
The chiefs came down to him, and without a word
they all walked off.
“ ‘Come Coster,’ I said, ‘ that finishes the pro-
gramme. I must get back in time to let them in.’
“‘And this,’ said Coster, recovered from his
fright now that the Maoris had gone, ‘is the
mumbo-jumbo nonsense that is to keep a man out
of his bed ar’ter midnight—an’ in a Christian
country, too. An’ a gen’leman like you counten-
ances such goings on. I'm disapp’inted.’
“And no doubt he was. I left him without
giving him a further tip—that was what he was
looking out for—and got back in time to open the
front door for the king, just as if nothing had
happened. The old boy nearly shook my hand off.
“I said, ‘You’ve had a good time?’ but he
wouldn’t speak. I offered him some supper, but
he shook his head. I took him up to his bedroom,
but he wouldn’t sleep in anything half so comfort-
able. . , . ...
“ He went down into the smoking-room with
never a word, pushed the table into a corner, and
motioned me to go away. Evidently, if he spoke
he would break the spell.
“ Next morning, Euphemia got up at half-past
six, as usual. One of the first things she did was

“Hearing the noise, I hurried downstairs,• and

“ When I went to the king he was sitting on

“ But the servants took it in bad part. They
voted the whole business disreputable. ‘ We’re
accustomed to live respectable,’ said the cook,
‘and not along of black persons that walk about
naked, and sleep naked under the tables. You
may call -such folks kings, you may call ’em the
Emperor of Chiny, the Sultan of Turkey, or what
you likes— we call ’em ’eathen savages / ’
“Euphemia was indignation personified. ‘I
never come here to be laid hands on,’ she said.
‘ When I do forget myself I ’ope it won’t be with
such as ’ im. I should ’ope not. I didn’t take your
money to be drove into hysterics by savage < with
carved faces. You may be accustomed to such —
you come from where such goings-on is common.
I give a month’s warning.
“It was the end of our domestic comfort.
Never a girl would come' near us ; we lost caste in
the village, and a sort of social boycott set in.
But we had the consolation of knowing that the
king’s ngerengere was quite cured. I, personally,
had the satisfaction of having witnessed the only
occult Maori rite performed in England.”
Karepa was awake again, and sat, smoking.
“ All that tapu too much bother,” he said. “ I
take t’e tapu off all my picaninny. 1 take it off
all my taonga, cup, plate, knife, mat, ewerryt’ing.
I make it allasame as the pakeha. Too much t’e
tapu. Too much t’e karakia. The man wit’
t'e makutu no frighten me now—l aliassame as t’e
pakeha. ”
Henderson took out his flask, and offered me a
“ nip.”
“ Thanks,” I said, “but if we are going'to camp
here for the night, isn’t it time we pitched the
tent ? You, Karepa, being a man with no tapu
and no mana to speak of, may take the tomahawk
and cut some tent-poles.”
The Maori laughed.
“That werry good way to make the Maori
work,” he said, “but I t’e pakeha now—l want
t’e utu, t’e pay. You gif me t’e wai piro too, and
I cut t’e tent-pole.”
But, without waiting to get his “nip,” he dis-
appeared, smiling, into the bush.

ALFRED A. GRACE.


Grundy’s Ghost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE AUSTRALIAN'S LOVE OF HORSEFLESH.
LITTLE BILLY'S UNCLE: “ What would you like for a Christmas present, Billy?"
BILLY : “ I’d just like to ride that mare, uncle.” [And he did.]
Here is not the story of the ghost of
Private “ Grundy ” M’Carthy, H.M’s 22nd.
Hussars—but of the ghost that afflicted him. The
lessee of that malignant spook was Private
“ Tokey ” Williams, borne, when in the flesh, or
the muster-roll of the same corps. The 22nd.
were of the “Dumpy Pice,” the small, savage,
soldier-wasps of the “Honorable” East India
Company’s service. When a Briton in moleskins
violently filches shillings, Society scourges him
with many stripes. When a syndicate of shop •
keepers robs and murders with both hands,
territories and lacs of gold mohurs being the loot,
it is “Honorable.” Great was the raj of “John
Kompani ” !
After the Indian Mutiny, Victoria took over,
with other of the Company’s property, the
Company’s white troops, though these had no
covenant with her. Any refusing Imperial livery
were branded “ rebels ” and driven aboard floating .
cattle-pens for England, many being done to
death at sea in foetid ’tween-decks by putrid
rations, bilge-water, and fever. Thus ever British
Bumbledom guerdons the rank-and-file whose
blood cements the Empire’s glory.
* * * «
Grundy and Tokey were not Company’s soldiers.
They enlisted together, years before either Mutiny
or “ Tiger Act,” in H.M. 40th Light Dragoons,
long antecedent to the advent as Secretary of
State for War of the doctrinaire who invented
“limited engagement” and the “territorial”
aberration—in the days when the British Army
stocked veterans; when to the soldier his regiment
was home, his comrades brothers, his colonel God—
a grim deity to be followed, nathless, to nethermost
hell.
From the first day of their service, Grundy—
Irish, raw, and red, and Tokey—Welsh medical-
student of education, sagged from virtue—were
sworn comrades. Tradition associated them in
malign enterprise before enlisting. They went to
India together; together rode, blood to their
stirrup-irons, the Mutiny, and, the 40th ordered
home, volunteered to the Dumpies and for further
Indian service, and were later both invalided and
joined the depot troop, Canterbury (England).
OTHER TIMES, OTHER MANNERS.
BOLDEST BATHER : “Excuse me, ladies; but will you kindly
retire a little farther from our clothes? We have been
waiting for an hour for you to go away and let us dress.”
LADIES (all at once and together): “Oh, don’t mind i;s l We’re

Ihere Tokey’s ghost became objectionable. Prior
transactions in India however, belong first to this
record.
* * * *
After the amalgamation, the 22nd, quartered at
Allahabad, lost its regimental treasure-chest
containing Its.3ooo—then worth all £3OO. Thi
chest was inside a padlocked tumbril, the tumbril
outside the main-guard. The chest, safe at sun-
down, had vanished when the paymaster unlocked
the tumbril next morning. The entire guard were
whipped on the shelf.” Yet the mystery held,
enough the adjutant, young and melodramatic,
tor weeks crawled cantonments at night, like the
serpent after the fall, on his belly, a loaded revol-
ver m his sword-belt. Bandicoots and jackals he
encountered, and nearly sampled the venom of
variegated snakes and scorpions, but found never
thief nor rupee. The Provost-Sergeant’s men were
as unsuccessful, though they long shadowed every
Dumpy on orderly-room black-list. Tokey and
Grundy, both on the Provost’s staff, much distin-
guished themselves by zeal and ingenious theories.
Therefore, when, months after, they put in for
five weeks’ leave of absence “ to visit Benares and
adjacent parts,” the grateful adjutant backed, and
the colonel signed, their furloughs.
“Listen, Grundy, you crimson, Irish, bog-
trotting Papist, you; this will be a general field-day
of a spree, a screeching, scorching tear that you’ll
remember when back at your native bog picking
the spuds out of the ashes with your toe-nails.
But we don’t blew all that three thousand now.
I’ll buy gold with fifteen hundred of it; you plant
the rest till we get another furlough next year. 5 ’
So Grundy nested Rs. 1500 in a new place,
while Tokey, going among the city baboos, bought
gold—mohurs and sovereigns—here and there a
few. Then they took furlough. Legends of that
lurid jamboree long lingered in Bengal. Benares
they visited, but mostly “adjacent parts,” where
were no inquisitive white troops ; “goinglarge” in
many lal bazars ; heard of in dak bungalows with
female companions of divers races. On foot they
journeyed never, “ Grabbies ”or “ toe-soldiers ”
might “mud-crush,” but not “gentlemen-hus-
sars ” with red gold belted around their waists.
They were borne, rajah-wise, in palanquins, or
tore through the night in tongas, and always the
spur-clad boots of men and the necks of bottles
protruded from their chariots, and the scent of
aralc perfumed an unfragrant land. Evading in-
fantry pickets and the “ frogs’-inarch,” they duly
reported at the guard-room of the 22nd, and, re-
invigorated, returned to duty, every pie of Rs.
1500 royally “ blewed.”
* » * *
The torrid months came and dragged. The
comrades relieved monotony by circumspect
minor debaucheries, leaving intact their nest-egg.
Tokey provided funds—how, not even Grundy
quite knew. Queer robberies in city and canton-
ments happened those days. The city jewellers
jabbered of a strange native,
swarthy yet Fering hi-faced,
who bought old gold coins
and ornaments, and carried
a white umbrella, which he
hooked on foot of the
sloping board whereon the
dealer, sitting at head there-
of, scattered his wares for
inspection. Treasure and
the stranger vanished simul-
taneously. The bazar pun-
dits concluded the stranger
was a white budmasli who
palmed valuables into his
accursed sun-shade. Then,
did not Sergeant “Kicker”
Kavanagh, who disciplined
his Dumpies, when they
were phenomenally crapu-
lous and pugnacious, with
his boots, lose the bag of
rupees he had acquired by
lavish blending of water
with Commissariat rum,
while canteen sergeant.
Tokey carried the rupees
over to Kavanagh’s bunga-
low for him, and the latter,
throwing them into his box,
affixed thereto a Chubb’s
padlock. “ Let the blashted
tumbril thieves pick that
lock, if they can ; an’, by
the howly, if I catch them
trym’, I’ll brain ’em wid
me collar-chain, so I will! ”
Kicker was a big, strong
man, of vicious temper;
nevertheless, there were no
rupees in the box when, next
morning, the trumpets
jrayed the reveille. There was
an inch-thick grease-track from
cot to door, and a box, with the
bottom cut out and a greasy rope
fast to the handle, in the veran-
dah. The adjutant was hideously
disconcerted. But Tokey showed
that only a naked nigger could
possibly have crawled in and
lubricated floor and box un-
heard, or have had the patience
to drag the latter, inch by inch,
to the door without awakening
the sergeant. Life thereafter was
made unpleasant to strange
natives visiting cantonments —
even for those money-changers
who came to report they had
been despoiled by a sahib, habited
as a baboo, and with a white um-
brella. Provosts and promiscuous
barrack-room dogs hunted these
before they could attain the ear
of higher authority.
» * »
A year passed. Tokey and
Grundy sat in the provost-cell
wherein they bivouacked when
on provost duty. Time ripened
for another gorgeous furlough.
“Go fetch those fifteen hun-
dred rupees, Grundy, and shove
them in your box, till I can get
gold for them, and we’ll put in
for another furlough.”
Grundy departed, returning
speedily exuding perturbation.
“ Howly saints! ut’s gone, every
anna av ut! ”
Tokey, blanching under his
tan, yet, from glittering, un-
canny eyes, shot the Irishman a
searching glance ; then gnawed his
wiry moustache: “Let’s go look !”
They followed the inside of
the prickly-pear hedge, walling
the twelve cells, to a hut, where
were tools for digging graves.
Tokey lit a lantern hanging unere;
first swiftly touching the wick and
top of the lamp. Both were
dead-cold. As the light streamed
out the men heard the hiss an
angry cobra and saw the evil thing erect,
swaying with expanded hood in the very hole, at
toot of the wall facing the door, in which the trea-
sure had been hidden.
Grundy slid outside. Tokey, his own snakelike
ayes fastened on the cobra, reached swiftly for a
oamboo, and, as the reptile struck, met it with
iu ( r ( n b e r two sword-cut and broke its back.

HER BEAUTY AND THE BEASTS.
THE LAD: “That’s the Chow that Kite

HIS BIT O' SKIRT: “ Yus. What I can’t make out is

“To hell with the hole ! When did you last

“When, is ut? Sure, last night; didn’t I lift

“Fist the light! ”
Tokey went gingerly to the hole, his bamboo
ready to strike. In it were young cobras, writh-
ing and wicked. He killed them. Then he Mew
his comrade had lied and had himself taken the
money. But the Welshman made no sign.
* * * *
Weeks after, Tokey, calling Grundy at mid-
night to relieve him on bazar duty, said ; “Have
you seen these nights one of our chaps prowling
about cantonments, as if searching for some-
thing? ”
Grundy’s face twitched, and he shook. “ Nivir
won ; why ? ”
“Some fellow’s sniffing around; but he’s too
smart for me and gets away.”
Grundy, gripping his stick, went out. Tokey,
grinning, lay on his charpoy, and, seemingly,
fell asleep. Soon a shadow crept athwart the
floor—Grundy watching the sleeper. Satisfied, he
strode to the chapel-compound, paced, counting
his steps, the long cactus hedge, halted, hesitated,
stooped, lifted a stone, examined a bulky parcel
beneath, replaced the stone, raked leaves above it,
and departed—chuckling.
He might not have chuckled had he known he
was shadowed by one carrying a keen knife in his
cummerbund ; he might have been less merry had
he seen his tracker prone on his belly, a few yards
away on the other side of the hedge ; murder
might have been, had he returned while the
shadower took up and removed the treasure.
Grundy strolled leisurely back to the cells.
There Tokey snored harder than before, and
Grundy chuckled once more at the humor of the
situation.
* . * • •
Next day a European soldier deposited Rs. 1500
in the local bank.
“ Prize-money ?” said the accountant, with the
genial smile and languid curiosity of one who
receives.
‘‘ Sweat-money ! My savings after seventeen
years of this God forsaken country.”
* * * •
So, when they were old—for 24 years of guard-
bed and variegated alcohol age the cavalryman
before five-and-forty—these two came to Canter-
bury depot, to dodge-in their small remaining
service. Recruits grinned at their middle-aged
attachment and grim old-soldier ways. But un-
ostentatiously ; as the comrades argued with
collar-chains or horse-logs. They guarded one
another’s barrack-room interests with aggressive
vigilance ; were inseparable, and nightly —bril-
liantly burnished, chrome-yellowed, and pipe-
clayed—clanked and swaggered out of barracks,
as horsemen go arrayed for love; Tokey,
a lean, weather-beaten and grizzled war-wolf;
Grundy, a pink-complexioned, red-headed, and
well-oiled dandy. Neither affected female so-
ciety. Both had realised alike the falsity of
woman, and the eternal verity of malt and hops.
Yet they met their fate, and their fate was—a
woman.
* * * *
Avoiding the grog-shops of a garrison town,
they sought, in country lanes, slow, old-fashioned
inns, in the low-pitched rooms of which scents of
lavender and musk mingled with coarser odours of
casks, and clustering roses, red and white, rapped
gently at the diamond window-panes. There they
refreshed, far from the guffaws of raucous re-
cruits and the giggling of girls enticed from rural
homes and the monotony of chastity by the irre-
sistible lures of jingling spurs, of shining steel and
scarlet.
* * * *
Such a serai they happened-on one moonlit
summer night. Roses bloomed over the low
porchway and climbed the creaking sign—a
Headless Horseman ; great hollyhocks and giant
sunflowers waved languorous in the garden ; the
air was heavy with perfume of jasmine and stocks.
Under the porch a girl, her head uncovered, stood
a’ tip-toe trying to reach the shining glow-worms
in a flower-box hung high. Tokey passed in.
Irish gallantry forbade Grundy leave woman in
distress.
“ Let me help ye, miss,” and waiting no leave—
as is the right way with women—he lightly swung
her up by the waist, then let her gently down and
saluted.
She laughed, the glow-worms shining in her
pink palm ; then came without the porch, so that
the moonlight glanced upon face and form of
such a woman as, since the world was, has had
power to bind the wills of men—a light, lithe,
almost fragile, but full-throated girl, with dark,
melancholy-gay eyes of shifting color, and coils of
brown hair shot with sunbeams. Her dress was a
spotless print ; sweet flowers and a snatch of soft
lace at her shapely breast—rustic she, yet all frag-
rant and delicious ; her speech and manner free
alike from gawkiness of rural Kent and mincings
of the modem board-school miss.
A white-haired dame, soft of voice, showed
them to a parlor and brought them drink. Tokey,
unbuttoning his stable-jacket, took a long pull at
s l ean; big back luxuriously,
“ Lady-killing, eh ; you dashed old fool ?”
“By th’ powers ! Tokey, but did ye see th’ two
eyes av her ? ”
“ i’ ve looked in the eyes of many women, white
and yellow, and never got good from the looking
yet The eyes of a woman ! My God, what
might I not have been now, but for the eves of a
woman ! ”
Nevertheless, when, later, the girl came, laugh-
ing, with a rose for Grundy’s jacket, Tokey looked
into her eyes and—his doom !
She sat carelessly on the arm of a big old chair
showing her pretty feet and ankles, and made
them tell her of the army, of the Mutiny—their
own story. Tokey was spokesman, and, under
the spell of the girl’s glances, the manner and lan-
guage of an earlier day came back to him. Grundy
Sa m. llttle ’ devourin & the woman with his eyes.
Then she told them of herself. She had been
in London, “in business with a friend,” but, fall-
ing delicate, had come back to be nursed by her
mother, who kept the “Headless Horseman”
Her name was May. And, when they were going
she made them promise to come again, well know-
ing they would come.
* * * «
Nightly they made love to her, each his own ’
way. Then fell a time when Grundy, horse-
kicked, went to hospital. Tokey faithfully tended
Ins comrade, smuggling in to him rum, tobacco,
and other delicacies. After, he rewarded himself
at the shrine of love. His wooing of Mav was
fierce—the courting of a fighting Celt.

The evening of the day Grundy was discharged
from hospital he (Tokey being on “fatigue”)
swaggered, alone and radiant, to the inn.
Mays out,” said her mother; “it’s no use
•' ou coming here. She’s going to marry respect-
“ple, to Mr. Williams, who saved £2OO in India.
May s not for you, Mr. M‘Carthy ! ”
He stood awhile, his mouth open ; then, com-
prehending, purpled with passion and held, shak-
ing, to a chair. Two hundred pounds ! —he knew
where that came from ; saw, as in a dream, white,
ant-scoured Allahabad, a long cactus hedge, an
empty hole, and felt the agony a rogue feels at
encountering a superior rascal. He said little,
incoherently cursed his comrade and the girl, and
staggered into the night.
The old lady laughed softly.
Before “last post ” he stalked into the barrack-
room.
“Come and drink, you pink-snouted bog-
trotter ! ” sang out Tokey, his normal irascibility
mellowed by beer, and pointing to a pot on the
table.
The other answered nothing. He went straight
to his shelf, took thence an uncut loaf (his and
Tokey’s ration of bread for following day) and
halved it with exactness. One moiety he threw
°n tl,e shelf, the other he left on the table.
“ Th’ curse av Judas an’ ye, ye Welsh bastard,
an’ an the Gov’mint as sarves out no ball-ammuni-
tion to this dam depot, so’s I might shoot ye dead
to wonst ! ”
There was dead silence in the wondering room.
The act of “cutting the loaf,” solemn as a sacra-
ment, meant final severance of comradeship. The
room, loving neither man, was yet honestly
affected. The British soldier of that day knew
nothing so sacred as comradeship. Loudly and
malignantly laughed Tokey.
* • • •
Henceforward neither spoke to the other.
Tokey nightly went courting. The recruits in the
room were ready enough to annoy the former com-
rades, in detail, but when the boldest once abused
Tokey in the latter’s absence, Grundy promptly
stretched him across his cot with a broken nose.
“ Lie still, ye pariah, till ye knows how to spake
respectful of your shupariors ! ”
Some weeks after the breach the two received
their prize-money. Three days later Tokey mar-
ried “without leave.” His wedding-present to
his bride was £4OO. She banked it the same day;
was taken “ suddenly ill,” and borne home by her
mother. Thus was Tokey’s honeymoon deferred,
and he repaii ed to his solitary martial cot. The
wedding had been kept quite quiet; if Grundy
knew, he gave no sign. Time went, but the bride
continued, her mother said, “dangerously ill.”
In six weeks Tokey was only permitted to see the
girl twice for a few minutes.
He began to age strangely, to pine and sicken.
He mooned drearily about barracks, cr lay speech-
less on his cot. Daily he grew more gaunt; some
unsightly cutaneous disorder attacked him ; the
room humorist opined he was “a bloomin’ old ruin
crumblin’ to bits.” But Grundy gathered liveli-
ness. He went out every evening that Tokey
remained in barracks. It was whispered he had
been seen much with “a square girl—her mother
keeps a country pub.” Grundy always returned
mentally elevated but painfully sober. He, on
these occasions, had a trick of looking at Tokey—
they still slept in adjacent cots —and of guffawing
in a manner which exasperated the wild-eyed
veteran to the verge of madness. But neither
spoke. The room waited an explosion.
* * * O'
For many days Tokey nad not quitted barracks
—even to enquire after his wife. What he had
seen, heard, or suspected was never known. But
all saw he was dying as he walked. One night
Grundy, just before “watch-setting, ’’ swaggered
noisily into the room. Undressing, he banged his
boots, spurs, and whip down, whistled, sang un-
melodiously, and stridently laughed whenever he
glanced in Tokey’s direction. The latter lay still
on his cot, only his eyes, alive and alight, showing
above his blankets. Grundy, hanging up his
jacket, so managed that the buttoned sleeve flipped
Tokey in the face.

“ Where’s the joke, Dad? ”
Then the latter started up, pouring out a stream
of black curses which made even the hardened
room shudder. He wound up: “Listen, you
sneaking hound, that tried to rob your own com-
rade, and hadn’t the savee to know how. I fol-
lowed you that night down the prickly-pear hedge,
and I’ve been sorry ever since 1 didn’t stick you,
for the Irish pig you are. But I’ve got the
money, and, ha ! ha ! I’ve got the girl ! ”
The other listened, half-hypnotised, not by the
words, but by the concentrated venom and malig-
nity of the other, and by his terrible eyes. But,
when Tokey ceased, Grundy burst into a wild
laugh.
“Hev ye that same? Hev ye? Ye lie; the
1 as the money and—howl and ramp, ye mur-
derin’ devil ! —Fve got the gal; when I like,
when I like.”
Then, half mad with liquor and revenge, he
tore Tokey’s pouch-belt from its peg and
dashed it in the owner’s face. The sharp
brass tip deeply gashed the old soldier’s
forehead. Only a few drops of black blood
oozed from the wound.
“ Take that, ye black-hearted Welsh divil ;
I’d give ye more, but ye’ll be dead in a
week ! ”
Tokey lay still. His face was grey and set.
When he spoke, he spoke quietly, without
heat : “ Yes, I’ll be dead in a week. And
take notice, you men ; witness for me ; when
I’m dead, if there’s a God in Heaven, or a
devil in Hell, I’ll return that foul blow, and
I’ll come and sit on that thing’s chest till it
dies of fright ! ” He turned his face to the
wall ; the doleful trumpet wailed —“Lights
out ' "
- .iey carried Tokey to hospital next morn-
ing. That night Grundy took the news to
the “Headless Horseman,” and was received
by May (well and blooming—she had never
been ill) and her placid mother with effusion.
He left the former some £2OO richer ; they
were to be married immediately Tokey’s death
left the innocent child free. Six nights later
the orderly-sergeant came into the room at
roll-call.
“Which of you’s next for fatigue?” He
looked at his duty-roster. “ Lewis, you go to
hospital to sit up with Private Williams ! ”
“Tokey bad, sergeant?” asked the room-
corporal.
“Doctor says he can’t live a week; smart
old soldier in his day, Tokey. Answer to
your names, you chaps.”
Grundy came in before “last post ’’—sober
and apparently depressed. The room said
nothing to him, but grinned cavernously.
* * * *
“Lights out !” sounded. The stillness of
night fell, broken only by kick of unquiet
horse, the passionate whinny of mare in the
stable below, or the melancholy monotone of
the hoarse sentry—“ Number three —and all’s
'veil ! ”
» • * *
The room never could explain why, about 2
a.m., every man woke, coldly sweating; each
fancying he alone was awake and eacli ashamed
to call-out. When the corporal, quaking, tried
to strike a match, there arose an awful and piercing
shriek, with the rattle and jingle as of belts and
arms throv.n down. A cold air stole through the
close and stable-scented room, and with the gleam
of the corporal’s candle arose a babble of ex--
clamation.
“ What the devil was that ? ”
The corporal leapt from his cot. “ Stop him !
Stop him ! ” he yelled to the man lying next the
room door.
But a figure in hospital dress, a figure seen by
all the room and by the sentry on No. 3 post (no
recruit can be trusted to stay his two hours on
that post in Canterbury barracks, even to this
day, though this true story has long been forgotten)
vanished, though there was neither opening nor
closing of the room door.
Grundy lay white and still, Tokey’s pouch-belt
across his breast; on his brow a broad gash trick-
ling blood. It was Grundy who had shrieked.
Now he lay insensible, his open eyes staring into
space. They dashed water over him and he
seemed to revive, but spoke never a word. In his
eyes was the terror of death and hell.
An hour after, just when the room had recovered
sufficiently to pretend to laugh, the door was
suddenly opened. The men, their nerves fretted
like worn fiddle-strings, almost yelled.
It was only Lewis, however, returning from
hospital-fatigue.
“Hello, you chaps, what’s up? Poor old
Tokey’s dead, died an hour ago. Died hard, my
oath ! He jumped up in his cot, howled, laughed,
made as if to snatch something from a peg, threw
his hand out, shrieked, ‘ Here I am, Grundy ! ’
and fell back, grinning, but dead as a ’orse-log.
Orderly and me finished Tokey’s medical comforts;
and—l’m not for morning stables, corporal; mind
that! ”
* • * *
When the principal military medical officer saw
Grundy’s dead body next morning, he was puzzled.
“ Heart, I suppose ; though I should say he died
of nightmare.” He lifted a heavy eyelid with his
strong white fingers. “Whew! Look at the
horror in that eye ! ”
J. EVISON.
TO LAURA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If winter snows are falling
On you, my only love,
They fall as soft as twilight,
Or cooing note of dove.
The newer years are swelling
The music of the old,
No tale of discord telling.
Life’s fires refine Love’s gold.
I sing no song of Winter :
The garden whence 1 glean
Hath flowers, an l scents of Summer,
And melody, rny Queen.
vie.
You do not dull your lustre
Nor lose your early art;
You have the richer sweetness
The ripening years impart;
The same old lights come playm
Like sunbeams in your eyes,
My little griefs allaying—
You know where my heart lie
nd still your hand hath cunning
To strike a chord that thrills;
It is a goodly garden
That love, my Laura, tills.
There have been tearful partings.
And changes not a few :
One flower was plucked in Spring-time,
All wet with morning dew;
There has been winter weather;
There have been summer joys;
But still, we are together,
Not Time nor Season cloys;
t he years that have been garnered
Are fair, as you are fair,
All pearls, that make a chaplet
For you, my Queen, to wear.
The voices of the old-time
Come whispering in the win ,
'There is one strain of sadness,
One gone we left behind.
The Wine of Life is sweeter
When old, to you and me;
The stream of Love runs deeper
The nearer to the Sea.
A nd still we are together;
The winter snows may fall—
You shall be still, my Laura ;
Keep what I gave you—All.
ANDREW SARD.
Johnson.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Johnson and his mate came to the cattle-station
“per boot,” and camped beside the garden.
J ohnson led a dissolute life when he could. His
mate did worse ; he played the concertina, the
strains whereof nightly attracted Ah Sam, who,
leaning over the garden-fence, applauded vigor-
ously.
After two days the mate went away. “It
ain’t a caper of mine, smooging to a Chow for
tucker, ” he said.
Johnson stayed, and his acquaintance with Ah
Sam ripened into friendship. He listened sympa-
thetically while the latter poured forth his horti-
cultural troubles. He made mental notes while
fche unsuspecting Chow just sweated out informa-
tion—uncommon, though “garden.”
Armed with this stock of new ideas, Johnson
again asked the boss for work.
“You see, it’s this way,” said he, “I’m a gar-
dener by trade, and that yellow agony down there
is just ruining that garden. I don’t want to jump
another man’s job, but I’ve a wife and kids to
support.”
Johnson was a fluent liar, and the boss just
hated Chinamen. “ Tell Ah Sam to come up, and
you can start work to-morrow.”
A Chinaman does not fly into a rage ; he works
himself up. So when Ah Sam returned with his
cheque, he at first merely paused near Johnson’s
camp to say, “You welly sinar’ man, Misser
Johnson,” and passed on. He stopped at a let-
tuce-plat to add, “My wo’d ! Plen’y you logue !
Blully new-chum, I t’ink.” Then, gathering fire
and inspiration from some ravishing cabbagee, he
came back flaming. “Hi ! You blully new-
chum ! You come heah. Me give’m you tucka.
You take’rn my job ! Hi ! See ! You take’m
my hat ! Come on ! You take’m my coat ! You
take’m my tlousel, too ! You t’ink me no savee
fi’? Come on, you blully lascal—me fi’ you all
li! ”
“ Oh ! give it a rest,” said Johnson.
• * * *
Years after, in Plumton, the two again met.
Ah Sam owned a garden. Johnson owned a wife,
temporarily ; or, rather, the woman owned him.
The garden and a lucrative opium-trade with
the blacks kept Ah Sam in affluence. The
buxom wife, young, but with a varied experience
of husbands, mainly kept Johnson—kept him in
order, too. Ruled him with a rod of iron if a
stick wasn’t handy.
Johnson was the town nondescript. But there
was one thing on which he could always rely,
when times were bad—an unfailing supply of
vegetables.
Ah Sam was the most forgiving of men. He
beamed unctuously on Johnson ; would occasion-
ally bring the daily cabbage—when Johnson was
out.
A race-meeting down the river took Johnson
out of town for a few days. On his return the
news was “ broken ” to him by Ebenezer, the bell-
man “ I say, Johnson—your old woman’s given
you brusher, and gone down to Ah Sam’s.”
The devil she has!” Johnson answered,
languidly. Them Chows has a great fancy for
big women.” J
“Hum !” said Ebenezer, “you take it prettv
cool If it was my old woman, I’d break that
heathen’s skull for him.”
“There’s no need for me to do anything of that
sort, said Johnson, reminiscently feelin" his
head. “ She’s about big and willin’ enough to do
hat herself. Seems to me,” lie added, reflec-
tiveiy L was born to bring misfortune on that
there Chinaman !
A. D. ELLERMAN.


Jealousy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Flies buzzed, heat wavered in the air, glare beat
furiously through the window, and Maloney’s goat
was on the verandah again. The C.P.S. seized
the first thing handy, which happened to be the
cash-book, and hurled it at the friendly creature.
The volume fell unerringly into the bucket by the
tank. The goat, more in sorrow than in anger,
sought the Sergeant’s garden. It was doing nicely
on the last geranium and was passing on to the
Sergeant’s pants when Mrs. Murphy’s eye rested
upon it. Falling over a kopje of flower-pots she
broke the broom with one magnificent stroke
against the fence and rose with her mouth full of
grief and soil. The goat wandered into the house,
trod on the baby, and escaped by the back-door.
The C.P.S. recovered the Govt, property, and
spread it tenderly in the sun to dry. He felt
miserable and unstrung. It was more than a
month since a pink monkey had chased a blue ser-
pent over the ceiling, ora purple centipede stepped
out of the ink-bottle and addressed him by his
Christian name. He had not even been drunk for
a week. Why was the P.M. so diffident in start-
ing? Turn about-turn about. That had been
the arrangement. The C.P.S. felt keenly on the
matter. Was it Christian ?
The P.M.’s head, like an immense billiard-
ball, shone above the awful solemnity of the
Bench. Around it buzzed an excited army of
flies. Each tapped gently on the elderly legal
cranium. Then, terrified by the hollow sound,
whole contingents fled into his eye.
The harmless drunk, awaiting acquittal, suddenly
found himself fined £l, and was removed by Ser-
geant Murphy in a dazed condition. All the way
to the watch-house he was longing for sympathy,
but he received nothing but a heavy punch in the
stomach when Mrs. Murphy missed the goat.
The P. M. remained for a few minutes in splen-
did isolation on the bench, saying “ Damn ! ”
Then he rose. The Court and the day’s work
were over.
The P. M. sauntered into the clerk’s room with
a pompous air.
“ Plenty of work, Jinks ? ” he said, jauntily.
“Pounds of it,” retorted Jinks, shoving the
cash-book out of sight.
“Nothing like it ” said the P.M. loftily. They
fell to talking dog and horse.
Presently the P.M. said :—“ I may not be down
to-morrow, Jinks. I doubt if there will be any-
thing doing. In any case you can sit for me.”
That sounded like business. The C.P.S.
beamed.
“ Certainly, certainly,” he said, with a smile of
intense benevolence. “ Only too glad, I’m shuah.”
The P.M. cocked his hat and left with a
military swagger, slapping his leg with his cane.
The C.P.S. watched him behind the window.
“Straight for Maloney’s parlor—straight as a
bolt,” he chuckled, rubbing his hands. “He
meets Cherry, the banker. Good, they turn
simultaneously in the same direction. They are
accosted by Phipps—Aha !He also turns. They

WILLIAM WATTLE.
The Forerunners.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sleep ye lonely by the rivers, sleep ye lonely in
the ranges,
Where the weird bush voices whisper and the
night’s wind-anthem changes
To the singing of the wild birds and the music
and the calling ?
Do the shadow-voices call thee from a land the
wide seas over ?
Calling, oh, so softly calling, for the coming of the
rover—
For the one that never cometh are a woman’s
tears still falling ?
Do you fancy in your sleeping as the waving grass
caresses,
You can hear sweet women coming, hear the
rustle of their dresses,
Hear the soft tones of a mother —hear a glad girl
hail her lover ?
Oh, ye Sleepers of the Plains ! doth your lone
grave seem more lonely
When the ghostly moon is rising and your spirit
heareth only
The crying of the curlew and the piping of the
plover ?
Wiien the heated earth is cracking and the
Drought Queen’s hot breath hisses,
Calling to the Sun, her lover —does it bring to
thee her kisses ?
Ye who knew a woman’s scorning, ye who loved a
little while—
Do ye hear the words she uttered as she told you
how she loved you,
Do you see her standing queenly as an angel far
above you—
Do you hear her recantation —do you see her
mocking smile ?
Though the old folks sent you southward with
“God speed” for sailing-orders,
Though a woman sent you roaming to the Lost-
Lands’ farthest borders,
There ye sleep by creek and ranges, lost to old-
world love and hating,
And the Austral mother claims you as her first-
born and her dearest,
Ye who tasted her caresses —ye unto her heart are
nearest.
Ye were first to call her Mother —she was weary
with her waiting.
Waiting through the cold, dim ages, all her
beauties hid and folden,
Till ye came and saw her beauties, saw the wattle
blooming golden,
And she took ye to her bosom thrilling in her
expectation,
Took the hopeful and the hopeless, laughed in
pride to see your daring,
Watched you tread her silent bushways, saw your
lonely desert-faring,
Saw ye as the lone forerunners of her people—and
a nation.
Ye who were the old world’s failures, ye were
foremost in the sowing,
Hearts of lead and bitter spirits, all uncaring and
unknowing,
Took her love and braved her anger, flinging wide
the door of changes.
World-forgotten, she forgets not, she will guard
your place of sleeping
And the anthem of the nightwind is thy Austral
mother weeping
For the dying of the first-born by her rivers and
her ranges.
N.S.W.
C.P.Q.C.

THE POET AND THE MUSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE POET
AND
THE MUSE
THE POET.
The Darling of the Year with sifted gold
Of sunshine makes the old earth young again;
Spring’s dancing music lilts in pulse and vein ,
And all the world is merry as of old :
But shadows only dwell within my brain ;
My heart is like a hearth with ashes cold.
0 Muse, if I have loved thee late and long,
If I have worshipped thee, and made a shrine
To hold thine image in this heart of mine,
And served thee with the service of my song,
And poured my years out at thy feet divine—
Where art thou now when ghosts around me
throng ?
Where is the pride, above the pride of kings,
That once I felt Avhen in the glowing air
I saw the shining wonder of thy hair,
And heard the rustle of thy radiant wings
Alas, and have I come by ways so fair
To dust and ashes and the end of things i
My soul is compassed round by phantoms vast,
Whose black wings slmt from me the sweet
blue sky
And blue broad sea I knew when thou wert nigh.
O Muse, return to me ! . . . She comes at last !
And I can now--, clear-voiced, like Agag, cry—-
Surely the bitterness of Death is past!
THE MUSE.
Thou wert my servant in the time gone by,
And through the world I led thee by the hand,
And showed thee all the beauty of the land,
And all the marvels of the Earth and Sky.
lliy nights and days I held at my command,
And unto thee I gave the Seeing Eye.
The sacred secret of the infinite
That burns beneath the beauty of the rose,
A.nd in the hearts of youth and maiden glows,
And fills and thrills the world with life and light,
And is the soul of all that breathes and grows—
made it visible unto thy sight.
But now another Muse holds thee in thrall.
Thou canst not serve us twain : that is the law.
THE POET.
“ 0 Goddess, ere thou dost from me withdraw,
Show me what other Muse I serve withal! ”
THE MUSE.
“ Behold ! ”
The Poet turned and saw
The shado wof a Wine-Jar on the wall.
N.S.W.
V. J. DALEY.


AN OLD PHOTO
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
ISS LAURA, with a careless laugh,
Cried — “ What a funny photograph l
To think Papa, now grown so fat,
Was ever such a ‘sketch’ as that/”
Her mother for a moment smiled.
“ And what a meek and saintly face 1
Just like an angel saying grace—
A sort of Good Young Man who Died.”
The mother turned her head aside:
And answered, “Don’t talk nonsense, child!"
She’d kept the photo, hid away—
'Twas taken on their wedding-day—
And as she thought of then and now
She brushed her hand across her brow,
For truth is not to be denied;
Reluctantly the wife confcss’t
Her girl had spoken truth in jest.
The “old man” lives, so coarse, so changed,
And still her heart from his estranged
Clings to the “ Good Young Man who Died.”
EDMUND FISHER.
East Lynne on the Castlereagh.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Uncle Jim was kneeling on the hearth, giving
Bobbie a lesson in boxing, and from time to time
he ducked cleverly to show us all how to dodge a
“stinker.” Uncle has the dirtiest left on the
Castlereagh ; in fact, you can’t see it for dirt at
times. Little Bobbie danced across the hearth,
keeping his feet well in hand and his eye on
Uncle’s nose.
“I’d like Bob Fitzsimmons ter come up th’
river. Holy Peter! I’d back my chance; I’d
put th’ selection on myself.” Uncle feinted at
Bobbie carelessly, and Bobbie rushed in like a
young tornado, bringing his hard little right across
Uncle’s nose. Uncle Jim drop >ed his hands ; his
wrinkled face went red with fury. “ Yer a spite-
ful little crawler ! What did ye hit me like that
fer ? Couldn’t yer see I wasn’t lookin’ ? ”
Bobbie stood with his guard up, his small body
quivering with excitement. “I—l didn’t mean
ter hit yer father, but yer said I was ter hit-out.”
“Go ter bed,” thundered Uncle, “ Yer a spite-
ful little dog ! ”
Bobbie went to bed.
Aunt smiled sarcastically, but did not look up
from her ironing. In the silence that followed we
could hear the rain pelting on the leaves outside,
and the clitter, clitter of stones where the sluicing
torrents raced in the wheel-ruts past the verandah.
“ Moloney lost eight rams yisterday, an’ he’d a
lost eight more if Black Harry hadn’t swimmed
’em over the crick one at a time. Moloney gave
him tixapen for doin’ it.” Somebody spoke from
an obscure corner, but Uncle only rubbed his
nose.
“What a night for tramps and stockmen !”
sighed Aunt. “ Thank God ! no one’s out be-
longing to me.”
There was a sudden tap ! tap !at the door. We
all looked at each other. Uncle said, “Gawd’s
truth ! ”
Tap ! tap ! tap !
“Goto the door, Jim.” Aunt looked at him
sternly, but he remained rooted to his place.
“You coward ! ” she whispered ; “ I’ll go.”
She opened the door gently, and the inrushing
wind brought in the smell of wet cattle and trees,
“ Who are you ? What do you want ? ” Aunt
faced Something in the darkness, and the Some,
thing whispered feebly and put a thin hand inside
the door.
“ I’m Jim Sloan’s wife,” it said ; “ and I’ve come
back.”
Aunt made a sharp, outward movement with
her hands, and then she screwed up her mouth
and laughed hysterically. And the querulous
voice in the darkness broke in upon it like a wail:
I was married to Jim in Sydney years an’ years
ago. . . . I’ve walked miles. Let me in, for God’s
sake. ... I am Jim’s wife. He will know me.
He will. ...”
Uncle Jim remained on his knees; his livid jaws
gasped with an expressionless horror. He made a
lunatic gesture with his left hand. “ By—God—
that’s—Norah ! Holy Peter ! ”
Nobody stirred or lifted a hand until Aunt
mode a wild forward clutch and dragged the
Voice into the room. Then she did a strange
thing—locked the door and barred it. “ I’ve
heard something of this business,” she said,
quietly. “Now you hold your tongue?” She
made a fierce gesture at Uncle Jim. The Voice
stood in the centre of the room, a dripping em-
bodiment of Rags. The face was gipsy-dark and
the eyes glittered like knives through clotted,
rain-drenched hair. She rocked forward and her
lips smiled ; then she dripped, dripped.
Aunt stood off and beat her foot on the boards.
The children looked at Uncle Jim as though lie
had turned suddenly into a dingo. We had all
heard of the Woman who ran away, but everyone
had agreed that she died in America long ago.
None of us had read story-books, so the event was
terribly new and full of possibilities.
Every man exhibits some peculiar trait in his
hour of agony. Uncle Jim sparred at an imagin-
ary Bob Fitzsimmons.
“ I’m his wife,” said Rags ; “ I’m Norah Sloan.
I’m hungry, cold. May I look at the fire ? I’m
so cold ? ”
Aunt Sloan is an exceedingly sensible bush
woman ; she didn’t unbar the door that night oi
sit outside on the rail and scream. She simply
packed the kids to bed and slammed Uncle Jim
into the back room. Then she returned to Rags
and shook her severely by the shoulders. “So
A COLONIAL ROUGEMONT.
IRONBARK : “ Have you ever been in London, Stumpy? ”
STUMRY : “ Well, wat a queschin to ast a bloke! O’ course I ’ave; ’ad a job ringbarkin' there
at a pound a week.”
you’ve come back from the dead to disturb me and
my children. What are you going to do ? ”
Rags prostrated herself on the hearth.
“Don’t send me out to-night.”
Aunt laughed bitterly. “ Send you out, to hear
of you scandalising me all over the country. No,
I’ll keep you, d’ye hear? I’ll make you work.”
Aunt kicked the fire into leaping tongues of light.
Then she did another furious thing—took out a
tin of oatmeal, threw it into a billy with some
milk, and stirred it over the flames. Rags
stretched out her hand to the fire, and the white
blaze from the myall log illumined her with a
ghostly brilliance. “I’ve walked from Dubbo,”
she whispered. “I thought I’d never find the
place. . . . Brannigan’s Siding, isn’t it?” She
took in Aunt with the shift of an eye, and sighed
wearily.
“ You are Jim’s wife? The children are yours ;
but there is one . . . mine : you will not deny
that ? ”
Aunt whitened to her temples. “Don’t speak
to me of your child, you—runaway! ”
The gipsy face and knife-like eyes looked up
from the fire. “Don’t deny him to me, Emily.
You have your own ; aren’t they enough ? ”
i Aunt crouched over her with clenched hands.
“If I were a churchwoman I’d drive you and
your brat from under my roof to-night; but I’m
not. So . . . you will stay and work —ay, I will
see that you work.”
Suddenly a small voice rose from behind the
thin partition: “Mother, mother; tell father I
didn’t mean to hit him so hard. ... I didn’t
mean ter.”
Rags locked her hands together, her face turned
slowly towards the sound of Bobbie’s voice.
“ Will you, Emily . . . let me go in ? . . . He
will not see me in the dark. ”
Aunt stirred the oatmeal scornfully, without
looking at her visitor. Then, with sudden anger,
she said, “ You will sleep in this corner to-night—
after you have eaten —and, if you attempt to see
that child, I’ll——.” Aunt stood on her toes and
choked an imaginary Rags.
“I can wait,” said the visitor, over her shut
hands ; “I can wait.”
* * * *
A little rest and food made a startling differ-
ence in Norah Sloan’s appearance. Her dark
gix»y face became a matter of surpassing interest
THE HEATHEN IN HIS BLINDNESS.
“ I fear,” said the new shepherd, sweetly smilinj, “I fear that your father, the chieftain, does
not like me!”
CANNIBAL BELLE (apologetically): “ Well, you see, Pa has turned vegetarian .” (Pa is seen in
the middle distance pensively cracking a cocoanut.)
to the cookies and flash boundary - riders who
sometimes dropped in for a cup of Aunt’s tea.
But Norah would sit at table
with head bent, and eyes star-
ing vacantly at the handful of
violets in her waistband.
Uncle Jim’s position was
hard to define.
He became absent-minded,
and never took his gaze off the
shingles. One night by chance
his eye met Norah’s ; her face
relaxed, and it was as thougn
someone had suddenly
drenched her cheeks with
wine. Uncle acted ridiculous-
ly, jerked his head backward,
and made the following irrele-
vant remark : “If Bobbie
hits me on the nose agin I’ll
stop it with me right.”
Norah took a strange in-
terest in Bobbie —he, too, was
gipsy-dark, and not a bit like
Aunt—and it was stranger
still that he should permit
this immobile woman to take
him in her arms. If he
mounted a horse she would
steal by, watching fearfully,
until Aunt bustled-up, saying,
“Oh, my goodness ! the ani-
mal won’t bite him.”
Norah would grip the bridle
with both hands.
“ But he isn’t old enough to
handle a raw man-eater like
this. Oh, Emily ! Sup-
pose. ...”
Aunt would suddenly leave.
At a preconcerted signal,
Bobbie and Norah would steal
from the homestead by dif-
erent tracks, only to meet
again beyond the bridge. And
the gully would hide them in
its tree-flanked depths, while
the big eucalyptus spoke to the
high wind above, saying: “Not here; not here.
1 lush, sh, sh, sli ! ” And from some invisible hollow
would come the echo of Norah’s voice : “ Oh, my
dear; oh, my dear !” Strange, too, that no one ever
hunted for them. Aunt would turn from the
children to wipe a bit of soot from her cheeks,
saying: “Oh, let them alone. Norah’s terribly
fond of the lad. God help us, it’s a queer world ! ’
Uncle Jim’s position was hard to define.
* » * *
One day Norah was sitting under the big
eucalypti, patching the children’s clothes. Uncle
came out of the shed where he had been talking
to a saddle—one he had won in a raffle. He
stopped as he caught sight of her, shuffling his
feet uneasily ; a new brand of blush suffused h:s
sun-dried visage as she turned and caught his eye.
He became a study in jerks. “ Well, Norah —well,
Norah, my gel, how yer bin giftin’ on ? ” He looked
at the sky and then towards the homestead, where
aunt’s big shadow leaped to and fro across the
doorway. “Ye had a long spell away, Norah ? I
should say ye had a roarty time of it here and
thear.”
Norah clicked her needle, but did not meet his
glance again. After a while she spoke, and her
voice was keyed in a loud whisper.
“Jim, I had a great time. I was never sorry
that I left you until I heard that my son had
grown so fine, and ... I came home. Jim, shall
I speak the truth ? ”
Uncle threw out his insignificant chest and
breathed hard. “Speak th’ truth, Norah —by-
gones is bygones.”
“ Well, Jim, I had a great time. Living with
you was horrible—l don’t want to offend —but it
was ghastly. So I cleared with a young drover
and overlanded it to Adelaide. Mother was a
gipsy, you know, and father an Irish planter, and
I had blood in me that sang to every word a man
dared buzz in my ear. I didn’t stay with my
drover sweetheart long . . . Oh no, not long. . . .
Jim, I’ve lived, lived ! If you think that I regret
my past you are wrong. I wish I could live my
life again.”
Her nimble fingers worked steadily ; she did
not take her eyes from the ground. “ Jim, how
—how have you been enjoying yourself ? ”
Uncle cast a rapid glance at the homestead,
and then took in a sharp breath.
“ Oh hell ! ” he said.
Aunt came to the verandah stealthily and cast
a swift glance in their direction.
“Jim ! ” she shouted, “ come here.”
Uncle loafed towards her, whistling absent] >\
Aunt’s eyes went from his face to Norah’s. S' e
frowned. “Jim, you’ll have to watch yourself,
d’ye hear ? ”
“Eh?” Uncle straightened himself suddenly.
“ Oh, what rot ! ” He turned away and walked
in the direction of Brannigan’s Hole.
Uncle Jim’s position is hard to define !
ALBA DORION.
“ Raspberry ” : When present scribe had
occasion, some years ago, to visit a certain un-
pretentious Tasmanian hamlet, it was a gay place,
consisting of a pub., a bobby, and 400 goats. A
strong goaty odour pervaded the vicinity, and, on
the evening of his arrival, writer was treated to
cold boiled goat and damper. Sustained by
golden visions of fish for the morrow’s breakfast,
he retired to a ghostly four-poster in the front
bedroom. All that night an endless procession of
goats sprinted through the verandah, bleating
horribly, whilst the owners of the caravanserai
snored, sweetly somewhere in the back. The
local goats appeared to be purely nocturnal in
their habits, the perfume alone telling of their
existence by day. Next morning there was
damper and fried goat for breakfast. The fisher-
man had forgotten to call, or catch, or some-
thing. For dinner, we had hot roast goat
and damper, and for tea damper and cold
roast goat. It was a trifle monotonous, but
so it went on—curried goat, grilled goat, hashed
goat, minced goat, hot stuffed goat, and cold
baked goat, and always the deathless damper.
One night it rained, and a multitude of goats
c .mped in the verandah. After heaving all the
furniture that would conveniently go out of the
window, writer fell into an uneasy slumber ; and
about sunrise was aroused by an ungodly disturb-
ance outside. On investigation he observed the
boss of the establishment chasing a herd of goats
round the building with a gun. After about three
laps he let fly and one of the fugitives dropped its
hind-end to the ground, but fled serenely along on
its forequarters, till the eldest hope of the family
smote it on the head with an axe. Then the
missus, clad mostly in purple corsets and red
carpet-slippers, rushed out and sawed its head off
with the bread-knife. That day I fled.

Cardy's Example!
A GOOD THING IN GODS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I don’t know what nation had the honor of pro-
ducing Cardy. His name was apparently an in-
genious contrivance for affiliating him, at will,
with any one of the four countries whose lan-
guages he spoke with equal fluency. “Cardy ”
presumably English—became French ip “Carde,”
Italian in “Cardi,” German in “Karde.” But
even the additional possession of sundry oriental
dialects didn’t make Cardy vain of his linguistic
attainments.
“ What’s the use,” he said, “of a man speaking
half-a-dozen languages, if he’s got to be a fool in
all of them ? ”
From which observation it will be evident that
Mr. Cardy himself was no fool. To what extent
he was the “other thing” I had not been able to
determine, for the pietetic element in his
talk, instead of being spread ostentatiously on
the surface thereof, was rather woven into it with
a skill that made me sometimes fancy I had
chanced at last upon a real live Christian, instead
of merely a pretender whose methods were less
crude than common.
At a little port on the south coast—which I
shall pseudonymise as Momanglain—Cardy told
me the steamer was to drop him. There he had
lived, he said, for a good many years, and there
he had been “enabled to do some excellent work
amongst the heathen.”
If he had called himself a “ humble instrument,”
or finished up with “ Praise the Lord ! ” my pre-
sent reader would never have heard of him. One
of his words, as it was, gave me its accustomed
jar.
“ Heathen ? ” I repeated. “ Mahometanism
isn’t heathenism. It’s as monotheistic as Christi-
anity ; more so, in fact—for you’ve got three
gods ! Of course ”
“When I said ‘heathen,’” Cardy put in, “I
meant heathen ! Haven’t you heard of the Rock
Temple of Momanglain ? ”
Of course I had, and of course I ought to have
remembered that in localities where reminders of
the old faith abound, Javanese Mahometanism is
little more than a name.
“It is one of the great sights of Java,” Cardy
said. “ Alas ! that such immense labour should
have been expended upon a mere monument of
superstition.
“ Oh, well,” I said, “ if you come to that, pretty
well everything that endures is the work of super-
stition.”
“My young friend,” Cardy answered, with the
nearest approach he had yet made to the groan-
commiserative, “you are, I regret to perceive,
deeply tainted with the appalling scepticism of
the age.”
“ Deeply tainted with an objection to be led by
the nose ! But, with regard to this temple, I
should very much like to see it. I think I’ll land
with you, and wait for the return-boat.”
“Do. And if you’ll stay with me I shall be
glad to have you.”
“ Very kind ! But don’t think me ungracious if
I ask, have you an eye to my ‘regeneration,’ as
the phrase goes ? ”
“ Well,” Cardy said, blandly, “as far as Chris-
tian example goes, I trust you may receive spiri-
tual benefit. Precept, I fear— ”
“ Would be thrown away ? You are right; but
I can stand any quantity of example.” •
It did not take me long to see that it
was only the old story —Cardy’s weasel-
eyed catechumens were Christian only to the
extent that Christianity meant credit at the
establishment wherefrom the Bible-dispensing
Cardy dispensed also the knife that cutteth not,
the gun that bursteth, and the cane-rum that
slayeth afar off. For of such matters —as well as
of Manchester shoddy and Birmingham pinchbeck
—Cardy was the only importer in Momanglain;
and I wasn’t much surprised to see how quickly
Salvation had to sit behind lucre when, on the
second day of my stay, a sawn-off old waggon of a
‘ ‘ country-wallah ” made her appearance with a
cargo re-consigned to Cardy from Singapore.
“ I’ll be up to my neck in it for the rest of the
week,” he said; “so I can’t go with you to the
temple myself. But I'll send Sajib with you—he
can jabber a little Malay.”
* * * *
The early sun was red upon the huge facade
that rose sheer out of a dark-green tangle of wild
pine-apple, azalea and vanilla—with here and
there a cotton-tufted cuppoo, throwing a graceful
shadow on the ruddy rock. My guide—three feet
of coffee-colored urchin, bisected by a six-inch
strip of blue batek —looked askance at the cotton-
trees. They were favorites, he told me, in such
Malay as he could muster, of the Poontiana —a
child-destroying demon much dreaded by these
people. This particular Poontiana, it appeared,
resided in a secret part of the temple before us.
“ But, with a white lord,” concluded the imp, “ I
fear not. Let us go up, lord ! ”
About twenty feet above us—reached by a flight
of worn stone steps —a square, door-like opening
was all—except the mighty scarping of the whole
vast rock—that showed the handiwork of man.
But, inside, by the light of the dawiar-torches, we
passed from chamber into chamber, each larger
than the one outside it, and all —in their dumb
destitution of emblem and ornament —their blind
privation of altar and of statue —more augustly
terrible than anything ever evolved from the
hyper-symbolical religion of the Hindus.
“ This is no Indie work,” I reflected, as we stood
in the middle of a chamber so immense that the
strong red light of our torches barely showed us
the far-away walls, and failed altogether to touch
the roof. “Some austerer and elder faith it was
that thus sublimated simplicity and left every-
thing so magnificently to the imagination. Let
us go on, Sajib ! ”
But the boy, when we got to what appeared to
be the inner egress by a narrow, slit-like passage,
objected with much trembling and a further re-
ference to the Poontiana.
“ There is only a cave,” he said —“near the sea.
And the passage thereto becometh low and narrow
as for a crawling beast, not for a great white lord.
Also, Cardee-Father hath seen the Poontiana in
that cave with his own eyes ! ”
“Cardy?” exclaimed the “great white lord,”
beginning to smell a rat.
“ Ay, lord ! And Cardee-Father knoweth all
things. Therefore go not in, lest thy bones be
made naked and thy friends brought to sorrow ! ”
Let Sajib stay then ; I would go in alone. But
at this he quaked more than ever. If the Poon-
tiana happened to be out, and, coming back,
should find him there alone, he was but a lost
Sajib ! _
I gave up the point, but as we went back
towards daylight I took the distance roughly in
paces, and made it 230 yards.
“ That certainly ought to go nearly to the sea-
face of the rock,” I thought, “and perhaps there's
an opening to the beach. And perhaps, again,
the ingenuous Cardy has an object in scaring the
natives away with this Poontiana-rubbish. Hanged
if I don’t come back here by myself, and see
if by any possibility this ‘just man made per-
feet ’ is coquetting with his Netherlandic Majesty’s
import duties.”
• • * •
Atduskof nextevening—as soonasCardy, piously
deploring the interruption to his bible-reading
classes, had gone down to the ship again—l set
out for the temple, equipped with my trusty little
Smith-Wesson, a pocket-compass, a flask of arrack,
and half-a-dozen damar-sticks.
It was a perfectly breathless night, with a
gauzy haze over the stars, and the slow regularity
of a sleeper’s breathing in the surf-pulse of the
resting ocean. Faintly the rhythmic fall came to
me over the huge rock as I clambered up the steps
and passed into the immemorial mystery of this
shrine that enshrined nothing but an idea,
I lit my torch, and went on—counting,
this time, the chambers I passed through.
Eight there were ; increasing in breadth from
about 5 yards for the first to no less than 60 for
the enormous final apartment, which had also a
length, as I found by stepping it, of 90 yards !
Ludicrously disproportionate to these vast
measurements appeared the fissure-like passage
by which I had to seek what had been, probably,
the adytum of this remarkable exemplar of
human persistence. About seven feet high, as I
judged—the passage was hardly two feet wide,
and, with the light held over my head, I fr’.lowed
it without noticing the gradual depression of the
roof, until the torch touched the stone above me.
Lower and lower, until I was obliged to stoop ;
then lower still—till, upon hands and knees, I had
to recognise that the height of the passage was
now only equal to its breadth. Badly hampered
by the torch, I crawled through about 20 yards of
these dimensions before I found myself suddenly
in the presumed residence of Sajib’s Poontiana.
It was, as he had said, a cave—inasmuch as the
roof was roughly vaulted by the hand of nature,
The sides I had no time to examine, for, from
somewhere not faraway, I caught at that moment
the voice of the saintly Cardy, vigorously cursing
somebody’s eyes—“and did they think he was
going to get a year or two in Padak because they
didn’t know how to parbuckle a blanketty cask
any more than so many beatified old women ! ”
Cut went my torch, and down went myself into
a corner, as a sudden opening in the gloom let me
see for a moment half-a-dozen figures struggling
against the stars. Then a few hurried words in
varying voices—all with the quick note of surrep-
titiousness—the dump of something heavy on the
rocky floor—and darkness again, until the flare of
a vesta sho wed me Cardy’s self-satisfied face, as,
with a soft whistling of “Safe in the Arms,”
he applied the match to a lamp brilliant enough
to enlighten me as to the distinguished coni-
pany I was in.
I was surrounded by gods !
In brass, and bronze, and earthenware ; care-
fully arranged in stacks, or contemptuously
tumbled into heaps ; Brahminically correct, schis-
matically erroneous in number of heads or arms,
or openly indicative of the debased phallicism of
ancient Java—here they were, in a multitude to
which Cardy was even now contributing from the
cask that had been hoisted-up and which he had
skilfully unheaded,
“Now, that,” and he held up to the light a
curiously-contorted brass Dramaja, “is a pretty
piece of work. I ought to know something about
these things, and hang me if I could swear this
wasn’t the best Burmese woik. How these
Brummagem chaps manage it is more than I
know ! ”
“ Christian example—eh, Cardy?”
The sudden voice, tumbling back in weird
echoes from the roof, made him start so that the
little brass god fell clattering into the cask again.
But he was quick to grasp the situation.
“ Oh, you’re there, are you ? ” he said carelessly,
“Come on deck! Now, what”—he added,
slowly, as I got up out of the shadows— “ what is
to prevent me putting a bullet into this ex-
ceedingly injudicious young man ? ”
“ Christianity ! ” suggested the injudicious one.
Cardy reflected my grin “ We’ll consider
Christianity ‘off,’ ” he said.
“It’s about time!”—and I took out my revol-
ver. “But, how does this go ?”
“ One better ! You’re a young man of foresight,
Brains, too ! You took my holiness alongside per-
haps, but I could see that you never exactly
hoisted it in.”
Coolly enough said, all this—considering the
shock attested by the fellow’s trembling fingers as
he went on unpacking his cask of paganism,
“Here!” I handed him my flask. “Hoist
some of that in !—and explain me this
pantheon business. All these gods, I mean ;
where do you get ’em, and what do you do with
’em ? ”
A liberal draught brought a flux of blood to his
f ace and of impudence to his manner,
“I get ’em from England—pious England !
the secret of whose greatness, they say, is the
Bible. And the biggest man in this god-making
hue is a pillar of some missionary society or other.
Oh, Lord ! ”—and Cardy chuckled till he choked—
then emptied the flask,
“And you?”
“Oh, I follow suit ! Any ass can run the ordi-
nary snuffiebusting business, but I fancy it’s a
stroke of genius to be ‘ converting ’ a couple of
score Javanese with the one hand, so to speak,
a fl d supplying idols to a couple of million of ’em
with the other !”
“ And this is your warehouse ? ”
“Has been!—for I suppose your conscience
won’t allow you to let the little game go on any
longer?”
“ Haven’t any conscience worth speaking of ! ”
I said, as I lit up my torch again. “ It’s only holy
humbugs like you and your Birmingham joss-
maker who make a fuss about their ‘ consciences.’
As for this god-importing business, it seems to me
you’ll hurt these people less with it than with
V our special brand of Christianity. I won’t inter-
fere.”
T) * * * *
. But fate dld ! A few months later Cardy “fell
la ” over an attempted opium-run, and now, I
understand, adorns the select society of Padak
Island—himself adorned in turn with a coarse
blue jacket, a big red number, and a neat steel
anklet.
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.

NEW SOUTH WAT.F.S GOVERNMENT RAILWAYS.
Christinas and New 7ear Holiday Excursions
IRTHf Snouiy mountains, the [ake Country, the Blue mountains, the Jenolan Coves,
a~ the (jauikesbury per, the Southern pi District, the |llauiarra District.
fin HE advent of the
I Christmas Holidays
is as heartily wel-
comed in New South Wa’es
as in England, and its value
locally is greater, in the
opportunity it affords the
metropolitan or the inland
resident of taking a rest at
a time when climatic condi-
tions cause a respite from
labour to be most enjoyed.
The former has every facility
for escaping for a time from
the routine of city life to
the cool atmosphere of the
charming mountain region
that lies so close to Sydney,
or the more distant, but
still easily accessible, snow
country “ down by Kosci-
usko.”
The inland dweller finds
the change equally pleasant
in leaving his wide and
silent areas to seek pleasure
in the many holiday attrac-
tions provided in the metro-
m
■
■ rad
I .
<yj£+'%
, :;4' . hf - -
OJST THE HAWHESBURY RIVER,
polis, or to enjoy the charms of the sea-
side. All are well-provided-for by the ex-
tended railway facilities arranged for at
Christmas time.
Sydney forms the main starting-
point for holiday-makers, most of the
leading pleasure resorts being readily and
cheaply accessible from the metropolis,
but the system of Holiday Excursion
Tickets enables country residents to visit
the principal beauty spots in their own
districts Thus, pleasure-seekers living
in Goulburn can enjoy the beauties of
the lovely country in which the FITZ-
ROY, MERYLA, and the other falls are
situated, or make a trip to the summit of
MOUNT KOSCIUSKO, while others resid-
ing in Bathurst may proceed to the
JENOLAN CAVES or the BLUE
MOUNTAINS, without the trouble or
cost of a journey to Sydney.
Special Excursion Tickets from Sydney
enable most of the picturesque localities
within easy reach of the metropolis to be
conveniently and economically visited.
On the Northern Line is KURRING-
GAI Chase, the new National Park, in
the heart of which is COWAN CREEK,
with its unlimited facilities for boating
and fishing ; the HAWKESBURY
RIVER, queen of Australian streams, on
which delightful trips may be made by
the commodious steamer General Gor-
don ; BRISBANE WATER, the TUG-
GER AH LAKES, and LAKE MAC-
QUARIE, where boating, sailing, fishing,
bathing, and other sports may be enjoyed
to the uttermost, alternated with delight-
ful rambles among the ferns and flowers
of the surrounding country.
To the West is the NEPEAN RIVER,
with its lovely surroundings ; the BLUE
MOUNTAINS, with their magnificent
panoramic views, glorious waterfalls and
beautiful scenery ; each stopping place
having its own special attractions; while
from KATOOMBA or MOUNT VIC-
TORIA a visit may be made by coach or
on horseback to the JENOLAN CAVES,
the great natural marvels of Australia,
and said to be equal to anything of their
kind in existence. The Government has
also recently provided excellent accommo-
dation convenient to the Caves, while the
road journey to them is alone worth the
trip, even if the subterranean wonders
did not exist.
Southwards is the charming tourist
district between MITTAGONG and
GOULBURN, which i s
rapidly becoming a fashion-
able Summer resort. From
here either by way of BOW-
RAL or MOSS YALE, the
FITZROY, BELMORE,
MERYLA, LODDEN, and
other singularly picturesque
waterfalls may be visited,
and a descent effected by
way of KANGAROO
VALLEY, the BULLI
PASS, o r MACQUARIE
PASS, into the richly fertile
Illawarra District. From
GOULBURN, Holiday Ex-
cursion Tickets can be
obtained for LAKE
BATHURST; Bungen-
dore, for LAKE GEORGE
or Cooma, for the YAR-
RANGOBILLY OAVES,
the most extensive in Aus-
tralia, fairly rivalling the
beauty of those at Jenolan ;
or the SNOWY MOUN-
TAINS, with their lofty
peaks glistening in the
oright sunshine, where, on the summit of
Mount Kosciusko, the visitormay enjoy the
refreshing coolness of an Alpine climate
during the hottest months of the year, a
pleasure unique in Australian tourist
travel. On the Illawarra or South Coast
Line is the NATIONAL PARK, the
grand and picturesque area reserved
for the people, the lower waters o
PORT HACKING RIVER within
the Park being much frequented by
lovers of boating and fishing. Beyond
the Park lies the enchanting ILLA-
WARRA region, the “ Garden of
New South Wales,” a land of fern and
and palm, with CLIFTON, BULL),
WOLLONGONG and other delightfully-
situated seaside townships ; Lake Illa-
warra, Kiama, with its wonderful
“ BLOW HOLE,” and Nowra, the
southern dairy-farming metropolis.

VITADATIO
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Agent for Webber’s “ Vitadatio.”
Sir, —It is with feelings of deep and heartfelt gratitude
that I write to thank you for the benefit I have received
from “ Vitadatio.” It has honestly made a grand change
in my life. For the past 15 years I have been a martyr to
indigestion in its worst form. For over 2 (two) years I
was practically helpless, being doubled up and quite
unable to straighten myself. My stomach was so weak
that I was unable to take more than four ounces of very
light food per day, consequently I became weak gener-
ally, and within a short time I fell away to a mere skele-
ton. I was attended by some of our leading doctors, and
must have drank gallons of various kinds of medicine, all
to no purpose. Whilst in this miserable condition I read
of several cures of indigestion by “ Vitadatio,” and almost
as a last resource I decided to give it a trial, and right
glad lam that I did so. After a few large bottles I felt
great relief. I continued with the medicine, and all along
could feel the “ slow but sure ” return of health, until
here I am to-day perfectly restored and without an ache
or pain in the stomach after food. I can now eat three
good meals per day and enjoy them. lam at a loss for
words wherewith to express my gratitude, but you may
be sure I shall spread the good news that Webber’s
Vitadatio can, and has, cured indigestion in its worst
form.
I hand you this testimonial perfectly voluntarily, and
you may have my permission to use it as you deem advis-
able. I shall be pleased to interview and give details of
my case to anyone.
