The Law’s Delay.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One morning, .when Bill James went up to oil the
pulleys, a sudden gust of wind toppled him off the
poppet-heads, and he was sent to hospital ; so
I had to do a double shift at the winding-engine
pending his replacement.
His successor, Cyrus V. Johnson, was a stranger
on the Lead, a quiet, self-possessed, capable man,
with a slight nasal accent and a dark goatee.
Cyrus applied for accommodation at the
Southern Cross, where I hung-out ; but the house
happened to be full. He could board there if he
pleased, Dodd told him, but must find temporary
lodgings elsewhere. Cyrus submitted to this,
only soliciting permission to pitch his 6xB in the
back of the hotel yard for the time being.
A fortnight or three weeks afterwards there was
a vacancy ; and Dodd offered Cyrus the half-room
with alacrity, inasmuch as the tent was proving a
greater obstruction and eyesore than had been
anticipated.
But Cyrus expressed a reluctance to make
trouble. Reckoned to hustle along comfortable
enough, now that he had set up his tabernacle.
Within the next /fortnight there were several
vacancies ; and the sight of the 6xB was becoming
more and more insufferable to Dodd.
“Look here, Johnson,” said he, at length,
“that tent of yours is a nuisance. If you don’t
remove the darned thing, I’ll pull it down and
chuck it into the road.”
“Better see your solicitor first, Colonel,” sug-
gested the man in possession.
Dodd invested 10s. 6d. in legal advice, and
learned that Cyrus had acquired a prescriptive
usufructuary interest in the land on which the
tent stood, as well as right-of-way thereto by
affirmative easement. Further, that his freedom
of domicile was impregnable at Common Law,
inasmuch as livery of seisin had been ceded un-
conditionally, and all claim for socage had lapsed
by consent. Hence the landlord was debarred
privilege of entry, as well as of escheat. And as
the grantee’s right was indeterminate as well as
allodial, no process of eviction would lie. Even a
nominal rent might have been multiplied to ex-
orbitance, and recovered by action of assumpsit,
with distraint on the tenement; but it was legally
impossible to translate a moral obligation into a
cash liability. And to seize the tent without
warrant would be merely placing in Johnson’s
hands the formidable right of replevin, as pro-
vided by 2 Will. IV., c. 39, sec. 21. Even to
enter the tenement by force was a thing that the
Queen of England, with all her army, couldn’t do
—much less a common licensed-victualler. Never-
theless, if Dodd would place £2OO to the credit of
his legal adviser, that learned gentleman would
take steps to have the case tested in Equity—
“ Steady, for Gossake! ” groaned Dodd.
“ Could anything be done to me for putting im-
provements on my own place, sposen I don’t inter-
fere with the tent itself ”
“If you obstruct Johnston’s right-of-way, you
certainly render yourself liable to a civil action of
estoppel by matter in pais.”
“ I’ll chance it, anyhow. If he summonses me,
I’ll show him up. And never another meal he sits
down to in my house from this out.”
Next morning—Cyrus being on day-shift—Dodd
procured 24 sheets of the longest corrugated-iron
available, with such timber as was required ; and
in a few hours the tent was enclosed by an un-
broken wall, 12ft. high and 12ft. square, the fur-
ther side being merely nailed to the paling fence
of the hotel yard.
But a couple of bare-footed boys had been glee-
fully scouting for Cyrus, and when that tenacious
citizen returned from work he brought with him
two light ladders, each 14ft. long. He leaned one
of these against the wall, and, ascending within a
yard of the top, he placed the other on the inside,
and bolted the tops firmly together. Then
nodding a courteous good-night to Dodd, he dis-
appeared over the brim.
Next day Dodd had the carpenter at work again,
raising the leaguer another 12ft., till it seemed to
command the Lead like a martello tower; the
ladders, of course, remaining in position.
But there was no appearance of Cyrus at knock-
off time. An hour passed ; then Dodd, in the
fullness of his heart, asked all hands in to have a
drink —for by this time the whole Lead was taking
an interest in the controversy. Presently, stroll-
ing back into the yard, the assembled quidnuncs
heard a faint rattle of tin table-service from within
the fortification, indicating that the firmly-rooted
tenant was at supper.
Immediate examination with a bull’s-eye lamp
showed the wall of circumvallation intact on all
sides, but also revealed the pegs of an alluvial
claim abutting on the rear of the hotel allotment,
and an aperture bandicooted under the wall.
Dodd pronounced a benediction, and the con-
gregation dispersed in reverent silence.
“Reckon I got the rortiest little squat on this
diggin’s; pard,” remarked Cyrus, when he came
to relieve me next morning. “ Sort o’ hermitage,
right agen the business centre. Wouldn’t sell her
for a red cent less’n 20 notes.”
Eventually, however, he compromised for
£l7 10s., with the right to remove the tabernacle.
Vie.
TOM COLLINS.
His Salary.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in the good old days of rough hand-harvest-
ing, 16-ox teams, sweltering dust heats, bush settle-
ments, Maori raids and big cheques in Taranaki.
Dan Boyle jogged into New Plymouth by the
south road, on a sweltering hot day, with a £75
cheque—the result of four months’ toil—a super-
abundant self-satisfaction, a chronic thirst, ac-
cumulated during aforesaid four months, and a
settled determination to “lay himself out,” and
“ to do it in,” and “ make a name for himself” in
the approved fashion. His idea was to hit upon
some chum who was prepared to devote himself
body and soul to the proposed spree, and to
“make things hum” while funds lasted. But
Dan found the accomplishment far more difficult
than the conception, for his ideal chum could not
be found. True, he stumbled across several
kindred spirits with whom he severally lubricated;
but no single one with whom he shared that
mental and moral affinity—-that perfect harmony
of idea and spirit—so essential to the complete
accomplishment of the brotherly drunk which
Daniel had in view.
Disappointed and perplexed, he strolled out of
the pub. and along a road that led into the open
country under the mountain. After about 20
minutes walk, he overtook a bullock - dray, re-
turning from town. He proffered the usual salu-
tation, and, on the solitary occupant of the dray
returning it with “ Good-day ! ” Dan jumped 2ft.
in the air and ejaculated :
“Bob, by all that’s holy! Come down outer
that donkified hassification you’re the feller
I’ve been looking for all day, and I’m blowed
if I could think of yer. I want yer ! Climb
down ! ”
“ Carn’t —toilin’,” laconically replied Bob.
“What’s the job worth ? ” asked Dan.
“ Five bob er day ! ”
“ Git down ! ” said Dan. “ I’ll give yer fi’ bob
er day t’ help me blew me cheque. ”
Bob cogitated, scratched his head, hesitated,
rolled his quid, and finally said, “What erbout
the dray ? ”
“ Whose is it ? ”
“ Saunders’s ! ”
“ I’ll go along while ye go in an’ call-off, an’ yer
can walk back with me.”
“ Right ! ” said Bob shortly.
Whereupon they safely delivered dray and bul-
locks. Bob briefly terminated his engagement,
and returned to town with Dan, and the Great
Taranaki Drunk was instituted.
* • * •
One evening, a month later, Dan raised his head
from his pillow (he was lying fully dressed on the
bed), and with one half-opened eye looked round
on the swollen and unsteady landscape. Then he
reached out his hand towards a chair that stood
by the bedside, took therefrom a bottle of beer,
drank, put it back, and articulated feebly :
“ Bob, ’y’ ’wake ? ”
“’M’— ! ”
‘‘Bob, ole feller—did it all in t’night. Las’
quid ! ”
“’ M >— j”
“’Avtergit back —mornin’.”
“’M’__!”
“G’night!”
“ Ni’ ! ”
• • • •
Next morning Dan and Bob got up early, for
them, had a wash, sat down at the table, looked
at the breakfast, got up again and went outside.
“ Bob,’ said Dan feelingly, “ the game’s goosed ;
’M off back ter the station. 01’ man, I never ’ad
s’ha time ’n me life. Yer bin a real frien’ ter me,
ol’ man, ’n I’ll never forget it, an’ next time I come
in I’ll look y’up first thing. Gi’s yer ’and ”
Dan’s voice quivered with suppressed alcohol as
they gripped. Then Bob said (he seldom said
anything):
“’Here!—l bin gettin’ this ’ere fi’ bob a day
frer month ’n I ain’t touched it yet. S’pose ”
Dan’s mouth opened and closed again slowly.
“Bob!” he said; “I never had a mother like
you—never!”
And they went inside.
WELLINGTON, M.L. .
A. P. ZETT.
The Cheery Coffinmaker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A certain Sydney undertaker well upholds the
reputation of his brothers of the trade as grim
humorists. In one of the coffins —open so that
the inside workmanship should be seen—in his
show-window, there is a large copy of ‘ ‘ The Day
of J udgment.” In the coffin next to it is a copy
of Dore’s representation of Dante’s Inferno ! As
much as to say —“You get into one of these
coffins of mine—-and there you are ! ” But a rival
eoffinmaker in the same street is a much more cheer-
ing soul. He has actually decorated his window in
the most approved Christmas fashion. Wisterias
and nasturtiums and honeysuckle make a floral
frame for the contents of the window, and climb-
ing roses of many hues riot gaily over the recep-
tacles intended for the future dead.
The merry Christmas Season
Makes tradesmen full of cheer ;
The eoffinmaker only
His shop-front keeps austere.
The shops of all the others
Are brave with blossoms bright—
Why should the coffinmaker’s
Be such a joyless sight ?
All’s well! An undertaker
This year has taken care
That in the merry season
His trade shall have its share.
He’s decked his tasty caskets,
In manner fair to see,
With blooms of many colors,
And cheerful greenery.
They seem, in floral language,
To passers-by to say—
“ O be a corpse at Christmas !
There’s naught in life so gay.”
O, gentle undertaker,
It was a kindly thought !
And if, as eoffinmaker,
You don’t succeed—you ought.
If, when this writer dies, he
Should own a pound or two,
He’ll leave his heirs instructions
To deal—for him—with you.
Then let us all, dear Brethren,
Go gaily to the tomb,
In the merry Christmas season,
When coffins are in bloom.

The N.S. W. Old Age Pensions Bill, though
a most valuable commencement, will have to be
amended sooner or later. So will the Maoriland
pension law. The age is fixed too high, and will
prevent the Bill doing so much as it should to
abolish the present state of charitable anarchy.
Sooner or later the Old Age Pension limit must
come down to GO. Otherwise there will be two
stages of old-age indigence: the pauper stage,
during which the decayed citizen will waste away
in the asylum while waiting to qualify for the
pension ; and the (comparatively) golden age
when he who has come through the great tribula-
tion of a term of asylum life will attain his
pension of 10s. per week. Also, 10s. is too little,
and will have to be supplemented by private
charity; it would pay the working classes to
endure some slight increase of taxation to provide
sufficient to keep the aged toiler out of the labor
market altogether. The decaved citizen cannot
live as he ought to live for less than 15s. per week.
Sublime is the calmness with which the
N.S.W. community—for want of a little simple
legislation (or vigorous administration of existent
law) —goes on poisoning itself through its over-
confiding stomach. Out of 339 samples of alleged
food and drink examined last year by the Govern-
ment Analyst 119 were adulterated. That is to
say that over 35 per cent, of the vendors of these
articles have been—putting gastric considerations
aside —simply obtaining money under false pre-
tences. It is probably the characteristic British
indifference to the welfare of the person as com-
pared with thric-e-sacred “ Property ” that has left
English-speaking communities so barbarously be-
hind the times in this respect. In France and
Germany the adulterator is remorselessly nosed-
out and prosecuted ; and in the former country at
least, after paying a heavy fine, or going to gaol
in default, the offender has to exhibit for a speci-
fied time, at the main entrance of his place of
business, an official notification that on such a day
Jean Craomd was convicted of adulteration and
fined so much.
A PRAHRAN PROOF.
Drugs, Doctors, and Hospitals prove quite
impotent.
Clements Tonic Brought in and all Diseases
Melt Away.
The Case of
MRS. ELIZABETH HILL,
Another Proof for Sceptics.
Of the many populous suburbs suri ound-
ing Melbourne not one is to be compared to
l’rahran ; its main stieet of thriving stores,
roads lowered in trees, and the beauty and
animation of the whole place, make a most
attiactive locality. r ihis was the feeling of
our reporter as he trod tne busy footpath of
Chapel-street seeking for No. 24 Chatham-
street, the home of Mis. Elizabeth Hill.
This lady’s residence was easily found, for
it is close to the Town Hall and the main
artery of traffic. The iady hexself chanced
to be at home, and in answer to our ques-
tions graciously consented to relate her
remarkable expei it nee :
“Some time ago,” said Mrs. Hill, “I
Buffered from one of the accidents peculiar
to married women. I was properly attended
and cared for, yet the shock to my system
was such that no medicine or food would
give me back my past health and vigor.
Something was radically wrong with me, I
had no recuperative power, and time,
instead of making me stronger, weakened
me.”
“Did you go to a doctor, Mrs. Hill?”
inquired the reporter.
“ I went to three medical men altogether.
First to our regular doctor, who said I was
suffering from neivous debility ; but his
treatment doing me no goed, 1 tried another
doctor, and later, when my husband went
to West Australia, 1 became an out-patient
at the hospital. But the result was always
the same; no matter where I went, the
drug!- prescribed were quite impotent to do
me good.”
“ What were the syn ptoms, Mrs. Hill, of
,his complaint that so set medical skill at
defiance? ’ inquired the ptessm n.
“lhada dieadful pain in the back part
of the top of my head, a fearful, horrible,
agonising pain that made me feel I was
going mad. Look ! it turned my hair quite
grey in that spot.” (The reporter here
notices that in the place indicated by Mrs.
Hill her naturally dark hair was streaked
with white.] “I could not sleep at night,nor
get proper rest, horrible dreams woke me
up in a cold sweat if I got a short doze, and
the hallucination would remain with me
after I was fairly awake, for I would jump
out of bed with terror and hurry from the
room. Only when the daylight was just
shining in the morning was I able to close
my eyes for a short time. As I lay restless
in bed, turning uneasily from side to side, I
tried all the ways I could think of to induce
sleep. I would count figures over and over
again, or repeat lines of poetry in endless
repetition ; but it was no use, for the <nd of
every effort was that my mind switched
back to the constantly melancholy thoughts
that possessed me. And the least thing
would excite this morbid brooding ; if I read
something horrible in the newspaper, some
murder or the like, I had a sort of irresist-
ible feeling that I was about to be 'nvolved
in a like catastrophe. This dread ,vou dso
master me that I dart d not stay in the
hfiuse, but had to go to my mother’s for her
company and assurance. The least thing
startled me, I would leap right out of my
chair at a sudden noise, even though I
expected it. My husband, knowing this,
would walk >. r\ quietly into the room and
touch me with lis hand g.-ntly; yet this
would mak'Vme tremb eall oyer with a kind
•f shiver. After these nei vous shocks rrry
yeart would flutter and vibrate, and if I
were iq bed J could hear it go thump
liniitup as it seemed to in <ve the covering.
1 rent iqber that once ti e doctor gave me a i
deeping diaught; he said it was of vital
importune* that I should have rest, and I
“'as so delighted thinking of the ob ivion
that was g mg to come for a short time to
my misery, that I got excited with expecta-
tion, and the medicine had no effect. It
was quite a common thing for me to ciy and
soh all night long. I simply could not eat
at ail, 1 had no app Lite, I became very
thin, losing my natural pioinpness of body,
my skin got very' yellow in color and was
blotched w.tli brown patches. In the
morning the rusty taste in my mouth made
rue fei i sick, and there was white fur over
my longue. Ther e was a sharp pain in the
back of my neck and a singing in my ears,
a sort of buzzing that hardly ever left me.
Wind on the stomach was another source of
annoyance. There were muscular pains in
my arms, and at times in the palms of my
hands and soles of my feet a tingling,
pricking sensation, as though they were
going to sleep. My eyes were affected, I
am sure they sank back in my head quite
an inch, and the sight became blurred and
misty with little stars dancing before me.
Sometimes a feeling like a veil falling would
come down over my head and face and creep
to my shoulders, a kind of dead ache, as
though all that part of my body was losing
life—an indescribably horrible feeling,* I
am naturally of a lively disposition and
cheerful in thought; but now I was utterly
tired of my life. If it had not been for
thoughts of my family I would have
welcomed death. And my imagination was
so worked up that I had only to hear
accounts of other people’s maladies to fancy
I was suffering from like complaints in
addition to my own special ailment. My
temper became very irritable; sometimes I
fancy I hardly knew what I was saying ; it
is certain I did not mean all I said. And I
was as easily upset and turned to tears as a
little child, my nerves and impulses being
quite beyond my own control. But while I
tell you of all these smaller things, do not
forget that my great and master trouble all
the time was the fearful pain in the head I
have told you of, that seemed in some way
to be the cause of all my other sorrows.
My last doctor did not speak very hopefully
of my case, he said it would take twelve
months’ tieatment to cure me. And after
my long experience of doctors’ treatment
you may be sure this prospect did not
encourage me. I tried the patent medicines
that are much advertised, but in my case
they failed to do as promised. Then I
chanced to read in the newspaper the re-
port of a cure by Clements Tonic, and I
fancied from what I read that this remedy
might suit my case. I purchased a bottle
of Clements Tonic, and it did me so much
good I bought another ; and it was after
this second bottle that the dreadful pain in
my head went right away, and that I began
to care for my meals and get sleep at night.
Then all the troubles I have told you of
melted away one after the other, like ice
melts under the sun on a warm day, so did
all my distressing symptoms dissolve in the
healing effects of Clements Tonic. I can
not over pia’se Clements Tonic for what it
has done for me ; there is no doubt it was
the one thing that had been wanted during
all my sickness, and the finding of it at lasD
saved my reason if not my life. I know
another case in my own family where
Clements Tonic wiought another cure as
surprising as my own, so that I have double
proof that my faith in Clements Tonic and
its wonderful powers is well founded.”
“ I should like to be allowed to report
your case, Mrs. Hill,” said the reporter
interrogatively, for it will make yet another,
and not the least important, of the mighty
volume of testimonials ga'heied by Clements
Tonic.”
“ I am sure you are quite welcome. * Out
of a full heart the tongue speaketh.' If I
have sa ! d much it is because I feel much,
and I shall only he too g ad if what 1 have
sa’d leaches an ' convince s some person w ho
suffered as I Miff- red. So publish it as n i c i
as you think advisable.”

Tae Bank allows interest on fixed deposits ; collects for
its customers dividends of public companies, and interest
on debentures; and invests money on their behalf in

takes the agency of other banks ; issues drafts, circular
notes and letters of credit, and conducts all customary
banking business.
